`聚光灯效应`（*spotlight effect*）指人高估他人注意自己外表、错误、尴尬或表现的程度。它产生于自己的注意力自然以自己为中心。如果衣服上的污渍、笨拙的一句话、糟糕回答或尴尬时刻占据了自己的心智，人就很容易想象它也占据了他人的心智。

`透明度幻觉`（*illusion of transparency*）与此相关：人高估自己的内部状态对他人的可见程度。紧张显得明显，内疚显得暴露，兴奋显得可读。人鲜明地体验内部信号，然后假定它比实际更多地泄露到外部。

这些偏差会塑造社会行为。学生可能因为觉得每个不完美都公开可见而避免发言。写作者可能因为每个别扭句子都显得刺眼而过度修改。一个人可能把他人的中性行为解释成评价。社交焦虑正依赖这种自我注意与真实公共注意之间的错配。

纠偏不是否认别人会注意任何东西。他人确实会注意某些东西。纠偏在于比例感。其他人也以自己的经验为中心。他们通常没有像自我监控暗示的那样专注于自己的错误。

### 为什么人解释自己行为和解释他人行为的方式不同？

`基本归因错误`（*fundamental attribution error*）指人在解释他人行为时，倾向于过度强调人格、倾向或性格，同时低估情境约束。`行动者—观察者偏差`（*actor–observer bias*）描述了相关不对称：行动者更多用情境解释自己行为，观察者更多用性格解释他人行为。

原因部分在于信息差。判断自己时，人能接触到压力、语境、历史、竞争性动机、不确定性、疲惫、恐惧和约束。判断他人时，人常常只看见行动。行动于是被贴到性格上。他插队是因为没礼貌；我插队是因为有急事。他失败是因为粗心；我失败是因为条件不合理。

归因偏差会被道德判断强化。性格解释令人满意，因为它把责任放在一个人身上。情境解释会复杂化责备。在冲突中，心智经常更需要清楚，而不是准确：恶人、动机、缺陷、模式。

这并不意味着性格无关。人确实有倾向、习惯和稳定模式。错误在于在缺少情境信息时过早用性格解释。更严谨的归因实践会追问：什么压力、激励、约束、角色和信息不对称，即使对一个合理的人，也可能制造这种行为？

### 为什么自我服务偏差会保护身份？

`自我服务偏差`（*self-serving bias*）指人倾向于把成功归因于内部品质，把失败归因于外部条件。成功来自能力、努力、洞察、自律或美德；失败来自不公平条件、坏运气、说明不清、时机不好或他人错误。

这种偏差保护自尊和行动感。一个把所有失败都内化、把所有成功都外化的人，会变得心理上不稳定。一定程度的自我保护是必要的。偏差出现在保护阻断学习时。

在学业中，好成绩可能归因于聪明，坏成绩归因于考官。在工作中，成功项目可能归因于领导力，失败项目归因于市场条件。在关系中，善意被当作自己的性格，伤人之举被当作压力。自我保持良好，失败变成情境性的。

危险在于，维持身份的机制同时阻止修正。如果失败从不作为关于自身方法、能力或判断的信息返回，自我就保持完整，却无法改变。成熟的自我评价必须在保留尊严的同时，不保护每个自我描述免受证据检验。

### 为什么群体会强化偏差？

群体通过告诉成员信任谁、哪些来源算数、什么语言可接受、什么威胁重要、什么解释正当，来降低不确定性。这在社会上有效率。没有人能独自评价所有信息。群体归属提供认识捷径。

可是，群体身份也会过滤现实。`内群体偏差`（*in-group bias*）使自己群体显得更多样、合理、道德和可信。`外群体同质性偏差`（*out-group homogeneity bias*）使其他群体显得更统一。`群体归因错误`（*group attribution error*）把部分成员行为当成整个群体本质的显示。政治、宗教、学术、职业、民族和网络共同体都可能制造这些效应。

一旦群体身份进入判断，证据就不再只是证据。它变成忠诚或背叛、我们或他们、威胁或确认。己方的弱论证可能得到修补；对方的强论证可能受到怀疑。同一行为在群体内部可以被框定为勇气，在群体外部则被框定为极端。

群体偏差不能靠简单宣称独立来消除。人需要信任共同体。问题在于，群体成员身份是否变成了过强的认识过滤器，以至于预先决定什么可以算作真实、公正、严肃或人性。

### 为什么自己的观点常常显得像现实本身，而不是视角？

`天真现实主义`（*naïve realism*）指人倾向于相信自己看见的是世界本身，而不同意自己的人则是不知情、非理性、有偏差或恶意的。它是最重要的社会认知偏差之一，因为它把视角转化为现实。

天真现实主义在心理上很自然。知觉通常不显得像解释，而像与世界直接接触。如果一项政策显然有害，一个论证显然薄弱，一个人显然不诚实，一个群体显然危险，那么分歧就变得难以理解。心智开始在异议者身上寻找缺陷：无知、意识形态、腐败、愚蠢、坏信念。

这种偏差会推动极化。如果双方都把自己体验为只是看见现实，那么分歧就会变成对方有缺陷的证据。双方都可能真诚相信自己较少受偏差影响，因为自己的结论感觉像由事实直接给出。

纠偏不是相对主义。有些观点确实支持更强，有些证据确实更好，有些主张确实是假的。纠偏在于认识论谦逊：承认通向现实的路径受到注意、语言、既有信念、群体身份、情绪和可得证据的中介。现实存在；但一个人对现实的即时视图仍然只是视图。

### 为什么人会展示社会赞许的自我？

`社会赞许偏差`（*social desirability bias*）指人倾向于以会被他人积极评价的方式报告信念、行为或特征。它影响问卷、访谈、公共讨论、自我报告和道德论证。人会低报偏见、自私、无知、成瘾、懒惰、残忍和不一致；高报慷慨、宽容、自律、知识、勇气和独立。

这并不总是有意识撒谎。人常常会内化社会认可的自我描述。如果一种文化赞赏理性独立，人会把自己描述成独立思考者。如果一个共同体赞赏同情心，人会记得自己的同情动机。如果一个机构奖励团队合作，人会报告合作意图。自我报告受到受众塑造，即使受众已经被内化。

社会赞许偏差重要，是因为许多心理和社会判断依赖自我报告。人们说自己重视什么，可能不同于他们在压力下选择什么。人们说自己相信什么，可能不同于他们自动推断什么。人们说自己会做什么，可能不同于激励改变时真正做什么。

这并不意味着所有自我报告都无价值。它意味着自我报告是社会情境中的行为，而不是通向心智的透明窗口。它必须与行动、激励、语境和隐性模式比较。

### 如何纠正自我与社会判断偏差？

自我与社会偏差难以纠正，因为它们保护身份和归属。但若干做法可以削弱其力量。

把信心和校准分开。记录预测，附上概率，回看结果。没有记录的信心只是表演。

寻找会疼但不会羞辱人的反馈。如果反馈过度威胁身份，它会被拒绝；如果反馈毫无代价，它会被忽略。

进行角色反转。问同样行为如果由对手、陌生人、盟友或自己做出，会怎样被判断。

区分性格与情境。在用性格解释行为之前，列出可能的压力、激励、约束和缺失信息。

审计群体过滤器。追问哪些来源被过于轻易地信任，哪些来源被过快排斥，哪些主张如果来自另一个群体会受到不同评价。

让自我描述容纳不确定性。一个人可以在某个领域有能力，在另一个领域无知；意图上道德，执行上有偏；动机真诚，判断错误。

目标不是摧毁自我。没有自我信任的人无法行动。目标是让自我信任接受证据约束。

### 自我与社会偏差如何通向记忆问题？

自我必须记住自己。群体也必须记住自己。一旦自我和社会判断进入记忆，过去就不再只是记录，而是身份、辩护、伤害、骄傲、教训、怨恨和叙事连续性。

这引向 Part 9。记忆偏差不只是忘记细节，而是心智如何重构过去，使当前自我和它所在的社会世界继续保持意义。下一个问题因此不只是“发生了什么”，而是“心智需要怎样的过去，才能继续以现在这种方式判断、行动和归属”。

## Part 9 记忆为什么会重构过去，而不是保存过去？

### 为什么记忆不是录音录像系统？

记忆常被想象成储存：事件发生，心智记录下来，后来提取时把被储存的内容取回。这个模型很直观，但过于简单。人的记忆不是被动档案，而是编码、选择、压缩、储存、提取、重构和重新解释的主动过程。

这对认知偏差很关键，因为记忆为后来的判断提供证据库。那些感觉像“发生过的事”的内容，可能已经是被重构过的版本：它受最初编码影响，也受后来暗示、反复讲述、情绪重要性和当前自我理解影响。记忆偏差因此不是认知末端的次要缺陷。它会反过来供给注意、意义建构、决策、自我判断和未来预测。

Pohl 的手册把记忆幻觉作为认知幻觉的一大类，包含`摩西幻觉`、方位记忆幻觉、联想记忆幻觉、标签效应、`误导信息效应`、`事后偏差`，以及变化或稳定性的幻觉。 功能性分类文章用另一种语言表达了同一点：信息太多，不可能全部保存，心智必须决定保存什么、概括什么、丢弃什么；被保存的东西又会反过来塑造后来的过滤器和心理模型。

核心思想是：**记忆的设计目标并不主要是复制过去，而是让过去可被使用。** 可使用性需要压缩、意义和整合。正是这些操作制造了扭曲。

### 为什么心智更容易记住意义，而不是细节？

记忆通常更擅长保存要旨，而不是表面细节。人会比起原话更容易记住一次谈话的意思，比起完整顺序更容易记住一段经历的情绪色调，比起所有条件更容易记住一次失败的教训。这不只是缺陷。一个把所有细节同等保存的记忆系统效率极低。它必须优先保存可能指导未来行为的东西。

代价是意义可能取代细节。一旦心智提取出摘要，后来回忆时就可能把摘要当成原始内容再现。一个人记得一场会议“充满敌意”，后来就可能“记得”更多实际并未出现的敌意表情。读者记得一篇文章的论点，后来可能记错它用了哪些例子。学生记得老师是鼓励型的，后来就可能把模糊评语重构成支持性话语。

这就是为什么记忆偏差与意义建构相连。心智不只解释现在，也解释过去。一旦过去事件获得了意义——背叛、胜利、羞辱、警告、证明、不公、成长——细节就开始围绕这个意义重新组织。有些被锐化，有些被拉平，有些消失。

当意义有充分根据时，这个过程是有用的。当被提取出来的教训强过原始证据时，它就变成偏差。

### `错误记忆`如何从联想中产生？

`错误记忆`（*false memory*）发生在人记住了并未发生的事情，或以扭曲形式记住事件时。一个强有力机制是联想。如果若干相关项目被呈现，那个把它们连接起来但实际没有出现的概念，后来可能显得熟悉，尽管它从未被遇到。

Pohl 的手册讨论了 DRM 范式，即 Roediger 与 McDermott 改造自 Deese 的实验范式。参与者学习一组与某个未呈现关键词强相关的词表。例如，词表中可能都是与 `rough` 相关的词，但 `rough` 本身并未出现。后来，参与者可能错误回忆或再认这个缺失词；由于这个关键联想词从未被呈现，它的回忆就构成记忆幻觉。

这种机制不是任意发明。心智编码的是语义网络。相关词会激活一个中心主题，而这个主题后来会感觉像曾经被直接看到或听到。错误记忆是有意义的。它正因为太连贯而错误。

这可以解释许多日常扭曲。一个人可能记得会议中有人说过某句话，因为这句话符合会议主题。读者可能记得作者提出过某个主张，因为它符合整篇文章的论证。目击者可能记得某个细节，因为它与现场强相关。记忆填补了联想中心，然后把这个中心误认为知觉。

### `误导信息效应`如何在事件之后改变记忆？

`误导信息效应`（*misinformation effect*）发生在事件之后引入的信息改变后来对原始事件的记忆时。一个问题、一篇新闻、一场谈话、一张照片、一个标签或一种反复描述，都可能与实际看到或经历的内容混合。

这个效应重要，是因为记忆不会在事件结束时关闭。事件结束后，它还会被讨论、询问、叙述、解释和社会框定。每一个后来的输入都可能进入重构。一个目击者听到“汽车猛烈撞上另一辆车”，后来可能比听到中性动词的目击者记得更剧烈的碰撞。一个人反复听到某个家庭故事，后来可能记得自己在场，尽管最初只是通过叙述知道那一幕。

Pohl 的手册把`误导信息效应`放在记忆幻觉中，并将其描述为事件后信息会干扰人准确回忆原始事件的情形。 它在目击证词、访谈、警方询问、新闻报道、心理治疗和家庭记忆中都有明显应用意义。

危险在于，当事人可能是真诚的。记忆污染不需要说谎。后来的暗示可以变得流畅、熟悉，并与原始痕迹整合。当人提取记忆时，提取的是一个混合体，却把它体验为过去本身。

### 为什么标签会改变被记住的东西？

`标签效应`（*labelling effect*）发生在附着于刺激的语言标签改变后来对该刺激的判断或回忆时。标签不只是命名，它会引导知觉和重构。Pohl 关于标签效应的一章指出，某个具体标签可以被贴到刺激上，并在后续判断或回忆中发挥扭曲作用；这一章还把标签效应与锚定、误导信息效应和事后偏差联系起来。

经典情形是模糊视觉材料。在 Pohl 讨论的 Carmichael 等人的研究中，参与者先接受两个标签之一，再观看模糊线条图。后来重画时，参与者的复现更接近被标签命名的物体，而不是原始模糊图形。Pohl 报告说，研究者据此认为，在视觉呈现之前命名一个形式，可能改变它后来被再现的方式。

这并不只属于实验室线条图。老师把一个学生标为“粗心”，后来模糊行为就会通过粗心被记住。葡萄酒被标成“甜”或“干”，可能被体验和记忆成不同味道。政治抗议被标成“暴乱”或“公民抵抗”，观察者后来记住的细节可能不同。一个职场中的人被标为“难相处”，后来就可能通过这个框架被回忆。

标签压缩了模糊性。它让知觉更容易，也让知觉更窄。心智通过名称观看，又通过名称回忆。

### 为什么语义契合会让错误更难被发现？

`摩西幻觉`（*Moses illusion*）显示，当错误词项与周围语义结构足够契合时，记忆和理解可能接受一个被扭曲的问题。经典形式是：“摩西把每种动物带了几只上方舟？”许多人会回答“两只”，尽管与方舟相关的圣经人物是诺亚，而不是摩西。

Pohl 的手册用部分匹配和语义相关性解释这个现象。当替换词与预期词具有较高语义相似性，并且适合周围脚本时，错配就更难被注意。摩西和诺亚共享足够多的圣经和水相关联想，因此扭曲可能通过理解；无关替换更容易被检测出来。

这个幻觉在理论上重要，因为它说明理解不是逐词核验。心智往往检查整个命题是否足够符合某个已知脚本。如果符合，细部错配就可能被错过。意义抹平了错误。

在日常阅读和听话中，同样机制会出现：人接受熟悉但错误的引文，漏看知名故事中被替换的名字，或因为整体框架连贯而没注意政策描述中的微妙变化。心智识别了脚本，于是不再检查零件。

### 为什么记忆会混淆来源？

`来源混淆`（*source confusion*）发生在人记得某个信息，却记错它从哪里来时。内容是可得的，来源却被错误归因。一个人可能记得一个主张，却忘记它来自同行评议论文、新闻标题、流言、小说、政治演讲，还是自己的推断。

这是重复性错误信息能够在纠正后继续存活的原因之一。后来，心智可能记得那个熟悉主张，却不记得附着其上的纠正。一个曾经被读作虚假的陈述，可能只剩下“我听过这个”的感觉。熟悉感留下，来源控制减弱。

来源混淆也解释了观念如何被错误拥有。在`隐性剽窃`（*cryptomnesia*）中，人可能相信自己产生了原创想法，实际上此前已经接触过它。这未必是有意剽窃，而可能是记住了内容，却没有把内容连回来源。

纠偏依赖来源标记。良好的知识实践会记录一个主张来自哪里，来源是否可靠，证据如何支持它，以及它是否后来被纠正。没有来源记忆，熟悉感就会成为认识货币。

### 为什么事后认知会重写不确定性？

`事后偏差`（*hindsight bias*）发生在人得知结果之后，高估自己过去已经预测到或本来会预测到该结果的程度。Pohl 的总结正是这样界定事后偏差：在事后，结果已知之后，人会高估自己在事前已经估计或本来会估计到的内容；认知解释包括知识基础被改变，或记忆搜索受到已知结果的偏置；这一现象稳健，而且很难通过有意努力降低。

事后偏差改变的是过去的不确定性。在选举、审判、商业失败、事故、关系破裂或医学诊断之前，许多结果都可能合理。结果出现之后，心智围绕它重组早期证据。模糊信号变成警告，微弱线索变成预兆，替代可能逐渐褪色。

这不只是傲慢，而是意义建构。一旦结果已知，心智会试图把它整合成连贯因果故事。问题是，这个故事随后伪装成先见之明。人把过去记得比它实际更可读。

事后偏差会损害评价。它让坏结果显得比实际更可预防，也让成功结果显得比实际更明显地明智。它会扭曲法律责任、管理复盘、医学诊断、政治分析和个人后悔。公平评价必须重建结果未知时的信息状态，而不能站在后来知识的舒适位置审判过去。

### 为什么人会错误记住改变与稳定？

人并不中立地记住过去的自我。他们常常重构改变与稳定，以服务当前身份。有时人会夸大连续性：“我一直就是这样。”有时人会夸大变化：“我以前很天真，但现在懂了。”哪个方向更突出，取决于身份需要。

Pohl 的手册把`变化或稳定性的幻觉`列为记忆幻觉章节，与事后偏差和其他重构性现象并列。 这个位置很重要：关于个人发展的记忆不只是自传，而是认知建构。

一个想感觉真实一致的人，可能记得价值观跨时间稳定。一个想感觉自己进步的人，可能把过去自我记得比实际更有缺陷。政治转向者可能把早期信念记得明显混乱。经过高强度学习的学生，可能低估自己此前已经知道多少。一个从糟糕关系中恢复的人，可能把早期疑虑记得比当时更强。

自我用记忆讲述发展。偏差出现在连续性或成长叙事覆盖早期状态的真实复杂性时。

### 为什么峰值和结尾会支配被记住的体验？

`峰终定律`（*peak-end rule*）指人对一段经历的回顾性评价，会不成比例地受到最强烈时刻和结尾影响。`持续时间忽视`（*duration neglect*）指经历长度的重要性低于通常预期。一段较短但结尾糟糕的痛苦经历，可能被记得比一段更长但结尾不那么难受的经历更糟；一次旅行可能主要由最好时刻和最后几天来决定评价，而不是由每个小时的平均质量决定。

这些概念与 Kahneman 对`体验自我`（*experiencing self*）和`记忆自我`（*remembering self*）的区分密切相关。体验自我生活在一个个时刻中；记忆自我建构后来用于评价和未来选择的故事。二者可能不一致。一段人生、旅行、讲座、关系、餐食或医疗程序，在逐时体验上是一回事，在事后记忆中可能是另一回事。

这很重要，因为决策常常由记忆自我做出。一个人可能因为结尾愉快而再次去一家餐厅，因为一个高峰时刻非凡而忘记旅行中的长时间无聊，或因为最后阶段痛苦而回避某种治疗。记忆不是完整平均数，而是被选择过的摘要。

偏差不在于峰值和结尾完全无关。它们经常重要。错误在于让它们代表完整经历，而不检查它们省略了什么：持续时间、分布、普通时刻、恢复过程和累积负担。

### 为什么首因和近因会塑造序列记忆？

当人记住一组列表、一场演讲、一段论证、一次会议、一场辩论或一段关系过程时，位置会影响记忆。`首因效应`（*primacy effect*）指早期项目常常被更好记住，因为它们获得更多注意、复述或组织作用。`近因效应`（*recency effect*）指后期项目常常被更好记住，因为它们仍在短期记忆中活跃，或塑造最终印象。

这些效应重要，是因为许多判断依赖序列。候选人的第一印象会框定后续信息。辩论中的最后一个论点可能因为最新鲜而显得更有说服力。一场会议开头糟糕、结尾良好，可能与开头良好、结尾糟糕被记成不同经历。一场长期冲突可能由最初背叛或最后道歉决定评价。

序列记忆不是中性日志。它受到位置、注意、情绪和叙事收束的加权。因此，顺序效应在面试、审判、教学、谈判、产品体验和政治传播中都很重要。

纠偏需要重建完整序列。中间发生了什么？哪些项目因为普通而被忘记？第一印象是否框定了后续解释？结尾是否覆盖了平均质量？没有这些问题，记忆会把顺序误认为重要性。

### 为什么记忆偏差不只是个体问题？

记忆是社会性的。事件通过谈话、记录、制度、仪式、媒体、照片、官方报告、家庭故事、法院文件、教材和纪念活动被记住。这些社会形式不只是保存记忆，也组织记忆。

一个家庭可能反复讲述某个童年事件的一个版本，直到它变成被记住的版本。一个国家可能通过英雄主义、受害、背叛、解放或羞辱来叙述一场战争。一个机构可能把失败记成不可预测的事故，而不是可预见的后果。一个政治群体可能以强化群体身份的方式保存怨恨和胜利。

这并不意味着集体记忆总是虚假的。它意味着集体记忆是选择性的、有结构的。那些在个体记忆中运作的机制——选择、压缩、标签、来源混淆、事后偏差、情绪峰值——也会在群体层面运作，并且后果更大。

记忆偏差因此与权力相交。谁有权给事件贴标签？哪些记录能保存下来？哪些声音被认为可信？哪些细节变成象征？哪些损失被计算？哪些伤害被正常化？认知偏差理论必须承认，记忆重构可以同时是心理的、社会的和制度性的。

### 如何减弱记忆偏差？

记忆不能变得完美，但某些扭曲可以被减弱。

在结果未知之前记录判断。书面预测、带日期笔记、决策日志和概率估计，可以减少事后重构。

把事件记忆与后来解释分开。标明什么是直接观察到的，什么是推断的，什么是后来听到的，什么是反思后得出的结论。

保护来源信息。把引用、访谈条件、文件来源和更正历史同主张保存在一起。

避免诱导性提问。在法律、医学、教育和治疗情境中，问题措辞可能改变后续回忆。

使用同时期记录。日记、消息、时间戳、照片和版本历史都不完美，但它们能约束重构。

追问记忆正在做什么。它是在保存证据，还是在保护身份、支持责备、维持归属、简化后悔，或解释现在？

比较多个见证者和记录。当记忆被放在独立痕迹旁边，它就不再是完全私人的、绝对主权的东西。

目标不是不信任所有记忆。一个不能依赖记忆的人无法生活。目标是把记忆当作有历史的证据，而不是直接通向过去的入口。

### 记忆如何通向纠偏问题？

前面几部分已经显示出一个循环：注意过滤信息，意义建构填补空白，概率判断难以处理分布，决策把判断转化为行动，自我与群体偏差保护身份，记忆又重构过去，并把重构后的过去变成未来判断的证据。

这意味着纠偏不可能只是简单地要求“理性一点”。偏差分布在整个认知过程中。等一个人开始修正结论时，输入可能已经被过滤，故事可能已经被补全，概率可能已经被扭曲，决策可能已经被辩护，自我可能已经被保护，记忆可能已经被重写。

Part 10 因此要提出一个实际而有限的问题：不是如何彻底消除偏差，而是如何建立程序、环境、记录、信息格式和反馈系统，来减少可预测的扭曲。

## Part 10 怎样减少偏差，而不是假装消除偏差？

### 为什么纠偏不能意味着变成“无偏的人”？

`纠偏`（*debiasing*）不应被理解为变成一个完全理性的观察者。这个目标不可能实现。偏差不是外加到纯净心智上的异物。它来自认知的日常操作：过滤信息、补全意义、简化概率、在不确定中行动、保护自我、归属于群体、重构记忆。

没有过滤的心智会淹没在信息中。没有意义建构的心智无法理解情境。没有信心的心智会无限犹豫。没有记忆压缩的心智无法使用过去。制造偏差的机制，同时也使判断成为可能。所以，纠偏不能意味着移除这些机制本身。

更现实的定义是：**纠偏是通过改变判断产生的条件，减少可预测的扭曲。**有时这意味着改变信息格式。有时意味着放慢决策。有时意味着使用外部记录。有时意味着建立问责、比较组、独立估计或反馈回路。

Pohl 的手册把认知幻觉理解为思维、判断和记忆中的系统性偏离，同时也强调许多幻觉难以避免，有些只能通过说明、任务设计或程序变化降低，而不是靠单纯意识到偏差就能消除。 功能性分类文章则从另一角度给出同样实践判断：偏差之所以存在，是因为它们帮助解决四个无法避免的认知问题——信息太多、意义不足、必须快速行动，以及决定什么值得记住。

实践结论很严格：目标不是纯洁无瑕，而是更好的错误管理。

### 为什么仅有意识很弱？

知道一个偏差的名字，很少能阻止它发生。一个人可能知道`确认偏误`，仍然选择性搜索。可能知道`锚定效应`，仍然被第一个数字牵引。可能知道`事后偏差`，仍然觉得结果本来就显而易见。可能知道`偏差盲点`，仍然主要用偏差理论诊断别人。

意识之所以弱，是因为大多数偏差并不以明确命题形式出现。它们表现为流畅、熟悉、信心、连贯、显著、不适、烦躁、紧迫或道德清晰。陷入偏差的人通常不会体验到“我有偏差”。他们体验到的是“这很明显”“这感觉对”“这个例子很重要”“这个人不可靠”“这个计划会成功”“我早就知道了”。

意识也经常来得太晚。等一个人说“我应该避免偏差”时，输入可能已经被过滤，故事可能已经完成，参考点可能已经设定，锚点可能已经吸收，群体立场可能已经激活，记忆可能已经被重写。在认知末端贴一个警告标签，无法完全修复认知开端已经造成的扭曲。

这并不意味着意识无用。意识给问题提供语言，使程序性修正成为可能。但意识只是开端。减少偏差需要一套系统，使有偏操作在硬化成判断之前变得可见。

### 为什么程序比意志力更有效？

意志力要求制造偏差的同一个心智，从内部纠正自己。程序则改变任务，使有偏操作较少有机会安静地运作。正因如此，清单、决策日志、盲审、基础率提示、独立估计、结构化比较和预先承诺，通常比“客观一点”“仔细思考”这类模糊指令更有效。

清单有效，是因为它不依赖压力时刻的记忆。决策日志有效，是因为它在事后偏差改写判断之前记录预期。盲审有效，是因为它在光环效应和刻板印象进入判断前移除无关身份线索。独立估计有效，是因为它防止群体锚定。自然频率有效，是因为它让分母可见。预先承诺有效，是因为它在诱惑到来之前改变未来选择环境。

程序不是智力弱点的标志，而是承认个体认知在负荷下不稳定。高风险领域早已理解这一点。航空、医学、工程、法律、金融和科学研究都使用程序，因为专家信心本身不够。专业能力会减少一些错误，也会制造另一些错误。程序给判断提供外部骨架。

原则很简单：**不要只要求心智变好，而要改变心智必须在其中判断的环境。**

### 如何纠正信息过滤偏差？

信息过滤偏差发生在推理之前。它决定什么变得可得、显著、熟悉、值得注意。因此，纠正必须从输入阶段开始。

第一种方法是来源多样化。一个反复消费同一批来源的人，不只是接收同一批结论，也会继承同一种显著性结构。同样的事实会显得核心，同样的例子会显得有代表性，同样的对手会显得愚蠢，同样的风险会显得紧迫。来源多样化不是道德装饰，而是改变心智能注意到什么。

第二种方法是暴露基础率。戏剧性案例应当与分母信息放在一起。震撼犯罪故事要放在犯罪趋势旁边。失败公司要放在行业失败率旁边。医学轶事要放在患病率和对照数据旁边。这不会抹掉鲜明案例，而是给它定位。

第三种方法是反显著性。如果心智自然注意戏剧性案例，分析者就必须主动寻找安静案例：普通成功、未发生之事、被避免的失败、沉默的大多数、缺失的比较组、无聊的基线。偏差经常隐藏在不吸引注意的东西中。

第四种方法是延迟判断。许多过滤偏差在第一批可得信息立即设置框架时最强。推迟判断可以让更多证据在故事硬化之前进入。实践中，这可以意味着读完整份文件再反应，收集多个例子后再形成类别，或者在回应情绪化信息前等待。

过滤不能被废除，但可以被训练。

### 如何纠正意义建构偏差？

意义建构偏差发生在心智过快把碎片转化为故事、类别、原因和身份时。纠偏要求打破第一个连贯故事的垄断。

核心技术是`考虑相反面`（*consider-the-opposite*）。形成一种解释之后，追问：如果相反解释是真的，我会期待看到什么？什么证据会削弱我的解释？什么替代故事也能解释同一批事实？一个聪明的反对者会怎么说？这不能保证客观，但能防止第一个故事变成唯一故事。

第二种技术是生成替代解释。一个解释常常是陷阱；两个解释制造比较；三个或更多解释开始削弱叙事俘获。如果一个学生失败，懒惰是一种解释。教学不清、隐藏压力、前置知识不足、考核设计糟糕、生病、激励错位，也都是可能解释。任务不是否认责任，而是防止过早封闭。

第三种技术是说明机制。不要只说“这是腐败导致的”“这是文化导致的”“这是人格导致的”“这是资本主义导致的”，而要追问实际机制：谁行动了，通过什么激励，在什么约束下，掌握什么信息，通过什么渠道，产生什么后果？模糊解释廉价地制造意义。机制要求意义支付成本。

第四种技术是选择比较类别。个案不能孤立解释。公司失败必须与同类公司比较。学生表现必须与同类学生比较。政治事件必须与同类事件比较。没有比较类别，故事会变得过于容易。

意义建构应当被放慢，直到替代解释有时间变得可见。

### 如何纠正概率判断？

概率偏差的纠正，依赖恢复直觉通常省略的结构：基础率、样本量、分母、假阳性、比较组和不确定性。

尽可能使用`自然频率`（*natural frequencies*）。不要让人只操作百分比，而要把问题表达成总体中的计数。例如：“1000 人中有 10 人患病；其中 8 人检测阳性；没有患病的人中有 95 人也检测阳性。”这种格式把真阳性和假阳性显示为同一总体中的部分。

在看个体化证据之前，先问基础率。判断创业公司是否会成功之前，先问同类创业公司通常有多少能成功。判断某种诊断是否可能之前，先问这种疾病在相关群体中有多常见。判断某人是否符合某职业之前，先问这种职业本来有多常见。

区分证据强度与假设流行度。一个检测可以很敏感，但疾病可能很罕见。一个描述可以很符合，但类别可能很少见。一个故事可以很鲜明，但结果的先验概率可能很低。

使用置信区间，而不是只给点估计。单个数字容易制造过度精确。范围迫使心智表达不确定性。预测不只要问“会发生什么”，还要问“这个估计可能错到什么程度”。

记录预测。校准需要记录。一个从不记录概率的人，无法知道自己是否系统性过度自信、过度不自信、乐观、悲观，或只是选择性记忆。

统计纠偏不是让人变得不再像人，而是让不可见的分母变得可见。

### 如何纠正决策偏差？

决策偏差发生在概率、价值、损失、时间、所有权和承诺被翻译成行动时。因此，纠偏必须改变选择结构。

第一，识别参考点。许多选择之所以像损失，只是因为某个特定期待变成了参考点。要问：这是相对于什么的损失？相对于去年、相对于期待、相对于权利感、相对于默认值、相对于竞争者，还是相对于想象中的未来？

第二，反转框架。如果治疗方案用存活率描述，也用死亡率描述。如果政策用成本描述，也用收益描述。如果一个选项被描述成改变，也要把当前状态描述成一种主动选择。等价重框架可以测试决策是在追踪实质，还是在追踪措辞。

第三，使用沉没成本测试：如果过去没有任何投入，现在这仍然是最好的选项吗？如果答案是否定的，继续就可能是由过去辩护，而不是由未来价值辩护。

第四，使用预先承诺。对涉及诱惑、延迟或自控的决策，不要依赖未来意志力。自动储蓄、设置截止日期、移除诱惑、制造惩罚，或把未来后果具体化。

第五，区分决策质量和结果质量。好决策可能产生坏结果，坏决策也可能产生好结果。评价应当问当时能知道什么、有哪些替代方案、推理过程是否健全。

决策纠偏需要面向未来的纪律：过去成本、当下情绪和未来价值不能被当成同一件事。

### 如何纠正自我与社会偏差？

自我与社会偏差难以纠正，因为它们保护身份、地位和归属。直接攻击常常会强化它们。因此，纠正既要保留足够尊严，使学习能够发生，又要强迫判断接触证据。

第一种工具是校准。给预测附上概率，记录下来，并回看结果。一个说“我很确定”的人，必须学会自己实际表现中的“确定”意味着 60%、80%，还是 95%。

第二种工具是反馈。好反馈具体、及时、聚焦行为，并连接标准。模糊批评威胁身份；模糊表扬滋养过度自信。有用反馈指出做了什么、产生了什么影响、符合或没有符合什么标准。

第三种工具是角色反转。问同样行动如果由对手、陌生人、盟友、下属、上级或自己做出，会怎样被评价。许多道德和社会判断会随行动者变化而变化。这种变化暴露出身份依赖的过滤。

第四种工具是分离视角。区分“我体验到了什么”“我推断了什么”“对方可能意图什么”“结构激励了什么”“还缺少什么证据”。社会冲突常常恶化，是因为这些层次被压缩成一个指控。

第五种工具是群体审计。每个群体都有被信任的来源、被禁止的主张、神圣价值、敌人刻板印象和地位奖励。减少群体偏差，要问这个群体让什么容易被相信，又让什么难以被注意。

目标不是变得社会性冷漠。目标是防止身份变成过强的证据过滤器，以至于修正不可能发生。

### 如何纠正记忆偏差？

记忆偏差的纠正，依赖在重构开始前制造痕迹。一旦结果已知，故事被反复讲述，自我被辩护，记忆就更难与解释分开。

决策日志是一种工具。在决策前记录可得证据、预期结果、概率、理由、疑虑和替代方案。后续复盘比较的是决策与当时真正已知内容，而不是与事后看来显而易见的内容。

来源标记是另一种工具。主张应当携带来源信息：它从哪里来，来源是否可靠，这个主张是否被纠正，它是直接观察到的还是推断出来的。没有来源标记，熟悉感很容易变成真实感。

同时期记录很重要。邮件、笔记、版本历史、照片、时间戳、草稿都不完美，但它们能锚定记忆，限制心智悄悄美化自己过去推理的能力。

把记忆和叙事分开。追问：究竟发生了什么？我当时感到了什么？我推断了什么？我后来知道了什么？我现在给它讲了什么故事？这些是不同层次。偏差在它们融合时增长。

记忆纠偏不要求不相信所有回忆。它要求把记忆当成证词，而不是事件本身。

### 为什么问责有时减少偏差，有时增加偏差？

`问责`（*accountability*）可以改善判断，前提是人知道自己必须向一个重视准确性、证据和公平推理的受众说明过程。它能促使人考虑替代方案、检查假设，避免粗率判断。

但如果受众重视忠诚、信心、意识形态纯洁、速度或地位，问责就会加重偏差。此时，人会更擅长生产可被接受的辩护，而不是更准确的判断。他们不是思考得更好，而是为群体表演得更好。

所以，“公开讨论”不自动等于纠偏。敌意受众会让人防御。党派化受众会奖励动机性推理。崇拜声望的受众会奖励信心而非校准。官僚化受众会奖励安全从众而非真实。

好的问责必须被设计。它应当要求人在结果未知前给出理由，奖励经过校准的不确定性，要求与替代方案比较，保护异议，并把过程评价与结果运气分开。坏问责只是教会心智哪一种偏差在社会上更有利。

### 为什么纠偏本身也会制造新偏差？

纠偏可能变成过度校正。学会可得性偏差的人，可能轻视鲜明案例，即使这个案例确实有诊断价值。学会刻板印象问题的人，可能在特定统计问题中忽视真实群体基础率。学会过度自信的人，可能变得过度不自信而不敢行动。学会沉没成本的人，可能过快放弃项目。

纠偏也可能变成傲慢。掌握偏差词汇会制造“自己比别人少偏差”的幻觉。这尤其危险，因为它给偏差提供了更高级语言。人不再说“我讨厌他们”，而说“他们正在表现出动机性推理”。人不再说“我方正确”，而说“只要不被意识形态俘获，证据就是明显的”。

纠偏还可能变成瘫痪。如果每个判断都可能有偏差，人可能无限犹豫。但行动不能等待完美认知。问题不是移除判断，而是在当前情境的风险程度下，把判断改善到足够好。

最安全的原则是比例性。利害越高、反馈越弱、不确定性越大、激励越扭曲、错误代价越高，就越需要复杂纠偏。简单决策不需要完整分析机器。

### 一个实用的个人纠偏系统是什么样的？

个人纠偏系统应当足够简单，可以被使用；也应当足够有力，能够打断主要扭曲。它可以围绕五个反复出现的问题组织。

第一：我的输入流是什么？哪些来源、例子、人物、记忆和平台，正在让某些证据比另一些证据更可得？

第二：我已经补全了什么故事？我添加了哪些超出证据的假设？什么替代故事也能解释同一批事实？

第三：分母是什么？基础率、样本量、比较类别、缺失案例和假阳性率分别是什么？

第四：什么选择结构正在塑造我？参考点、默认值、框架、锚点、沉没成本、即时奖励或所有权效应是什么？

第五：什么记录以后可以检查我？在记忆改变之前，哪些预测、理由、来源或决策痕迹应当写下来？

这些问题不是完整理论，而是一套工作纪律。它们直接对应前面的部分：过滤、意义、概率、决策、自我、群体和记忆。

### 一个好的制度纠偏系统是什么样的？

制度比个人更需要纠偏，因为制度会放大判断。一个有偏的招聘流程、风险模型、处分系统、投资委员会、编辑流程、警方讯问、医学诊断或政策审查，可能一次伤害许多人。

好的制度系统会在无关身份线索可能扭曲评价时使用盲审。它会把独立评估与群体讨论分开，以减少锚定和从众。它要求人在结果未知前写出理由，以减少事后偏差。它为重复出现的高风险决策设置清单。它追踪预测和结果，以改善校准。它让异议足够安全，使缺失证据能够浮现。它使用外部比较类别，而不是内部叙事。它审计数据中的缺失案例和选择效应。

制度也必须监控激励。如果晋升奖励信心，制度就会生产过度自信。如果速度被奖励而准确率没有复盘，过滤偏差会增长。如果忠诚比真实更受奖励，群体偏差会正常化。如果惩罚失败却不区分过程和运气，事后偏差会支配复盘。

制度纠偏因此不只是认知问题。它是程序性的、社会性的，也是政治性的。结构决定哪一种思考能够存活。

### 认知偏差的最终地图是什么？

整个系统现在可以被总结出来。

第一，心智面对`信息太多`。它必须过滤。由此产生可得性、显著性、重复、熟悉感、确认偏误和偏差盲点。

第二，心智面对`意义不足`。它必须填补空白。由此产生代表性、合取谬误、聚类错觉、虚假相关、刻板印象、光环效应、公正世界信念、拟人化和投射。

第三，心智面对`概率与不确定性`。它难以处理分布。由此产生基础率忽视、样本量忽视、赌徒谬误、均值回归忽视、幸存者偏差、分母忽视和概率忽视。

第四，心智面对`行动需要`。它必须在压力下选择。由此产生损失厌恶、框架效应、锚定、现状偏差、沉没成本谬误、禀赋效应、模糊性厌恶和双曲贴现。

第五，心智面对`自我与社会`。它必须维持能力感、正当性和社会位置。由此产生过度自信、乐观偏差、控制幻觉、自我中心偏差、虚假共识、归因错误、自我服务偏差、群体偏差、天真现实主义和社会赞许偏差。

第六，心智面对`记忆限制`。它必须压缩和重构。由此产生错误记忆、误导信息效应、标签效应、来源混淆、事后偏差、峰终定律、持续时间忽视，以及变化或稳定性幻觉。

这些不是彼此隔离的盒子，而是一个循环。注意过滤出的东西，成为意义建构的材料。意义补全的东西，塑造概率判断。概率暗示的东西，进入决策。决策承诺的东西，变成自我辩护。自我辩护选择的东西，变成记忆。记忆保存的东西，又成为下一轮可得性的来源。

### 核心结论是什么？

认知偏差不只是一张错误清单。它是受约束的人类认知投下的影子。

心智必须压缩一个过大而无法完整感知的世界，补全一个过于残缺而无法直接理解的世界，简化复杂到难以计算的概率，在确定性到来之前行动，保护一个必须继续存在的自我，归属于提供方向感的群体，并记住一个过于稠密而无法完整保存的过去。每当一个有用操作变得过快、过度自信、过窄、过于自我保护，或过于脱离反馈，偏差就会出现。

成熟的认知偏差理论必须避免两种错误。第一种是轻蔑：把人看成充满缺陷的非理性机器。第二种是浪漫化：把每一种偏差都解释成适应性智慧。更严格也更有用的看法是：**偏差是有限认知的可预测成本；当判断获得更好的格式、程序、环境、记录和反馈支持时，它们可以被降低。**

最后的教训是实践性的。不是更少信任心智，而是更谨慎地信任它。

# English Table

## A1. Foundational Concepts and Theoretical Framework

This table is the conceptual entrance to the whole encyclopedia. These entries are not ordinary bias items; they define what a bias is, why it differs from ordinary error, why fast thinking is sometimes useful, and why correction requires procedures rather than mere self-warning. Their common problem is conceptual confusion: treating every wrong judgment as a bias, treating every heuristic as irrational, or treating debiasing as a matter of “trying harder.”

| No. + Bias / Concept Name                                  | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                            | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                             |
| ---------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| A001 Cognitive Bias                               | A systematic deviation in judgment, reasoning, decision-making, or memory relative to a factual, statistical, logical, or rational standard. The key is not simply that a person is wrong, but that the error has a patterned direction. Cognitive bias explains why people repeatedly misjudge in similar ways under similar conditions. | **Case:** A company receives one angry customer complaint with a dramatic story: “Your product ruined my whole week.” In the meeting, everyone begins talking as if most users are angry. The actual data shows 97% satisfaction, but the vivid complaint dominates attention. The error is not that the complaint is false; the error is treating one emotionally available case as if it represents the whole user base. | Ask: What is the relevant standard? Is this an isolated mistake or a recurring pattern? What distribution, comparison group, or record would test the judgment? | Broader than logical fallacy. Not the same as ignorance, lying, value disagreement, or ordinary lack of information. A person can be intelligent and still show cognitive bias. |
| A002 Cognitive Illusion                           | A stricter subtype of cognitive bias. Like a visual illusion, a cognitive illusion is a stable and often reproducible distortion in thinking, judgment, or memory. It usually appears under a specific task structure and persists even when the person is not trying to be irrational.                                                   | **Case:** A teacher asks: “How many animals of each kind did Moses take on the Ark?” Many students answer “two” because the sentence fits a familiar biblical script. Only afterward do they notice that the figure should be Noah, not Moses. The mind accepted semantic fit before checking the exact term.                                                                                                              | Ask: Does this error recur across people and tasks? Is it produced by the structure of the question, memory, or representation?                                 | Narrower than cognitive bias. Especially useful for probability illusions, memory illusions, and reasoning illusions. Not every bad judgment is a cognitive illusion.           |
| A003 Heuristic                                     | A shortcut used to judge quickly under limited time, information, or computational ability. A heuristic is not an error by itself. It becomes problematic when the shortcut is applied in an environment where its cue is unreliable.                                                                                                     | **Case:** A traveler in an unfamiliar city chooses a restaurant because it is crowded. This is often a useful heuristic: crowds may signal quality. But if the crowd is there because of a tourist promotion, fake reviews, or location convenience, the shortcut misfires. The error is not using a shortcut; the error is assuming the shortcut always tracks quality.                                                   | Ask: What cue is this shortcut using? In this environment, is that cue valid? What feedback would show whether the heuristic works?                             | Availability, representativeness, and anchoring are major heuristic families. A heuristic can be adaptive in one setting and misleading in another.                             |
| A004 System 1 / System 2                         | A framework distinguishing fast, automatic, associative processing from slower, effortful, controlled reasoning. Many biases occur when an automatic answer feels sufficient and the slower system does not examine whether the task requires another kind of reasoning.                                                                  | **Case:** Someone hears: “A bat and a ball cost $1.10 in total. The bat costs $1 more than the ball.” They immediately answer: “The ball costs 10 cents.” The answer feels fluent, but it is wrong; checking the arithmetic shows the ball costs 5 cents. System 1 supplies an intuitive answer, and System 2 fails to intervene.                                                                                          | Ask: Did I accept the first fluent answer? Is slow reasoning actually checking the result, or merely explaining why the first answer feels right?               | Do not simplify this as “System 1 is bad, System 2 is good.” System 1 is often efficient and intelligent; System 2 can also rationalize a biased conclusion.                    |
| A005 Cognitive Ease                                | Processing fluency produces feelings of familiarity, truth, safety, elegance, or coherence. The error occurs when the mind mistakes ease of processing for evidence of validity.                                                                                                                                                          | **Case:** A political slogan is repeated for months: “Strong borders, safe families.” Even without new evidence, it begins to feel obvious because the phrase is short, rhythmic, familiar, and easy to recall. People may later say, “It just sounds true,” when what they are experiencing is processing fluency.                                                                                                        | Ask: Does this seem true because it is supported by evidence, or because it is familiar, repeated, simple, or well phrased?                                     | Related to illusory truth effect, mere exposure effect, rhyme-as-reason effect, familiarity, and fluency. Ease is a feeling, not a proof.                                       |
| A006 WYSIATI / What You See Is All There Is      | The mind constructs a coherent story from available information and neglects information that is absent, hidden, delayed, or uncollected. The error is treating the current evidence field as if it were the whole evidence field.                                                                                                        | **Case:** In hiring, one candidate speaks confidently, has an elite degree, and tells a polished career story. The interview panel quickly concludes: “She is clearly the strongest.” But they have not checked work samples, reference quality, actual team performance, or base rates for interview accuracy. What is visible becomes the whole reality; what is missing does not feel missing.                          | Ask: What information is absent? What would I need to know before treating this story as complete? Which missing evidence could reverse the judgment?           | Related to availability, narrative fallacy, confirmation bias, overconfidence, and halo effect. WYSIATI is a general mechanism behind many more specific biases.                |
| A007 Attribute Substitution / Question Substitution | A difficult target question is unconsciously replaced by an easier question. The answer feels relevant because the substitution is hidden.                                                                                                                                                                                                | **Case:** A voter is asked: “Which candidate has the better economic plan?” Instead of analyzing policy, trade-offs, implementation, and constraints, the voter answers a simpler question: “Which candidate seems more sincere and confident?” The emotional impression substitutes for policy evaluation.                                                                                                                | Ask: What question did I actually answer? Is it the same as the original question? If not, what hard information did I avoid?                                   | Core mechanism behind availability, representativeness, affect heuristic, planning fallacy, and many political judgments.                                                       |
| A008 Bounded Rationality                            | Human reasoning operates under limits of time, attention, memory, information, and computational capacity. Bias arises partly because the mind cannot calculate everything and must simplify.                                                                                                                                             | **Case:** A student choosing a graduate program faces hundreds of variables: ranking, cost, visa rules, faculty, city, job prospects, course design, alumni network, and personal fit. A fully rational calculation is impossible. The student uses shortcuts such as reputation, friends’ opinions, and city preference. These shortcuts are necessary, but they can distort the final choice.                            | Ask: What constraints are shaping this judgment? Can I reduce the burden by using a checklist, comparison table, external data, or expert feedback?             | Explains why perfect rationality is unrealistic. It does not excuse careless reasoning; it identifies where structure is needed.                                                |
| A009 Ecological Rationality                         | A judgment strategy is rational or irrational depending on the environment. A heuristic can be effective when cues are reliable and feedback is stable, but misleading in noisy, rare, or adversarial settings.                                                                                                                           | **Case:** An experienced firefighter senses that a room is unsafe before consciously explaining why. In that environment, repeated exposure and feedback may make intuition reliable. But the same confidence in stock picking may be unreliable because market feedback is noisy, delayed, and confounded by luck. The same “trust your gut” rule works in one ecology and fails in another.                              | Ask: Does this environment provide valid cues, repeated practice, and timely feedback? Or is it noisy, rare, strategic, and luck-heavy?                         | Associated with the critique that some bias tasks are artificial. Closely related to expertise, intuition, and heuristic validity.                                              |
| A010 Hot Cognition / Cold Cognition              | Hot cognition is emotionally and motivationally charged; cold cognition is more detached and analytic. The error occurs when a judgment driven by fear, anger, pride, desire, or identity feels like neutral reasoning.                                                                                                                   | **Case:** After receiving a harsh comment online, a person writes: “I have calmly analyzed this, and this person is clearly malicious.” But the analysis began from humiliation and anger. The judgment may contain real points, yet the evidence has been weighted by the need to defend the self.                                                                                                                        | Ask: What emotion is present? What would I think if I were calmer, less threatened, less hungry, less angry, or less eager to win?                              | Related to affect heuristic, motivated reasoning, moral judgment, identity-protective cognition, and social conflict.                                                           |
| A011 Debiasing                                      | The reduction of predictable distortions through procedures, formats, records, incentives, and feedback. Debiasing does not mean eliminating all bias; it means making judgment less vulnerable to known failure patterns.                                                                                                                | **Case:** A hospital finds that doctors sometimes skip steps during stressful emergencies. Instead of telling doctors “be more careful,” the hospital uses a checklist. The checklist does not make doctors smarter; it changes the task so memory failure and pressure have less room to operate.                                                                                                                         | Ask: Can this judgment be redesigned? Can blind review, base-rate prompts, decision logs, independent estimates, or structured feedback reduce the bias?        | Awareness alone is usually weak. Effective debiasing depends more on external structure than on self-confidence or moral sincerity.                                             |

### A2. Normative Standards, Statistical Tools, and Correction Tools

These entries are not ordinary biases. They are the standards, formats, and procedures used to identify and reduce bias. A judgment can only be called biased when there is some benchmark: a base rate, a probability structure, a factual record, a calibration record, a reference point, or an external trace. Their shared function is to prevent impression from pretending to be evidence. A story may feel coherent, a case may feel representative, a choice may feel like a loss, and a memory may feel true, but without these tools the structure needed to evaluate the judgment remains invisible.

| No. + Bias / Concept Name            | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                               | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                          |
| ------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| A012 Base Rate               | The general prevalence of an event, condition, or category in a relevant population. It is the starting point for probability judgment. The common error is to jump directly to the vivid case, personality description, or new clue without asking how common the target event was before the clue appeared.           | **Case:** A patient searches symptoms online and sees that one rare disease includes “fatigue, dizziness, and occasional chest discomfort.” He becomes frightened because the description fits him. But those symptoms are also common in stress, sleep loss, anemia, caffeine intake, and many minor conditions. The disease description is not irrelevant, but without the base rate he cannot know whether it is a likely explanation or merely a frightening match. | Ask: What is the relevant population? How common is this condition or event before this new evidence appears?                                                                      | A tool concept, not a bias. It becomes central in base-rate neglect, Bayesian inference error, medical diagnosis, legal evidence, hiring, and risk judgment. |
| A013 Natural Frequencies    | A probability format that expresses information as counts within a population. It makes denominators, true positives, and false positives visible. The problem it solves is that percentages often hide the comparison structure needed for correct judgment.                                                           | **Case:** A doctor says, “This test is 90% accurate,” and the patient thinks a positive result means they almost certainly have the disease. A clearer presentation is: “Out of 1000 people like you, 10 have the disease. Of those 10, 9 test positive. Of the 990 who do not have it, 99 also test positive.” Now the patient can see that many positive results are false positives.                                                                                 | Convert percentages into counts. Draw a frequency tree. Ask: among all positive results, how many are true positives and how many are false positives?                             | Not a bias, but a correction format. Closely related to Bayesian reasoning, base-rate neglect, denominator neglect, and prosecutor’s fallacy.                |
| A014 Calibration              | The match between subjective confidence and actual accuracy. Good judgment does not require never being wrong; it requires confidence levels to correspond to real performance. The error appears when a person says “I am 90% sure” but is correct only 60% of the time.                                               | **Case:** A commentator makes weekly political predictions with absolute language: “This will definitely happen.” When reviewed after six months, many “definite” predictions failed. The problem is not merely that some predictions were wrong; the problem is that confidence had no stable relationship to accuracy.                                                                                                                                                | Record predictions with probabilities and review them later. Ask: when I say “likely,” “almost certain,” or “impossible,” what percentage do those words mean in my actual record? | A tool concept. Central to overconfidence, expert judgment, forecasting, risk assessment, legal judgment, and medical diagnosis.                             |
| A015 Reference Point         | The psychological baseline against which outcomes are coded as gains or losses. People often react not to final states, but to movement relative to what they owned, expected, deserved, or imagined. The error appears when an arbitrary or manipulated reference point defines the emotional meaning of the decision. | **Case:** An employee receives a 10% raise. Objectively, income has increased. But she expected 20% because a colleague hinted that “people at your level usually get more.” The actual raise now feels like a loss. The emotional reaction is not based on the final salary alone, but on a reference point created by expectation.                                                                                                                                    | Ask: Compared with what is this a gain or loss? Is that comparison point factual, arbitrary, inherited, negotiated, or strategically framed by someone else?                       | Core tool in prospect theory, loss aversion, endowment effect, status quo bias, framing effect, and negotiation analysis.                                    |
| A016 Outside View           | A correction method that evaluates a case by comparing it with a class of similar cases, rather than relying only on the internal story of the current case. It reduces overoptimism by replacing imagined progress with empirical comparison.                                                                          | **Case:** A student says, “I can finish this 20-page essay in two days. I already know the topic.” The internal story sounds plausible: outline tonight, draft tomorrow, revise the next morning. The outside view asks: how long did similar essays actually take in the past? If previous essays took five to seven days, the two-day plan is likely fantasy.                                                                                                         | Ask: What is the relevant reference class? What happened in similar cases? How long did similar projects actually take?                                                            | Key correction for planning fallacy, optimism bias, project forecasting, budgeting, legal strategy, product launches, and academic writing.                  |
| A017 Inside View              | A perspective that focuses on the specific plan, intention, causal story, and imagined path of the current case. It is necessary for planning, but becomes biased when it ignores the statistical record of comparable cases.                                                                                           | **Case:** A start-up team says, “Our product is different. We have a better founder, better design, and a clearer market gap.” Every failed company also had an internal story explaining why it was special. The inside view may contain real details, but by itself it cannot estimate failure rate, execution risk, or market adoption.                                                                                                                              | Ask: Which parts of this plan are case-specific, and which should be compared with similar cases? What does the outside view say?                                                  | Not a bias by itself. It becomes dangerous when it excludes base rates, outside view, and reference-class forecasting.                                       |
| A018 Experiencing Self        | The self that lives through moment-by-moment experience. It tracks the actual flow of pleasure, pain, attention, boredom, fatigue, and emotion as life is being lived.                                                                                                                                                  | **Case:** During a three-hour museum visit, a person spends most of the time tired, hungry, and mildly bored, but has one breathtaking 10-minute encounter with a painting. The experiencing self lived through many ordinary or unpleasant moments, not just the highlight.                                                                                                                                                                                            | Ask: What was the experience like moment by moment? Was the whole duration pleasant, or only selected parts?                                                                       | Not a bias. It is paired with remembering self and is essential for understanding peak-end rule, duration neglect, and well-being judgments.                 |
| A019 Remembering Self         | The self that stores, narrates, and later evaluates experience. It often uses peaks, endings, and narrative meaning rather than full temporal averages. The error occurs when the remembered story is mistaken for the full experience.                                                                                 | **Case:** After the museum visit, the person says, “That was an amazing afternoon,” because the painting at the end was extraordinary. The memory is not fabricated, but it is selective. It turns the experience into a story organized around the best moment and the ending, while ignoring long stretches of fatigue.                                                                                                                                               | Ask: Is my later story faithful to the full experience? What did the experience feel like across its whole duration?                                                               | Related to peak-end rule, duration neglect, hindsight bias, autobiographical memory, and life satisfaction judgments.                                        |
| A020 Decision Log           | A procedural correction: record evidence, predictions, probabilities, reasons, doubts, and alternatives before outcomes are known. It prevents later memory from silently rewriting what seemed obvious at the time.                                                                                                    | **Case:** An investor buys a stock and writes: “I think there is a 60% chance of moderate growth, 25% chance of stagnation, 15% chance of serious decline. Main risks: regulation and weak demand.” A year later, if the stock falls, the log prevents two distortions: “I knew it was risky all along” and “This was obviously unforeseeable.”                                                                                                                         | Before deciding, write: what do I believe, why, with what probability, and what would change my mind? Review after the outcome.                                                    | Not a bias. Used to reduce hindsight bias, outcome bias, overconfidence, self-serving bias, and memory reconstruction.                                       |
| A021 Pre-mortem            | A decision procedure: imagine that the project has already failed, then list plausible reasons for failure before commitment hardens. It legitimizes doubt before group loyalty and optimism suppress it.                                                                                                               | **Case:** Before launching an app, the product lead says: “Imagine it is six months later and this launch failed badly. What happened?” One designer says onboarding was too complex; one engineer says server costs exploded; one marketer says users did not understand the value. These risks were already present, but the pre-mortem makes them speakable.                                                                                                         | Ask: If this fails, what will the most likely reason be? What warning signs would appear early?                                                                                    | Debiasing method for optimism bias, planning fallacy, groupthink, escalation of commitment, and product risk.                                                |
| A022 Red Teaming              | A structured adversarial review designed to expose weak assumptions, missing evidence, and alternative explanations. It forces a judgment to survive intelligent opposition before reality tests it.                                                                                                                    | **Case:** A legal team believes its argument is strong. The red team must argue for the other side: “Your best precedent is distinguishable, your factual record has a gap, and your remedy is too broad.” This is not sabotage; it is controlled opposition before the court supplies uncontrolled opposition.                                                                                                                                                         | Ask: What would a competent opponent attack? Which assumption, source, inference, or missing fact is weakest?                                                                      | Debiasing procedure. Related to consider-the-opposite, adversarial collaboration, devil’s advocate roles, and structured analytic techniques.                |
| A023 Blind Review               | A procedure that removes irrelevant identity cues before evaluation. It reduces distortions from status, prestige, halo effects, stereotypes, gender, race, school background, accent, or personal familiarity.                                                                                                         | **Case:** A hiring committee first sees candidate names, universities, and photos, and quickly forms impressions. In a blind review version, the committee first scores anonymized work samples and structured answers. Some candidates who looked less prestigious perform better on actual task quality. Blind review prevents status signals from becoming evidence.                                                                                                 | Ask: Which identity cues are irrelevant to the judgment? Can they be hidden until after task-relevant evaluation?                                                                  | Not a bias. Used to reduce halo effect, affinity bias, stereotyping, prestige bias, gender/race bias, and authority bias.                                    |

| A024 Risk Policy                                      | A decision rule for handling repeated risks as a portfolio rather than as isolated emotional choices. The error it corrects is narrow framing: evaluating each small risk separately and rejecting beneficial risk-taking because each case feels unpleasant.      | **Case:** A company can fund 20 small experimental features. Each has a 40% chance of failing, but successful ones produce large gains. If managers approve each experiment one by one, they reject many because each failure feels embarrassing. With a risk policy, they decide in advance: “We will fund a portfolio of experiments and judge the total return.” | Ask: Is this a one-time decision or part of a repeated class? What rule would I want if I made this decision 100 times?                                                                          | Not exactly a bias; it is a debiasing tool. Related to loss aversion, narrow framing, expected value, portfolio thinking, and prospect theory.                    |

## B. Information Filtering and Attention Biases

Information filtering biases appear before explicit reasoning begins. The mind cannot process everything, so it must select what becomes noticeable, memorable, familiar, emotionally charged, or easy to retrieve. These biases share the same basic error: information that is easier to notice, remember, repeat, or feel may be mistaken for information that is more true, more frequent, more important, or more representative.

### B1. Availability, Repetition, and Familiarity

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                        | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                   |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| B001 Availability Heuristic                       | The mind estimates frequency, probability, or importance by how easily examples come to mind. This is efficient when memory availability tracks real frequency, but biased when vividness, recency, media exposure, or emotion makes some examples easier to retrieve. | **Case:** After watching several news reports about airplane crashes, a person says: “I know statistics say flying is safe, but lately planes seem to be crashing all the time.” The crashes are not necessarily more frequent; they are more mentally available because the reports are vivid, emotional, and recent.                                                                                 | Ask: Are these examples easy to recall because they are common, or because they are vivid, recent, dramatic, or repeatedly reported? What is the actual base rate?          | A major prototype bias. Closely related to salience bias, frequency illusion, availability cascade, negativity bias, and probability neglect.                         |
| B002 Attentional Bias                                 | Attention is selectively drawn toward information related to current concerns, threats, desires, goals, or emotional states. The error occurs when what captures attention is treated as what objectively matters most.                                                | **Case:** A student worried about failing an exam reads the teacher’s feedback. Ten sentences are positive, one says “your argument still needs more precision.” The student spends the whole night thinking only about that one critical sentence and concludes: “The teacher thinks my essay is bad.” Attention has selected threat-relevant information and made it feel like the whole evaluation. | Ask: What kind of information did I notice first? What did I ignore because it was emotionally neutral, positive, ordinary, or less threatening?                            | Related to anxiety, threat detection, negativity bias, confirmation bias, and mood-congruent memory bias.                                                             |
| B003 Illusory Truth Effect                        | Repeated statements become easier to process, and processing fluency is misread as truth. The error is not that people consciously believe repetition proves truth, but that familiarity creates a truth-like feeling.                                                 | **Case:** A rumor says: “This supplement improves memory.” At first, a reader ignores it. After seeing the claim in ads, comments, short videos, and recommendation posts, they later say: “I’m not sure, but I’ve heard many times that it works.” The repetition has produced familiarity, and familiarity has begun to imitate evidence.                                                            | Ask: Have I seen real evidence, or have I merely encountered the claim many times? Can I identify the original source and its quality?                                      | Closely related to validity effect, cognitive ease, mere exposure, source confusion, and continued influence effect.                                                  |
| B004 Validity Effect                                 | Repetition increases the perceived validity or truthfulness of a statement, especially when the source of the repetition is forgotten. The error is that repeated exposure becomes a substitute for independent verification.                                          | **Case:** During an election, a slogan is repeated: “Candidate X is weak on security.” Voters may not remember where they first heard it, whether evidence was supplied, or whether it came from an opponent’s campaign. Later, the claim feels like common knowledge. The repeated statement has gained validity through familiarity rather than evidence.                                            | Ask: Is this claim supported by independent evidence, or does it feel valid because it has circulated repeatedly? Do I remember the source?                                 | Often overlaps with illusory truth effect. “Validity effect” is useful when emphasizing judged truth or credibility rather than mere familiarity.                     |
| B005 Mere Exposure Effect                             | Repeated exposure increases liking or preference, even without new evidence of quality. The error occurs when familiarity is mistaken for objective value, safety, beauty, or trustworthiness.                                                                         | **Case:** A listener hears a song in the background several times and initially finds it ordinary. After a week, they begin to like it and say: “Actually, this song is pretty good.” Nothing about the song has changed; repeated exposure has made it feel smoother and more likable.                                                                                                                | Ask: Do I prefer this because it is better, or because it is more familiar? Would I judge it the same if I encountered it for the first time beside alternatives?           | Different from illusory truth effect: mere exposure concerns liking; illusory truth concerns perceived truth. Related to brand familiarity and status quo preference. |
| B006 Context Effect                                   | The surrounding environment, comparison set, or interpretive frame changes how an object is perceived or judged. The error occurs when people treat the judgment as object-intrinsic while ignoring the context that shaped it.                                        | **Case:** In a luxury store, a $300 bag feels “affordable” because it sits beside $2000 bags. In an ordinary supermarket, the same $300 bag would feel extravagant. The object has not changed; the comparison context has changed the psychological meaning of the price.                                                                                                                             | Ask: Would I judge this the same in another context, beside another comparison set, or under another label?                                                                 | Related to contrast effect, framing effect, anchoring, decoy effect, and distinction bias.                                                                            |
| B007 Cue-dependent Forgetting                     | Memory retrieval depends on cues present at encoding and retrieval. When the right cue is absent, information may feel lost even though it remains stored. The error is assuming failure to retrieve means absence of knowledge or importance.                         | **Case:** Someone walks into the kitchen and forgets why they came. Returning to the desk makes them remember: they wanted to get scissors. The intention was not erased; the original context provided the retrieval cue.                                                                                                                                                                             | Ask: What cue was present when I formed the intention or learned the information? Can I recreate the context, route, note, or physical setting?                             | More a memory mechanism than a bias. Related to absent-mindedness, context-dependent memory, and retrieval failure.                                                   |
| B008 Mood-congruent Memory Bias                  | Current mood makes mood-consistent memories easier to retrieve. The error occurs when the present emotional state is mistaken for a balanced record of the past.                                                                                                       | **Case:** After one failed interview, a job seeker thinks: “I always mess things up.” Their mind retrieves previous failures, awkward conversations, and rejections, while successful interviews and compliments become harder to access. The current disappointment has selected the memory archive.                                                                                                  | Ask: What memories become easier to recall in this mood? What evidence would I remember if I were calmer, safer, or in a better mood?                                       | Related to attentional bias, negativity bias, depression-related cognition, availability heuristic, and affective forecasting.                                        |
| B009 Frequency Illusion                               | After noticing something for the first time, people begin noticing it everywhere and infer that it has become more frequent. The error is confusing a change in attention with a change in the world.                                                                  | **Case:** A person learns the term “gaslighting” and suddenly sees it in articles, comments, videos, and conversations. They say: “Why is everyone suddenly talking about this?” The term may not have suddenly increased; the person’s detection filter has changed.                                                                                                                                  | Ask: Did the phenomenon become more common, or did I become newly sensitive to it? Can historical data or search trends test this?                                          | Often overlaps with the Baader–Meinhof phenomenon. Related to availability, recency illusion, and attentional set.                                                    |
| B010 Baader–Meinhof Phenomenon                  | A popular name for the experience of newly noticing something and then repeatedly encountering it. Mechanistically, it combines selective attention and confirmation by repeated detection.                                                                            | **Case:** Someone decides to buy a blue Mazda. The next day, blue Mazdas seem to appear on every street. The cars were likely already there; the person’s goal has tuned attention toward that model.                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Ask: What changed: the world’s frequency or my attention filter? Was I counting these cases before?                                                                         | Best treated as a subtype or popular label of frequency illusion rather than a separate core bias.                                                                    |
| B011 Recency Illusion                                 | People believe something they recently noticed is itself recent. The error is converting “new to me” into “new in the world.”                                                                                                                                          | **Case:** A reader sees young people using a slang word online and says: “This generation just invented this word.” A quick historical search shows the word existed decades earlier. The novelty was in the reader’s awareness, not necessarily in the word’s existence.                                                                                                                              | Ask: Is this phenomenon actually new, or only newly visible to me? What historical evidence exists?                                                                         | Related to frequency illusion, availability heuristic, presentism, and cultural commentary errors.                                                                    |
| B012 Empathy Gap                                      | People in one emotional, bodily, or motivational state underestimate how differently they or others will think in another state. The error is treating the current state as the default human state.                                                                   | **Case:** After a full dinner, someone says: “Tomorrow I’ll just eat a light salad. I don’t understand why I snack at night.” At midnight, hungry and tired, they eat everything in the fridge. The earlier self failed to simulate the later bodily state.                                                                                                                                            | Ask: Am I judging from a different state than the one in which the decision will occur? How will hunger, anger, pain, fear, desire, fatigue, or loneliness change judgment? | Related to projection bias, restraint bias, present bias, affective forecasting, and hot-cold empathy gap.                                                            |

### B2. Salience, Strangeness, and Negativity

This group covers biases caused by what stands out. The mind gives priority to what is vivid, strange, emotional, funny, visually distinctive, negative, or personally concrete. This priority is often useful: unusual and threatening information may deserve attention. The shared error appears when salience is mistaken for importance, memorability for truth, emotional force for evidential force, or a single vivid case for the whole distribution.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                  | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                             | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                            |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| B013 Salience Bias                                 | Information that is vivid, concrete, emotionally charged, or perceptually striking receives excessive weight. The error is treating what stands out as what matters most.                                                              | **Case:** In a product review meeting, one manager plays a screen recording of a user angrily failing to find a button. Everyone immediately says: “The interface is broken.” But the analytics show that 94% of users complete the task without difficulty. The recording is useful evidence, but it becomes overpowered because it is visible and emotionally concrete. | Ask: Is this case important because it is representative, or because it is vivid? What does the full dataset say?                                | Closely related to availability heuristic, identifiable victim effect, negativity bias, and focusing effect.                                                   |
| B014 Bizarreness Effect                              | Strange, unusual, absurd, or unexpected information is more memorable than ordinary information. The error occurs when memorability is confused with significance or reliability.                                                      | **Case:** A teacher gives ten examples of weak reasoning. Nine are ordinary classroom examples; one is bizarre: “A man refuses to believe in gravity because his cat once landed badly.” Students later remember only the cat example and start treating it as the main form of bad reasoning, although it was just a memorable illustration.                             | Ask: Do I remember this because it is important, or because it is strange? What ordinary cases am I forgetting?                                  | Related to Von Restorff effect, humor effect, salience bias, and anecdotal reasoning.                                                                          |
| B015 Humor Effect                                     | Humorous material attracts attention and is remembered more easily. The error appears when wit, ridicule, or comic framing makes a claim feel more persuasive than its evidence warrants.                                              | **Case:** In an online debate, someone replies to a complex policy proposal with: “Great idea — let’s fix housing prices by putting a rooftop garden on every spreadsheet.” The joke gets thousands of likes. The humor makes the proposal look ridiculous, but it does not actually analyze cost, feasibility, alternatives, or evidence.                                              | Ask: Did the joke reveal a real flaw, or did it merely make the target look foolish? What argument remains if the humor is removed?              | Related to ridicule, rhetoric, rhyme-as-reason effect, salience bias, and social media persuasion.                                                             |
| B016 Von Restorff Effect / Isolation Effect       | An item that differs sharply from surrounding items is more likely to be noticed and remembered. The error occurs when distinctiveness is treated as importance.                                                                       | **Case:** A résumé list contains ten candidates with similar formatting. One candidate uses a bright red design and a strange title: “Growth Alchemist.” Recruiters remember that candidate more clearly, even though the actual work history is weaker than several others. The isolated feature improves memory, not necessarily merit.                                 | Ask: Is this item better, or merely more distinctive? Would I still choose it if all options were formatted the same way?                        | Related to salience bias, contrast effect, novelty bias, and visual design effects.                                                                            |
| B017 Negativity Bias                                | Negative information often receives more attention, stronger emotional response, and greater memory weight than positive or neutral information. The error appears when negative evidence is overweighted beyond its diagnostic value. | **Case:** A student receives 19 positive comments on an essay and 1 criticism: “The final paragraph is weaker than the rest.” For the rest of the day, the student thinks: “My essay was bad.” The criticism may be useful, but the mind gives it disproportionate weight because negative information signals possible threat, rejection, or failure.                    | Ask: How many positive, neutral, and negative data points are there? Is the negative item diagnostic, or merely emotionally stronger?            | Related to loss aversion, attentional bias, mood-congruent memory, and threat detection.                                                                       |
| B018 Omission Bias                                   | Harm caused by inaction is often judged less harshly than harm caused by action. The error appears when “not acting” is treated as morally or causally neutral, even when it predictably produces harm.                                | **Case:** A parent refuses a vaccine because they fear a rare side effect. Later, the child contracts the preventable disease. The parent says: “At least I didn’t actively put something dangerous into my child.” The mind treats action as more responsible than omission, even though omission was also a choice with risk.                                           | Ask: If inaction predictably causes harm, why is it being treated as neutral? What are the risks of acting and not acting?                       | Related to status quo bias, default effect, loss aversion, and moral judgment. Also belongs partly in decision bias.                                           |
| B019 Identifiable Victim Effect                   | A single named, pictured, or personally described victim evokes stronger concern than a larger anonymous group. The error occurs when concreteness and emotional identification distort proportional judgment.                         | **Case:** A charity shows a photo of one child named Lina and tells her story: she walks three hours for clean water. Donations surge. Another report says 200,000 children face the same water crisis, but receives less attention because the victims are statistical. The single story is true and morally important, but it can crowd out scale.                      | Ask: How large is the problem beyond the visible person? Am I responding to need, or to identifiability?                                         | Related to salience bias, availability heuristic, compassion fade, and scope neglect.                                                                          |
| B020 Novelty Bias / Appeal to Novelty                 | New ideas, technologies, products, or theories may be judged as better because they are new. The error is treating novelty as evidence of superiority.                                                                                 | **Case:** A company replaces a stable internal tool with a newly hyped AI platform because “this is the future.” After adoption, the team discovers that the old tool was faster, cheaper, and better integrated. The new system was not chosen because it solved the problem better; it was chosen because novelty carried prestige and excitement.                      | Ask: What problem does the new thing solve better than the old one? What evidence shows improvement beyond novelty?                              | “Appeal to novelty” can be a rhetorical fallacy; novelty bias is the psychological preference. Related to pro-innovation bias, salience, and status signaling. |

### B3. Anchoring, Contrast, and Presentation Format

This group covers biases caused by the way information is first placed, compared, or framed. The mind rarely evaluates objects in isolation. A number becomes meaningful relative to an anchor; a product looks cheap or expensive relative to nearby products; a policy feels acceptable or unacceptable depending on whether it is described as a gain, loss, survival rate, death rate, cost, or protection. Their shared error is mistaking a judgment shaped by presentation conditions for a judgment about the thing itself.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                 | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                             | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                             |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| B021 Anchoring Effect                               | An initial number, value, claim, or reference point pulls later judgment toward it, even when the anchor is arbitrary or weakly relevant. The error is that later adjustment usually remains insufficient.                                            | **Case:** In a salary negotiation, the company first says: “For this role, we are thinking around $65,000.” Even if the candidate originally hoped for $85,000, the conversation now starts near $65,000. A later offer of $72,000 may feel like a gain, although the whole range has been pulled downward by the first number.                                                                         | Ask: What would I estimate before seeing this number? Is the first number evidence, or just a starting point someone gave me? Generate an independent estimate before negotiation or comparison. | One of the major heuristic effects. Related to adjustment and anchoring, reference point, framing effect, price psychology, negotiation, sentencing, forecasting, and planning. |
| B022 Adjustment and Anchoring                        | People often begin from an anchor and then adjust away from it, but the adjustment is usually too small. The error is not only accepting the anchor, but failing to move far enough from it after recognizing it may be wrong.                        | **Case:** A project manager says: “This task should take about two weeks.” The engineer knows the estimate is too optimistic and adjusts: “Maybe three weeks.” In reality, similar tasks have taken six to eight weeks. The engineer corrected the anchor, but only within the psychological neighborhood created by the original number.                                                               | Ask: If I completely ignored the first estimate, what would the estimate be? What does the outside view say? How large would the adjustment be if I started from a different anchor?             | Best treated as the adjustment mechanism within anchoring. Closely related to planning fallacy, outside view, negotiation anchors, and forecasting errors.                      |
