| B023 Contrast Effect                                  | An object is judged differently because it is compared with something immediately before or beside it. The error is treating the comparative impression as an intrinsic property of the object.                                                       | **Case:** A landlord shows a terrible apartment first: dark, noisy, old, and overpriced. The second apartment is only average, but after the first one it feels bright and reasonable. The tenant says: “This one is actually good.” The judgment is partly about the second apartment, but partly about relief after the first.                                                                        | Ask: Would I judge this the same if I saw it alone? What if the comparison order were reversed? What absolute criteria should be used?                                                           | Related to context effect, distinction bias, anchoring, decoy effect, and price framing.                                                                                        |
| B024 Focusing Effect                                 | Attention is concentrated on one salient factor, causing people to overestimate its influence on overall value, happiness, risk, or quality. The error is confusing “what is currently in focus” with “what determines the whole outcome.”            | **Case:** A student choosing between universities focuses almost entirely on ranking. They say: “This school is ranked higher, so it must be the better choice.” Later they discover that course structure, city cost, visa constraints, internship access, and teaching style matter more to their daily life. Ranking was important, but the focus effect made it crowd out the rest of the decision. | Ask: What am I focusing on because it is easy to compare? What important factors are not currently in the spotlight? Would this factor dominate my actual day-to-day experience?                 | Related to WYSIATI, salience bias, affect heuristic, and single-factor decision errors.                                                                                         |
| B025 Framing Effect                                 | Equivalent information leads to different judgments depending on how it is described. The error appears when wording changes the psychological meaning of the same facts.                                                                             | **Case:** A doctor says: “This operation has a 90% survival rate.” Most patients feel reassured. Another doctor says: “This operation has a 10% mortality rate.” Many patients become anxious. The numbers are equivalent, but one frame activates life and survival, while the other activates death and loss.                                                                                         | Ask: Can the same fact be described in the opposite frame? Would I choose the same option if the wording changed from survival to mortality, gain to loss, cost to benefit, action to inaction?  | Central in prospect theory and decision bias. Related to reference point, loss aversion, wording effects, spin, and policy communication.                                       |
| B026 Distinction Bias                                 | When options are evaluated side by side, small differences become exaggerated; when evaluated separately, those differences may matter much less. The error is overvaluing distinctions that are easy to notice in comparison but weak in actual use. | **Case:** Someone compares two laptops in a store. One has slightly better screen brightness and a marginally faster processor. Side by side, the difference feels decisive. After buying the more expensive one, they mostly write documents, browse websites, and watch lectures, and the difference rarely matters. Joint comparison magnified what separate experience would barely notice.         | Ask: Will this difference matter in real use, or only during comparison? If I experienced each option separately, would I still care?                                                            | Related to contrast effect, choice architecture, feature comparison, consumer decisions, and less-is-better effect.                                                             |

### B4. Belief Filtering and Selective Evidence

This group covers biases that occur when beliefs, expectations, identities, or prior theories shape what people search for, notice, remember, and accept as evidence. The shared error is not merely “having a belief.” Beliefs are necessary for thinking. The error appears when belief becomes a filter that protects itself: confirming evidence feels strong, disconfirming evidence feels flawed, and missing evidence does not feel missing.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                         | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                           |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| B027 Confirmation Bias                              | People tend to search for, interpret, remember, and value information that supports existing beliefs. The error is not merely liking supportive evidence, but using a one-sided evidence procedure while feeling objective.                   | **Case:** A person believes a certain city is unsafe. They search “crime in X city,” read three violent incidents, and conclude: “See, I was right.” They never search crime rates, comparisons with similar cities, or long-term trends. Their search method already selected the conclusion.                                                                                    | Ask: What evidence would make me change my mind? Have I searched for disconfirming evidence with the same effort?                                          | Prototype-level bias. Related to congruence bias, selective perception, motivated reasoning, belief perseverance, and bias blind spot.                        |
| B028 Congruence Bias                                 | People test only the hypothesis they already have, instead of testing alternative hypotheses. The error is designing a search that can confirm but cannot seriously distinguish.                                                              | **Case:** A product manager thinks users drop off because the onboarding page is too long. They test a shorter onboarding page and see slight improvement, then declare the hypothesis confirmed. But they never tested loading speed, unclear value proposition, wrong traffic source, or pricing anxiety. The test checked one possible cause while leaving alternatives alive. | Ask: What alternative explanations could produce the same result? Does my test distinguish between them?                                                   | Close to confirmation bias, but more specific: it concerns hypothesis testing and failure to compare rival explanations.                                      |
| B029 Selective Perception                            | Perception itself is influenced by expectations, motives, and prior attitudes. The error occurs when people experience their interpretation as direct perception rather than filtered perception.                                             | **Case:** Two fans watch the same football foul. One says: “That was clearly intentional.” The other says: “He barely touched him.” Both watched the same replay, but team loyalty shaped what seemed obvious. They are not only interpreting differently; they are seeing differently.                                                                                           | Ask: Would this look the same if the teams were reversed? What would a neutral observer notice?                                                            | Related to confirmation bias, attentional bias, motivated perception, naïve realism, and group bias.                                                          |
| B030 Observer-expectancy Effect                    | The observer’s expectations influence the behavior being observed or the interpretation of what is observed. The error is that expectation becomes part of the evidence-generating process.                                                   | **Case:** A teacher is told that several students are “high potential.” Over the semester, the teacher calls on them more, gives warmer feedback, and interprets mistakes as signs of growth. The students may improve partly because the expectation changed the interaction.                                                                                                    | Ask: Did the expectation change how the observer behaved? Would the same behavior be interpreted differently under another label?                          | Related to self-fulfilling prophecy, experimenter bias, labelling effect, and expectancy effects in education and research.                                   |
| B031 Experimenter’s Bias                             | Researchers’ expectations influence experimental design, data collection, coding, exclusion, or interpretation. The error is that the study appears empirical while hidden degrees of freedom steer the result.                               | **Case:** A researcher expects a therapy to work. Ambiguous participant comments are coded as “improvement,” borderline outliers are excluded, and null secondary measures are described as “inconclusive.” No single act is obvious fraud, but many small choices lean in the expected direction.                                                                                | Ask: Were hypotheses pre-registered? Were coders blind? Are exclusion rules fixed before seeing data?                                                      | Related to observer-expectancy effect, confirmation bias, publication bias, p-hacking, and file-drawer problem.                                               |
| B032 Expectation Bias                                | Expectations shape perception, evaluation, or experience. The error occurs when the expected outcome is experienced as if it came from the object itself.                                                                                     | **Case:** Two glasses contain the same wine. One bottle is labeled “premium reserve,” the other “budget table wine.” Tasters describe the first as deeper and more refined. Their sensory experience is partly guided by expectation.                                                                                                                                             | Ask: Would I judge this the same if the label, price, source, or reputation were hidden?                                                                   | Related to placebo effect, labelling effect, halo effect, anchoring, and observer-expectancy effect.                                                          |
| B033 Semmelweis Reflex                             | New evidence is reflexively rejected because it conflicts with established norms, theories, or professional routines. The error is rejecting threat to the framework before evaluating the evidence.                                          | **Case:** A junior analyst finds that a long-used marketing metric is misleading. Senior managers respond: “We have used this for years; if it were wrong, someone would have noticed.” The evidence is dismissed not because it has been refuted, but because accepting it would unsettle the system.                                                                            | Ask: Am I rejecting this because the evidence is weak, or because it threatens an established belief, identity, or practice?                               | Related to confirmation bias, status quo bias, system justification, authority bias, and resistance to paradigm change.                                       |
| B034 Ostrich Effect                                   | People avoid information that may be psychologically painful, threatening, or action-demanding. The error is treating not knowing as if it reduces the underlying problem.                                                                    | **Case:** An investor sees market headlines turning negative and stops opening the brokerage app. They say: “I don’t want to stress myself out.” The portfolio risk does not decrease; only awareness decreases. Avoidance protects emotion while weakening control.                                                                                                              | Ask: What reality am I avoiding because it may require action? Does not checking reduce the risk, or only reduce discomfort?                               | Related to information avoidance, loss aversion, status quo bias, normalcy bias, and denial.                                                                  |
| B035 Post-purchase Rationalization                  | After making a purchase, people strengthen reasons that support the purchase and downplay reasons against it. The error is using later justification to protect the self from regret.                                                         | **Case:** Someone buys an expensive phone mainly because of hype. Afterward, they repeatedly say: “The camera is professional, the design is timeless, and it will last for years.” Some points may be true, but the intensity of the justification comes after the choice, not before it.                                                                                        | Ask: Did I believe these reasons with the same strength before buying? If I had not bought it, would I evaluate it this way?                               | Related to choice-supportive bias, cognitive dissonance reduction, sunk cost, endowment effect, and self-justification.                                       |
| B036 Choice-supportive Bias                         | After choosing an option, people remember or evaluate the chosen option more positively and the rejected option more negatively. The error is that memory and evaluation shift to defend the choice.                                          | **Case:** A student chooses University A over University B. Months later, they remember A’s strengths vividly and B’s weaknesses clearly, but forget that before choosing they were deeply worried about A’s course structure and liked B’s internship access. The chosen path becomes retrospectively cleaner.                                                                   | Ask: What did I think before the choice? Do I have notes from the decision point? What were the real trade-offs?                                           | Related to post-purchase rationalization, memory reconstruction, self-serving bias, and sunk cost.                                                            |
| B037 Backfire Effect                                  | Corrections may sometimes strengthen the original false belief, especially when the belief is tied to identity, group loyalty, or moral commitment. The error is assuming correction is purely informational.                                 | **Case:** A person believes a conspiracy theory about a public event. A friend sends a fact-check. The believer replies: “Of course they would publish that. It proves the cover-up is deeper.” The correction is absorbed into the belief system as further evidence.                                                                                                            | Ask: Is the belief serving identity or belonging? Would direct correction threaten the person’s self-image or group status?                                | Empirical scope is debated. Related to motivated reasoning, identity-protective cognition, confirmation bias, and continued influence effect.                 |
| B038 Bias Blind Spot                                | People recognize cognitive biases in others more easily than in themselves. The error is using bias theory as a tool for diagnosing opponents while treating one’s own view as neutral perception.                                            | **Case:** After learning about confirmation bias, someone says: “This explains why my political opponents are so irrational.” They do not ask how their own media diet, identity, and emotional investments shape their judgment. Bias knowledge becomes another weapon of bias.                                                                                                  | Ask: How would this bias appear in my own reasoning? What would an opponent say I am failing to notice?                                                    | Prototype metacognitive bias. Related to naïve realism, overconfidence, motivated reasoning, and intellectual arrogance.                                      |
| B039 Naïve Cynicism                                 | People overestimate the degree to which others’ beliefs or actions are driven by selfish, corrupt, or biased motives. The error is mistaking suspicion for insight.                                                                           | **Case:** A researcher publishes a conclusion someone dislikes. The response is: “They must be funded by someone.” No evidence of corruption is provided. Instead of examining method, data, or reasoning, the critic treats hidden motive as the easiest explanation.                                                                                                            | Ask: What evidence supports the motive claim? Are there ordinary reasons someone might honestly disagree?                                                  | Related to hostile attribution bias, fundamental attribution error, conspiracy thinking, and cynicism as status performance.                                  |
| B040 Naïve Realism                                | People experience their own view as reality itself, rather than as a perspective shaped by information, values, concepts, and social position. The error is assuming that disagreement must come from ignorance, irrationality, or bad faith. | **Case:** In a policy argument, one person says: “I’m not ideological. I’m just looking at the facts. Anyone who disagrees is either stupid or dishonest.” They do not notice that fact selection, importance weighting, and interpretation already depend on a perspective.                                                                                                      | Ask: What assumptions make this view feel obvious to me? What would the same facts look like from another position?                                        | Central social-cognitive bias. Related to bias blind spot, confirmation bias, selective perception, group polarization, and moral disagreement.               |

## C. Meaning Completion, Pattern Recognition, and Causal Interpretation Biases

When information is incomplete, the mind does not leave it incomplete. It supplies patterns, categories, causes, intentions, and stories. This is necessary for understanding the world, but it also creates bias. The shared error in this class is that the mind often treats coherence as evidence: if something looks like a category, it is judged as belonging to that category; if one trait is salient, it spreads to the whole person; if a description feels personally fitting, it is treated as accurate.

### C1. Representativeness, Similarity, and Category Judgment

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                             |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| C001 Representativeness Heuristic                 | People judge probability or category membership by how much something resembles a stereotype, prototype, or familiar pattern. The error appears when similarity replaces probability, especially when base rates are ignored.                                         | **Case:** A description says: “Linda is outspoken, majored in philosophy, and was deeply concerned with discrimination and social justice.” Many people judge “Linda is a bank teller and active in the feminist movement” as more likely than “Linda is a bank teller.” The richer description fits the image better, but logically the combined event cannot be more probable than one of its parts. | Ask: Am I judging by resemblance or by probability? What is the base rate? Does adding detail make the claim more vivid but less probable?                 | Prototype-level bias. Closely related to conjunction fallacy, base-rate neglect, stereotyping, halo effect, and illusion of validity.                           |
| C002 Halo Effect                                      | A positive impression in one dimension spreads into unrelated dimensions. The mind uses one salient good trait as a shortcut for overall quality. The error is allowing one virtue, status marker, or attractive feature to substitute for separate evaluation.       | **Case:** A founder gives a charismatic presentation: confident voice, elegant slides, strong personal story. Investors begin saying, “She must be a great operator.” But charisma in a pitch does not prove hiring ability, financial discipline, product judgment, or resilience under crisis. One bright trait has illuminated the whole object.                                                    | Ask: Which specific trait is influencing the whole evaluation? Have I separately assessed the relevant dimensions?                                         | Related to authority bias, attractiveness bias, prestige bias, brand effect, and representativeness.                                                            |
| C003 Horn Effect                                       | A negative impression in one dimension contaminates broader evaluation. The mind uses one disliked trait, mistake, or appearance cue as evidence of general inferiority.                                                                                              | **Case:** A candidate arrives five minutes late to an interview because a train line was suspended. The interviewer immediately feels the candidate is careless and disorganized, then interprets later answers through that lens. A single negative first impression becomes a general character judgment.                                                                                            | Ask: Which negative cue is contaminating my whole evaluation? Would the same later behavior look different if the first cue were absent?                   | Reverse of halo effect. Related to negativity bias, fundamental attribution error, first-impression effects, and stereotyping.                                  |
| C004 Stereotyping                                   | Category knowledge is used to infer individual traits, abilities, preferences, or behavior. It can reduce cognitive load, but becomes biased when a group-level expectation overrides individual evidence or when the category itself is distorted by social history. | **Case:** A manager hears that a candidate studied philosophy and says: “Probably not practical enough for product work.” The candidate’s actual portfolio includes user research, data analysis, and shipped projects, but the category “philosophy student” has already filled in personality and ability.                                                                                           | Ask: What individual evidence do I have? Am I using a category as a starting hypothesis or as a conclusion?                                                | Related to prejudice, implicit bias, representativeness, group attribution error, and base-rate reasoning.                                                      |
| C005 Implicit Stereotype                            | Automatic associations connect social categories with traits, roles, or behaviors without deliberate endorsement. The error is that a person may sincerely reject a stereotype while still being influenced by it in fast judgment.                                   | **Case:** A teacher consciously believes girls and boys are equally capable in mathematics. Yet during class, she more often calls on boys for difficult technical questions and praises girls for neatness and effort. The explicit belief is egalitarian, but automatic role associations still shape behavior.                                                                                      | Ask: Are my actions aligned with my explicit beliefs? What do behavioral records show about whom I call on, hire, praise, interrupt, or trust?             | Related to implicit bias, implicit association, affinity bias, gender/race bias, and stereotype threat.                                                         |
| C006 Essentialism                                     | The mind treats a category as if its members share a deep inner essence that explains their behavior. The error is converting historical, social, institutional, or situational patterns into fixed nature.                                                           | **Case:** Someone says: “People from that region are naturally aggressive in business.” This turns a possible mixture of local industry structure, competition, migration history, education, and social expectation into an inner essence. The explanation feels deep because it is simple, but it erases mechanism.                                                                                  | Ask: Am I explaining behavior by an alleged essence? What historical, institutional, incentive-based, or situational mechanisms could explain the pattern? | Related to stereotyping, racism, sexism, group attribution error, naturalization, and genetic or cultural determinism.                                          |
| C007 Subjective Validation                            | People accept vague, general, or ambiguous statements as personally accurate when they expect them to be meaningful. The error is treating personal resonance as evidence of diagnostic accuracy.                                                                     | **Case:** A fortune-telling description says: “You are independent, but you sometimes need reassurance. You dislike being controlled, but you also fear making the wrong choice.” The reader says: “That is exactly me.” The statement feels precise because the reader supplies personal memories that fit it.                                                                                        | Ask: Would most people also find this statement accurate? Is the statement specific enough that it could clearly be false?                                 | Related to Barnum effect, Forer effect, confirmation bias, astrology, personality tests, and cold reading.                                                      |
| C008 Barnum Effect                                   | People perceive very general descriptions as uniquely applicable to themselves. The error is that a high-fit feeling is produced by broad applicability, not by genuine individual diagnosis.                                                                         | **Case:** A workplace personality test says: “You value honesty, but you sometimes avoid conflict. You can work independently, yet you appreciate recognition.” Several employees read the same report and each says: “This profile really understands me.” The description succeeds because it is flexible enough to fit many people.                                                                 | Ask: How many people would accept the same description? Does it make risky, specific predictions, or only safe and flattering claims?                      | Often used interchangeably with Forer effect. Subjective validation is the broader mechanism; Barnum effect is a common form.                                   |

### C2. Pattern, Randomness, and False Correlation

This group covers errors in detecting patterns. The mind is built to find order, because real patterns often matter: disease clusters, repeated mistakes, market signals, social tendencies, and causal regularities can all be important. The shared error is over-detection. People see structure in noise, causal direction in coincidence, trend in small samples, and meaningful correlation in selective memory. These biases are dangerous because they often feel like discovery: “I noticed something others missed.”

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                            | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                       |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| C009 Illusory Correlation                           | People perceive a relationship between two variables when the relationship is absent, weak, or exaggerated. Distinctive events and emotionally charged pairings are especially likely to be linked in memory.                                    | **Case:** A manager says: “Remote workers are less reliable. Every time there is a missed deadline, it seems to be someone working from home.” But they do not count remote workers who meet deadlines, office workers who miss deadlines, or the total number of each group. The remembered pair “remote + missed deadline” becomes a correlation without a full comparison table.      | Ask: Have I counted all four cells: A with B, A without B, non-A with B, and non-A without B?                                          | A major judgment illusion in Pohl’s framework. Related to stereotyping, confirmation bias, availability, prejudice, and fourfold-table reasoning.         |
| C010 Pareidolia                                      | The mind perceives meaningful images, especially faces or agents, in ambiguous or random stimuli. This is a by-product of a useful pattern-detection system that is tuned to detect socially important forms quickly.                            | **Case:** A person sees a face in the pattern of a burnt piece of toast and says: “This looks like a sign.” The perceptual system is doing what it often does well—detecting face-like structure—but the meaning is supplied by interpretation, not by the stimulus itself.                                                                                                              | Ask: Would independent observers see the same pattern without suggestion? Is the pattern stable, repeatable, and specific?             | Related to apophenia, anthropomorphism, agency detection, superstition, and pattern over-detection.                                                       |
| C011 Anthropomorphism                                  | Human traits, intentions, emotions, or motives are attributed to nonhuman animals, machines, markets, algorithms, institutions, or natural processes. The error is replacing mechanism with personality or intention.                            | **Case:** A user says: “The algorithm hates me. Every time I post something important, it hides my content.” The platform may indeed rank posts poorly, but the mechanism could involve engagement prediction, timing, topic category, follower behavior, or moderation flags. Treating the algorithm as a resentful person blocks causal analysis.                                      | Ask: What mechanism would produce this result without intention? What inputs, incentives, rules, or constraints are operating?         | Related to agency detection, illusion of external agency, conspiracy thinking, animism, and intentionality bias.                                          |
| C012 Illusion of Validity                          | A coherent judgment feels accurate because the information forms a consistent pattern. The error is that internal coherence is mistaken for predictive validity.                                                                                 | **Case:** An interviewer spends 30 minutes with a candidate and says: “I can just tell he will be a strong leader.” The candidate’s answers, posture, and career story fit together neatly. But if interview predictions have low validity in that organization, the interviewer’s confidence comes from a coherent impression, not from reliable predictive evidence.                   | Ask: Does this judgment have a track record? Is confidence based on predictive accuracy or on a coherent story?                        | Related to representativeness, halo effect, overconfidence, interview bias, and WYSIATI.                                                                  |
| C013 Anecdotal Fallacy                                | A vivid personal story or isolated case is used as if it outweighed systematic evidence. The error is not using anecdotes at all, but letting a story replace sample size, comparison, and base rates.                                           | **Case:** Someone says: “My uncle smoked every day and lived to 92, so smoking can’t be that dangerous.” The anecdote is real, but it does not answer the statistical question. It selects a survivor and ignores millions of people whose outcomes went the other way.                                                                                                                  | Ask: What does the larger dataset show? Is this case typical, rare, or selected because it is memorable?                               | Related to availability heuristic, survivorship bias, base-rate neglect, and vividness effects.                                                           |

### C3. Causality, Responsibility, and Moral Order

This group covers biases that impose moral and causal order on complex events. The mind does not merely ask “what happened?” It also asks “who caused it?”, “who deserves blame?”, “who deserves the outcome?”, and “what kind of world would make this event understandable?” These biases share a common error: they turn uncertainty, accident, structure, and multiple causation into a morally satisfying explanation. The resulting story may feel fair, deep, or psychologically comforting, but it often overassigns intention, responsibility, blame, or legitimacy.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                               | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                  | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                            |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| C014 Just-world Hypothesis                        | People are motivated to believe that the world is basically fair, so outcomes are interpreted as deserved. The error is turning misfortune into evidence of personal fault, because a random or unjust world feels threatening.     | **Case:** A worker is laid off during an industry downturn. Instead of asking about automation, declining demand, company debt, or restructuring incentives, others say: “He probably didn’t work hard enough. Good employees don’t get fired.” This protects the comforting belief that bad outcomes happen mainly to people who deserve them. | Ask: What structural, situational, random, or institutional factors contributed? Am I blaming the victim because injustice feels psychologically uncomfortable?       | Related to victim blaming, defensive attribution, moral luck, system justification, and fundamental attribution error.                         |
| C015 Moral Credential Effect                        | A previous moral action or identity gives people a feeling of moral license, making later biased or questionable behavior feel less problematic. The error is using past virtue as evidence that current judgment cannot be biased. | **Case:** A manager says: “I have always supported women in leadership, so my criticism of this female candidate cannot be sexist.” The criticism may be valid, but the past record is being used as a shield against examining whether the present evaluation applies the same standards to male and female candidates.                        | Ask: Am I using previous good behavior as an exemption from present scrutiny? Would I apply the same standard to someone without my moral record?                     | Related to moral licensing, self-serving bias, implicit bias, social desirability bias, and identity protection.                               |
| C016 Moral Luck                                       | People judge moral responsibility partly by outcomes that depend on luck. The error is evaluating the agent as if the final consequence were entirely under their control.                                                          | **Case:** Two drivers both text while driving through similar intersections. One reaches home safely; the other hits a pedestrian in a crosswalk. The second driver is judged as far more morally monstrous, even though the negligent action was similar and the difference in outcome partly depended on circumstance. The outcome matters legally and practically, but luck has changed moral judgment.                | Ask: Which part was under the person’s control, and which part depended on outcome luck? Would I judge the same action differently if the outcome had been different? | A philosophical concept with strong cognitive relevance. Related to outcome bias, hindsight bias, blame attribution, and legal responsibility. |
| C017 Intentionality Bias                             | People overattribute intention to ambiguous or accidental behavior. The error is assuming that if an action harmed or affected us, it was probably meant to do so.                                                                  | **Case:** A colleague does not greet someone in the hallway. The person immediately thinks: “She ignored me on purpose.” Later it turns out the colleague was distracted before a difficult meeting. The behavior was real, but the intentional explanation was added too quickly.                                                              | Ask: What non-intentional explanations could produce the same behavior? Do I have evidence of intention, or only evidence of effect?                                  | Related to hostile attribution bias, anthropomorphism, conspiracy thinking, and fundamental attribution error.                                 |
| C018 Hostile Attribution Bias                       | Ambiguous behavior is interpreted as hostile, disrespectful, or malicious. The error is treating uncertainty as threat and filling ambiguity with aggression.                                                                       | **Case:** In a group chat, someone writes: “We should revise this part.” One teammate reads it as: “They are saying my work is bad.” The comment may simply be task-focused, but under insecurity or conflict history, ambiguous feedback becomes an attack.                                                                                    | Ask: What neutral, practical, or benign explanations are possible? What exactly was said, and what did I infer beyond it?                                             | Related to intentionality bias, naïve cynicism, rejection sensitivity, conflict escalation, and attribution bias.                              |
| C019 System Justification                          | People tend to defend, rationalize, or naturalize existing social, institutional, or organizational arrangements. The error is treating the mere persistence of a system as evidence of its legitimacy or necessity.                | **Case:** Employees complain that promotion criteria are opaque and favor insiders. A senior manager replies: “This is how the industry works. If people are really good, they rise.” The current system is treated as self-validating. The possibility that the rules themselves create unequal outcomes is dismissed before analysis.         | Ask: Am I treating the existing arrangement as justified because it exists? Who benefits from this system, and who bears its costs?                                   | Related to status quo bias, just-world belief, authority bias, hierarchy defense, and ideological rationalization.                             |

### C4. Mind-Reading, Perspective, and Social Visibility

This group covers biases that arise when people infer what others know, see, feel, intend, or understand. Social life requires mind-reading: we constantly guess what is obvious to others, how visible we are, what another person meant, and why they acted. The shared error is perspective failure. People overproject their own knowledge onto others, overestimate how visible their inner states are, overestimate how much others notice them, or assume they understand others better than others understand them.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                            | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                              |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| C020 Curse of Knowledge                             | Once people know something, they find it difficult to simulate not knowing it. The error is assuming that what is obvious after learning is also obvious before learning.                                                        | **Case:** A programmer explains recursion to a beginner and says: “It’s simple — a function just calls itself.” The beginner is still confused. The programmer becomes impatient because the explanation feels clear from inside expert knowledge. What the beginner lacks is not intelligence, but the mental structure that makes the explanation obvious.          | Ask: What does the listener not yet know? What background concepts am I silently assuming? Could I explain this without using the target concept?                      | Related to expert blind spot, illusion of transparency, teaching failure, jargon, and communication bias.                        |
| C021 Illusion of Transparency                      | People overestimate how visible their internal states are to others. The error is assuming that nervousness, guilt, attraction, confusion, or intention leaks out more clearly than it actually does.                            | **Case:** A student gives a presentation and feels their voice shaking, heart racing, and mind going blank. Afterward they say: “Everyone could tell I was panicking.” In reality, classmates noticed only a short pause and some fast speaking. The internal experience was intense, but not as externally transparent as it felt.                                   | Ask: What did others actually observe? Am I confusing how strongly I felt something with how clearly others could see it?                                              | Related to spotlight effect, social anxiety, egocentric bias, and mind-reading errors.                                           |
| C022 Spotlight Effect                              | People overestimate how much others notice their appearance, mistakes, awkwardness, or performance. The error is treating oneself as more central in others’ attention than one actually is.                                     | **Case:** Someone arrives at class wearing a shirt with a small stain. For the whole lecture, they think: “Everyone must be looking at it.” Later, no one remembers the stain. Other people were mostly thinking about their own notes, messages, deadlines, and worries.                                                                                             | Ask: How much attention do others realistically have for this? What were they likely focused on besides me?                                                            | Related to illusion of transparency, self-consciousness, social anxiety, and egocentric bias.                                    |
| C023 Streetlight Effect                               | People search for answers where looking is easiest, not necessarily where the answer is most likely to be found. The error is letting accessibility of evidence define the search space.                                         | **Case:** A company wants to understand why users cancel subscriptions. Instead of interviewing churned users, it analyzes only active users because those data are already clean and accessible. The team finds what satisfied users like, but learns little about why dissatisfied users left. The search occurs under the streetlight, not where the key was lost. | Ask: Am I looking here because the evidence is relevant, or because it is easy to access? What important evidence is harder to obtain?                                 | Related to availability heuristic, sampling bias, survivorship bias, convenience samples, and measurement bias.                  |
| C024 Illusion of Asymmetric Insight              | People believe they understand others’ motives, biases, and inner life better than others understand them. The error is applying psychological depth to oneself and one’s group, while flattening others into simpler motives.   | **Case:** In a political argument, one side says: “We understand why they think this way — they are scared and manipulated. But they don’t understand us, because our position is based on real principles.” The other side says almost the same thing in reverse. Each group grants itself depth and grants the opponent diagnosis.                                  | Ask: Would the other side describe the asymmetry in the same way? Am I giving myself complexity and giving others a simple motive?                                     | Related to naïve realism, bias blind spot, out-group homogeneity bias, attribution error, and group polarization.                |
| C025 Illusion of External Agency                   | Complex events are attributed to a hidden external agent, controller, or intentional force when distributed mechanisms may be sufficient. The error is converting structural or emergent processes into personal control.        | **Case:** A video suddenly receives few views. The creator says: “The platform must be suppressing me.” Suppression is possible in some cases, but low views may also result from timing, topic saturation, weak retention, follower inactivity, recommendation competition, or random variation. A complex system is reimagined as a single hostile actor.           | Ask: What distributed mechanisms could produce this outcome without a central intention? What evidence would distinguish intentional suppression from system dynamics? | Related to anthropomorphism, conspiracy thinking, intentionality bias, pattern detection, and attribution error.                 |
| C026 Extrinsic Incentive Error                      | People overestimate how much others are motivated by external rewards such as money, status, grades, or punishment, while underestimating intrinsic motives such as interest, identity, duty, curiosity, care, or craftsmanship. | **Case:** A manager says: “Employees only care about bonuses. If we increase the reward, they will work harder.” But some employees are demotivated because the work feels meaningless, their judgment is ignored, and quality is not respected. The manager sees incentive, but misses autonomy, mastery, belonging, and purpose.                                    | Ask: Am I assuming others are driven only by external rewards? What intrinsic, relational, or identity-based motives might matter?                                     | Related to motivation crowding, self-determination theory, fundamental attribution error, managerial bias, and incentive design. |

## D. Probability, Statistics, and Numerical Judgment Biases

This class covers errors that arise when the mind must reason with probabilities, distributions, samples, denominators, and uncertainty. The shared problem is that intuition prefers stories, similarity, and vivid cases, while statistical judgment requires base rates, comparison classes, conditional structure, and invisible counterexamples. These biases are central because they often appear in high-stakes fields: medicine, law, investment, policy, risk assessment, hiring, and scientific interpretation.

### D1. Base Rates, Bayesian Reasoning, and Conditional Probability

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                          | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                           | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                      |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| D001 Base-rate Neglect                             | People underweight or ignore the general prevalence of a category or event when vivid individuating information is available. The error is letting a description, symptom, or story override the prior probability.            | **Case:** A profile says: “Chen is quiet, precise, enjoys reading alone, and dislikes parties.” Many people judge that Chen is more likely to be a librarian than a salesperson. But if there are far more salespeople than librarians in the relevant population, the base rate matters. The personality description may fit the librarian stereotype, but resemblance is not probability. | Ask first: how common is this category in the relevant population? Then ask how strongly the new evidence should update that starting point.   | Closely related to representativeness heuristic, Bayesian reasoning error, stereotyping, conjunction fallacy, and diagnostic reasoning.                  |
| D002 Base-rate Fallacy                               | A reasoning error in which a conclusion is drawn from a specific signal while neglecting the prior probability of the hypothesis. It is the argumentative form of base-rate neglect.                                           | **Case:** A security system flags someone as “high risk.” The officer says: “The system is 95% accurate, so this person is probably dangerous.” But if genuinely dangerous people are extremely rare, a large share of flagged people may be false positives. The argument uses test accuracy as if it were the probability that the flagged person is truly dangerous.                     | Ask: What was the prior probability before the signal? Among all people flagged positive, how many are true positives?                         | Often overlaps with base-rate neglect. Useful when the bias appears inside an explicit argument, diagnosis, or institutional decision.                   |
| D003 Bayesian Inference Error                    | People confuse prior probability, likelihood, false-positive rate, and posterior probability. The error is failing to update belief according to both the base rate and the diagnostic value of the new evidence.              | **Case:** A disease affects 1 in 1000 people. A test detects 99% of real cases but also falsely flags 5% of healthy people. A patient tests positive and thinks: “I almost certainly have it.” But among 1000 people, about 1 true patient may test positive, while about 50 healthy people may also test positive. A positive result is serious, but not near certainty.                   | Draw a frequency tree: out of a fixed population, count true positives, false positives, true negatives, and false negatives.                  | Core statistical reasoning issue. Related to base-rate neglect, natural frequencies, conditional probability confusion, and medical-test interpretation. |
| D004 Statistical Format Effect                      | The format in which probabilistic information is presented changes reasoning performance. Percentages and abstract probabilities often obscure the denominator; frequencies and visual trees often make the structure clearer. | **Case:** A lawyer hears: “The DNA profile occurs in only 1 out of 100,000 people.” This sounds decisive. But if the suspect pool contains millions, many people may match by chance. Expressing the information as expected matches in a population makes the reasoning less misleading.                                                                                                   | Convert abstract probabilities into frequencies. Ask: out of how many people, how many would match, test positive, or be falsely accused?      | Not a bias itself, but a source of bias or correction depending on the format. Related to natural frequency effect and prosecutor’s fallacy.             |
| D005 Natural Frequency Effect                       | People reason more accurately when probabilities are expressed as natural counts within a population. This format reduces error by making the denominator and comparison groups visible.                                       | **Case:** Instead of saying “the false-positive rate is 10%,” a doctor says: “Out of 1000 people without the disease, about 100 will still test positive. Out of 10 people with the disease, about 9 will test positive.” The patient can now compare true positives and false positives in the same frame.                                                                                 | Use “out of N people” statements. Draw branches for condition present/absent and test positive/negative.                                       | A debiasing effect, not a bias. Related to Bayesian inference, denominator neglect, statistical literacy, and risk communication.                        |
| D006 Conditional Probability Confusion              | People confuse the probability of evidence given a hypothesis with the probability of the hypothesis given the evidence. The error is reversing the condition.                                                                 | **Case:** A teacher says: “Most top students submit assignments early.” A student then concludes: “If I submit early, I am probably a top student.” But P(early submission \| top student) is not the same as P(top student \| early submission). Many non-top students may also submit early.                                                                                                | Ask: What is the condition after the vertical bar? Am I asking “evidence given hypothesis” or “hypothesis given evidence”?                     | Related to inverse fallacy, prosecutor’s fallacy, Bayesian reasoning error, and diagnostic reasoning.                                                    |
| D007 Prosecutor’s Fallacy                            | A legal form of conditional probability confusion. The probability of observing the evidence if the defendant is innocent is mistaken for the probability that the defendant is innocent given the evidence.                   | **Case:** In court, the prosecutor says: “Only 1 in a million people would match this trace evidence by chance. Therefore, there is only a 1 in a million chance the defendant is innocent.” This reverses the probability. If the search population is very large, or if other evidence is weak, the posterior probability may be very different.                                          | Ask: Is the statement about P(evidence \| innocence) or P(innocence \| evidence)? What is the relevant suspect population and prior probability? | Crucial in legal reasoning. Related to base-rate fallacy, DNA evidence interpretation, inverse probability, and wrongful conviction risk.                |
| D008 Inverse Fallacy                                 | People treat P(A given B) as if it were interchangeable with P(B given A). The error is assuming that a strong relationship in one direction automatically holds in the reverse direction.                                     | **Case:** A commentator says: “Most cybercriminals use encryption, so anyone using encryption is suspicious.” The first statement may be true, but the reverse does not follow. Journalists, lawyers, businesses, activists, and ordinary users also use encryption.                                                                                                                        | Ask: Does the reverse conditional actually hold? How common is B among non-A cases?                                                            | Related to conditional probability confusion, prosecutor’s fallacy, base-rate neglect, stereotyping, and guilt by association.                           |

### D2. Conjunction, Denominator, and Probability Weight

This group covers errors in the structure of probability judgment. The mind often responds to narrative richness, emotional intensity, numerator size, or the psychological appeal of certainty, while probability requires attention to logical inclusion, denominators, base populations, and risk magnitude. The shared error is that people treat a more detailed story, a more frightening outcome, a larger visible number, or a zero-risk label as if it automatically means greater probability or better choice.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                   | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                           | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                   |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| D009 Conjunction Fallacy                            | People judge a combined event as more probable than one of its parts when the combined description is more coherent, representative, or narratively satisfying. The error violates the basic probability rule that A and B together cannot be more likely than A alone. | **Case:** A profile says: “Maya studied environmental law, volunteers for climate groups, and often criticizes corporate pollution.” Many people judge “Maya is a lawyer and works for an environmental NGO” as more likely than “Maya is a lawyer.” The first description feels more fitting, but it adds a condition and must be less probable or equal.        | Ask: Does the more detailed statement include the simpler statement plus an extra condition? If yes, can it logically be more probable?                        | Closely related to representativeness heuristic, Linda problem, base-rate neglect, narrative coherence, and stereotype-based reasoning.               |
| D010 Subadditivity Effect                           | People often assign a higher total probability to an event when it is broken into detailed subcategories than when it is judged as a whole. The error is that unpacking makes possibilities more imaginable and therefore more probable-feeling.                        | **Case:** Asked “What is the chance that this project fails?” a manager says 30%. Asked separately about failure from engineering delays, weak demand, budget cuts, legal issues, and team conflict, the manager assigns 15%, 20%, 10%, 10%, and 15%. The unpacked list now totals far above the original estimate because each cause becomes mentally available. | Ask: Do the subcategory probabilities add up consistently with the overall probability? Are the categories mutually exclusive, overlapping, or double-counted? | Related to availability, unpacking effect, denominator neglect, planning fallacy, and risk assessment.                                                |
| D011 Denominator Neglect                            | People focus on the number of cases in the numerator while neglecting the total population from which those cases come. The error is treating visible counts as meaningful without asking “out of how many?”                                                            | **Case:** A headline says: “300 people reported side effects after taking this medicine.” Readers become alarmed. But if 20 million people took it, the rate may be extremely low. If 500 people took it, the rate is catastrophic. The numerator alone cannot tell the risk.                                                                                     | Ask: Out of how many? What is the rate, not just the count? What is the comparison rate for alternatives?                                                      | Related to base-rate neglect, ratio bias, availability, media risk reporting, and medical decision-making.                                            |
| D012 Ratio Bias                                      | People are influenced by the absolute number of favorable or unfavorable cases rather than the actual ratio. Larger numerators feel more compelling even when the proportion is worse.                                                                                  | **Case:** A person can draw from Bowl A with 1 winning ticket out of 10, or Bowl B with 9 winning tickets out of 100. Many feel tempted by Bowl B because “9 winning tickets” sounds better than “1 winning ticket,” although the probability is lower.                                                                                                           | Ask: What is the actual proportion? Am I reacting to the numerator or the ratio? Convert both options to percentages or natural frequencies.                   | Related to denominator neglect, probability neglect, numeracy, framing, and risk communication.                                                       |
| D013 Probability Neglect                            | When an outcome is emotionally vivid, people may respond mainly to the severity of the outcome and neglect its probability. The error is treating fear or hope as if it were risk size.                                                                                 | **Case:** A parent hears about a terrifying but extremely rare school accident and immediately wants to ban all school trips. The event is emotionally unbearable, so the probability becomes psychologically irrelevant. The policy reaction is driven by the image of the worst case, not by expected risk.                                                     | Ask: How severe is the outcome, and how likely is it? Am I reacting to magnitude without probability? What is the expected harm compared with alternatives?    | Related to availability, dread risk, identifiable victim effect, zero-risk bias, and risk perception.                                                 |
| D014 Appeal to Probability Fallacy                 | A possibility is treated as if it were probable, actual, or decisive. The error is moving from “could happen” to “will happen” or “must be treated as true” without probability or evidence.                                                                            | **Case:** In a workplace dispute, someone says: “It is possible that he deleted the file on purpose, so we should assume he sabotaged the project.” The possibility may deserve investigation, but possibility alone is not evidence of likelihood. Many other explanations may also be possible.                                                                 | Ask: How probable is this possibility? What evidence favors it over other possibilities? Are we treating a mere possibility as a conclusion?                   | More a logical fallacy than a standard cognitive bias, but psychologically related to probability neglect, fear, suspicion, and conspiracy reasoning. |
| D015 Pseudocertainty Effect                         | People prefer options that appear to eliminate risk in a subpart of a decision, even when the total risk is not eliminated. The error is mistaking local certainty for overall certainty.                                                                               | **Case:** A travel insurance plan advertises: “100% protection against baggage loss.” A buyer prefers it over a cheaper plan that reduces several larger risks, including medical evacuation and trip cancellation. The “100%” label creates local certainty, while the overall risk profile may be worse.                                                        | Ask: Certainty about what part of the problem? What risks remain? Does this option reduce total expected harm or only create a pocket of certainty?            | Related to zero-risk bias, framing effect, loss aversion, prospect theory, and insurance decisions.                                                   |
| D016 Zero-risk Bias                                | People prefer reducing a small risk to zero over reducing a larger risk by a greater amount. The psychological appeal of “zero” can outweigh total risk reduction.                                                                                                      | **Case:** A city can spend the same budget in two ways: eliminate a tiny chemical exposure that may cause 1 expected illness per year, or reduce traffic risk enough to prevent 20 expected injuries per year. The public strongly supports eliminating the chemical risk because “zero contamination” feels cleaner and safer.                                   | Ask: How much risk is actually reduced? Are we choosing zero because it reduces more harm, or because zero feels psychologically special?                      | Related to probability neglect, pseudocertainty effect, loss aversion, risk perception, and public policy trade-offs.                                 |

### D3. Samples, Randomness, and Regression

This group covers errors that arise when people interpret samples, streaks, extremes, and selected cases. The mind expects small samples to look like large populations, expects randomness to look balanced, treats extreme outcomes as meaningful, and often studies only the cases that survived. The shared error is confusing what is visible in a small or selected sample with the structure of the whole population. These biases are especially common in education, sports, investment, business success stories, policy evaluation, and everyday causal reasoning.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                    | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                    | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                         |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| D017 Law of Small Numbers                           | People expect small samples to resemble the larger population more closely than they actually do. The error is treating a few observations as if they already reveal the stable pattern.                                                                 | **Case:** A teacher tries a new reading method with 5 students.
