Four improve in one month, so the teacher says: “This method works.” But with such a small sample, improvement may come from student motivation, parental help, easier materials, chance fluctuation, or regression to the mean. The sample is too small to carry the conclusion being placed on it. | Ask: How large is the sample? How much random variation should be expected? Would I trust the conclusion if the next 5 cases went the other way?                        | Central in Kahneman’s account of statistical intuition. Related to sample-size neglect, clustering illusion, overgeneralization, and anecdotal evidence.    |
| D018 Insensitivity to Sample Size                   | People neglect the fact that small samples produce more extreme results and less stable estimates than large samples. The error is judging sample results without adjusting confidence for sample size.                                                  | **Case:** A news report says: “A small school has the highest test scores in the region.” People conclude that the school must be excellent. But small schools are more likely to appear at both the top and bottom simply because small samples fluctuate more. Extreme performance may reflect instability rather than true superiority.                           | Ask: How many observations produced this result? Would a larger sample produce a more stable estimate? What is the confidence interval?                                 | Closely related to law of small numbers. Important in school rankings, hospital rankings, product reviews, and A/B testing.                                 |
| D019 Regression to the Mean                         | Extreme observations partly caused by chance tend to be followed by less extreme observations. The error is interpreting this natural statistical return as the effect of praise, punishment, training, reform, or intervention.                         | **Case:** A sales manager publicly criticizes the worst salespeople after a terrible quarter. Next quarter, many of them improve. The manager concludes: “Public pressure works.” But the worst performers were selected because their last results were unusually low; some rebound would likely occur even without the criticism.                                  | Ask: Was the case selected because it was extreme? What happens to similar extreme cases without intervention? Is there a comparison group?                             | A statistical principle, not a bias itself. Misunderstanding it produces regression fallacy, false causal inference, and misleading performance evaluation. |
| D020 Regression Fallacy                               | A causal explanation is invented for a change that is actually partly or wholly due to regression to the mean. The error is assigning credit or blame to an intervention simply because it occurred between an extreme result and a less extreme result. | **Case:** A parent punishes a child after one unusually bad exam. The next exam is closer to the child’s usual level. The parent says: “Punishment improved the score.” If the bad exam was an outlier, the next score might have improved anyway. The timing creates the illusion of causation.                                                                     | Ask: Did the intervention happen after an unusually extreme measurement? Would similar cases naturally move back toward average?                                        | Distinct from regression to the mean: regression is the statistical tendency; regression fallacy is the mistaken causal interpretation.                     |
| D021 Clustering Illusion                              | Random data naturally contains clusters, streaks, and unevenness, but people often treat these as meaningful patterns. The error is expecting randomness to look smooth and evenly distributed.                                                          | **Case:** A lottery player sees that the numbers 7, 17, and 27 appeared close together over recent draws and says: “The system is favoring sevens.” Random sequences often produce apparent clusters. The visible pattern feels designed because the mind underestimates how messy randomness is.                                                                    | Ask: How often would this pattern appear by chance? Did I define the pattern before looking, or only after seeing the data?                                             | Also appears in C2 as a pattern-recognition bias. Here the emphasis is on sample randomness and post hoc pattern selection.                                 |
| D022 Gambler’s Fallacy                                | People believe that an independent random process is “due” to balance itself after a streak. The error is applying long-run balance to short-run independent trials.                                                                                     | **Case:** A coin lands heads six times in a row. Someone says: “Tails is more likely now.” But if the coin is fair, the next toss remains 50/50. The sequence looks imbalanced, but the coin has no memory and no need to repair the past.                                                                                                                           | Ask: Are these trials independent? Does the process remember previous outcomes? Am I forcing a small sequence to look like the long-run distribution?                   | Also part of C2. Related to law of small numbers, clustering illusion, randomness misunderstanding, and hot-hand reasoning.                                 |
| D023 Hot-hand Fallacy                                 | People infer that a short streak of success indicates a higher probability of continued success. The error is treating recent success as a stable change in ability or state without enough evidence.                                                    | **Case:** A trader makes three profitable trades in a row and says: “I’m seeing the market clearly today.” They increase position size on the fourth trade. The streak may reflect skill, but it may also reflect luck, favorable market movement, or selective memory of wins. A short run is not enough to prove a changed probability.                            | Ask: What is the larger performance record? Is this streak statistically unusual compared with ordinary variation? What would count as evidence of a real state change? | Empirical status varies by domain. In some sports or skilled activities, state changes may exist; in random or noisy domains, streaks are often overread.   |
| D024 Survivorship Bias                             | People draw conclusions from visible successes while ignoring failures, dropouts, or cases that did not survive the selection process. The error is studying only what remains and mistaking it for the whole population.                                | **Case:** A motivational article lists habits of billionaires: waking early, reading daily, taking risks, and ignoring critics. It ignores thousands of people who woke early, read daily, took risks, ignored critics, and went bankrupt. The visible winners make the habits look causal because the invisible non-winners are missing.                            | Ask: Who is absent from this sample? What happened to people who tried the same thing and failed? What selection process determined which cases became visible?         | Major cross-domain bias. Related to publication bias, availability, success narratives, selection bias, and anecdotal reasoning.                            |

### D4. Numbers, Money, and Mental Accounts

This group covers biases that arise when people attach psychological meaning to numbers, money, budgets, and quantities. Economically equivalent values may feel different depending on their label, source, unit, account, or presentation. The shared error is that people do not treat numbers as neutral magnitudes. They place them into mental containers, compare them against local reference points, mistake nominal amounts for real value, and often fail to separate absolute change, relative change, and practical consequence.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                            | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                               | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                               |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| D025 Mental Accounting                              | People divide money, time, or resources into separate mental accounts, even when the resources are economically interchangeable. The error is treating the same money differently because of its source, label, or intended use. | **Case:** Someone receives a $500 work bonus and spends it on an expensive dinner, saying: “It’s bonus money.” In the same month, they refuse to pay down a high-interest credit card because “that comes from my salary budget.” The money is economically the same, but the mental labels make one amount feel spendable and another feel protected. | Ask: If all this money were in one account, would I make the same decision? What is the best use of the next dollar regardless of its label?                                                       | Central in behavioral economics. Related to framing, sunk cost, budgeting, loss aversion, and narrow bracketing.                                  |
| D026 Money Illusion                                 | People evaluate money in nominal terms rather than real purchasing power. The error is treating a larger number of currency units as a real gain even when inflation or price changes erase it.                                  | **Case:** An employee receives a 4% salary raise during a year when prices rise 8%. They feel better because the salary number is higher. In real terms, their purchasing power has fallen. The nominal increase hides a real decrease.                                                                                                                | Ask: What is the inflation-adjusted value? Can this money buy more, less, or the same amount as before?                                                                                            | Related to framing, reference points, inflation misunderstanding, wage negotiation, and economic perception.                                      |
| D027 Denomination Effect                              | People are less willing to spend large-denomination money than the same value in smaller denominations. The error is that physical or unit form changes spending behavior without changing economic value.                       | **Case:** A person carries one $100 bill and avoids buying snacks because “I don’t want to break it.” Another person carrying five $20 bills spends $20 without much thought. The total value is identical, but the large bill feels like a protected whole.                                                                                           | Ask: Would I spend the same amount if it were divided into smaller units? Am I preserving value, or preserving the feeling of an intact denomination?                                              | Related to mental accounting, payment decoupling, cashless spending, loss aversion, and budgeting behavior.                                       |
| D028 Zero-sum Bias                                  | People assume that one person’s gain must come at another person’s loss, even when value can be created, expanded, or shared. The error is applying a fixed-pie model to non-fixed-pie situations.                               | **Case:** In a workplace, one team receives extra funding for a new project. Another team immediately says: “They are taking resources from us.” But the funding came from a new external partnership and may increase the company’s total opportunities. The gain is interpreted as theft because the situation is mentally framed as a fixed pie.    | Ask: Is the resource actually fixed? Could cooperation, innovation, trade, or scale create additional value? Who really bears the cost?                                                            | Related to scarcity mindset, distributive negotiation, envy, status competition, and political economy reasoning.                                 |
| D029 Conservatism Bias                               | People update beliefs too slowly when new evidence arrives. The error is clinging to an old estimate, forecast, or belief even after the evidence has shifted the probability.                                                   | **Case:** An investor believes a company is stable because it has performed well for years. Three quarterly reports show declining margins, weak demand, and rising debt. The investor says: “It is probably temporary.” Their belief updates, but not enough. The old model remains too heavy.                                                        | Ask: If I saw this evidence without my previous belief, what would I conclude? How much should this evidence change the prior estimate?                                                            | Related to anchoring, belief perseverance, confirmation bias, Bayesian under-updating, and status quo bias.                                       |
| D030 Normalcy Bias                                 | People underestimate the likelihood or severity of a disaster because they assume things will continue as usual. The error is using ordinary past experience as a default model when conditions have changed.                    | **Case:** A town receives a flood evacuation warning. Some residents say: “It has rained before; nothing serious happened.” They delay leaving until roads are blocked. Their everyday experience makes the emergency feel unreal, even though the current warning contains new information.                                                           | Ask: What has changed from ordinary conditions? What would I do if I fully believed the warning? What is the cost of preparing versus the cost of being wrong?                                     | Related to optimism bias, status quo bias, risk perception, information avoidance, and disaster preparedness.                                     |

## E. Fast Action, Decision, Risk, and Time Biases

This class covers biases that appear when people must choose, act, commit, stop, wait, invest, or take risks. The mind often prefers immediate action, clear confidence, simple control, and emotionally manageable trade-offs. The shared error is that decisions are not made from neutral calculation alone. They are shaped by confidence, ownership, loss, regret, sunk cost, default options, ambiguity, time pressure, and the need to preserve a coherent self-image.

### E1. Confidence, Control, and Action Initiation

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                             | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                       | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                           | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                             |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| E001 Overconfidence Bias                            | People overestimate the accuracy of their judgments, the precision of their knowledge, or the probability that their plans will succeed. The error is not confidence itself, but confidence exceeding evidence, feedback history, or uncertainty.                 | **Case:** A startup founder says: “We are 90% sure we can reach 1 million users in six months.” The team has no comparable launch history, the channel costs are unknown, and retention has not been tested. The number expresses commitment more than calibrated probability.                                                        | Ask: What is my track record in similar predictions? What would an 80% confidence interval look like? What evidence would lower my confidence? | Prototype-level decision bias. Related to calibration error, illusion of control, planning fallacy, optimism bias, and Dunning–Kruger effect.                                   |
| E002 Illusion of Control                            | People overestimate their ability to influence outcomes that are partly or largely determined by chance, system constraints, or other actors. The error is mistaking involvement, effort, or choice for control.                                                  | **Case:** A trader manually refreshes charts, changes watchlists, and feels that close monitoring gives control over short-term price movement. In reality, market movement is shaped by countless external forces. The activity increases the feeling of control more than actual control.                                           | Ask: Which parts of this outcome are under my control, partial influence, or no control? What would happen if I did nothing?                   | Related to overconfidence, superstition, action bias, gambling behavior, and agency overattribution.                                                                            |
| E003 Optimism Bias                                    | People believe that desirable outcomes are more likely for them, and undesirable outcomes less likely, than evidence warrants. The error is personalized probability distortion: “bad things happen, but less to me.”                                             | **Case:** A student knows many classmates underestimate exam preparation time. Still, they think: “I work better under pressure; I’ll be fine with three days.” The belief is not based on a reliable record of last-minute success, but on a self-protective optimistic forecast.                                                    | Ask: What happened the last time I made this kind of plan? What would I predict for another person in the same situation?                      | Related to planning fallacy, normalcy bias, self-serving bias, overconfidence, and defensive attribution.                                                                       |
| E004 Hard–Easy Effect                                 | People tend to be overconfident on difficult tasks and underconfident or better calibrated on easier tasks. The error is that perceived difficulty and actual accuracy do not move together in a simple way.                                                      | **Case:** In a hard multiple-choice law exam, a student feels that several answers “must be right” because they recognize some legal terms. After grading, many confident answers are wrong. In easier questions, the same student is more cautious and accurate. Hard tasks create room for false familiarity to imitate competence. | Ask: Is this task objectively hard, ambiguous, or low-feedback? Am I confusing recognition with knowledge?                                     | Related to overconfidence, calibration, fluency, Dunning–Kruger, and illusion of explanatory depth.                                                                             |
| E005 Third-person Effect                            | People believe media, propaganda, advertising, or persuasive messages influence others more than themselves. The error is asymmetric vulnerability: “others are manipulated; I am mostly immune.”                                                                 | **Case:** A person sees a political short video and says: “This kind of content brainwashes ordinary people, but I can see through it.” Yet their own feed, emotional reactions, and repeated exposure also shape what they notice and remember. The belief in immunity blocks self-audit.                                            | Ask: Why would the same persuasive design affect others but not me? What exposure, emotion, repetition, or identity cue might be shaping me?   | Related to bias blind spot, naïve realism, media effects, propaganda analysis, and overconfidence.                                                                              |

### E2. Loss, Ownership, and Reference Points

This group covers biases created by reference points, ownership, and the psychological asymmetry between gains and losses. The mind does not evaluate outcomes by final states alone. It asks: compared with what? Did I gain, lose, keep, give up, sell, waste, or protect something? The shared error is that equivalent outcomes feel different depending on whether they are framed as losses or gains, whether the object is already owned, and whether the decision is judged locally or as part of a repeated risk policy.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                              | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                             | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                               |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| E006 Loss Aversion                                  | Losses usually feel psychologically stronger than equivalent gains. The error is not caring about losses, but letting the pain of losing dominate choices even when the expected value or long-term policy favors taking the risk.                                 | **Case:** A person is offered a coin-flip gamble: lose $100 if tails, win $150 if heads. Mathematically, the expected value is positive. But the person refuses immediately because the imagined pain of losing $100 feels stronger than the pleasure of gaining $150. In repeated opportunities, this refusal may be irrationally conservative.                    | Ask: What is the reference point? Am I rejecting a positive expected-value option because the possible loss feels more vivid than the possible gain? Would I accept this if repeated many times? | Core concept in prospect theory. Related to endowment effect, status quo bias, disposition effect, zero-risk bias, and risk policy.                               |
| E007 Endowment Effect                               | Ownership increases the subjective value of an object. The error is treating “mine” as if it were evidence of higher objective value.                                                                                                                              | **Case:** In an experiment-like market, a student receives a coffee mug for free. A few minutes later, they will not sell it for less than $12. Another student who did not receive the mug is willing to pay only $5 for the same object. Ownership has changed valuation even though the object has not changed.                                                  | Ask: If I did not already own this, how much would I pay to buy it? Am I valuing the object, or valuing the fact that it is mine?                                                                | Closely related to loss aversion because giving up an owned object is coded as a loss. Also related to status quo bias, sentimental value, and sunk cost.         |
| E008 Disposition Effect                               | Investors tend to sell winning assets too early and hold losing assets too long. The error is using realized gains and losses to manage emotion rather than evaluating future prospects.                                                                           | **Case:** An investor buys two stocks. Stock A rises 20%; Stock B falls 20%. The investor sells A to “lock in the win” and keeps B because “it will come back.” But from today forward, the purchase price is irrelevant. The right question is which asset has better future expected return.                                                                      | Ask: If I held cash today, would I buy this asset now? Am I holding it because of future value or because I do not want to realize a loss?                                                       | Specific investment form of loss aversion and reference dependence. Related to sunk cost fallacy, regret avoidance, mental accounting, and status quo bias.       |
| E009 IKEA Effect                                      | People overvalue things they have partly created or assembled themselves. Effort increases attachment and perceived value. The error is treating personal effort as evidence of objective quality.                                                                 | **Case:** Someone builds a simple bookshelf by hand. It is slightly crooked and less durable than a cheaper store-bought shelf, but they insist: “This one is better because I made it.” The labor gives the object personal meaning, but that meaning is not the same as market value, design quality, or usefulness.                                              | Ask: Would someone else value this equally without knowing my effort? Did effort improve quality, or only increase attachment?                                                                   | Related to effort justification, endowment effect, sunk cost, self-serving bias, and creator attachment.                                                          |
| E010 Unit Bias                                        | People tend to consume or choose one default unit, whatever size that unit happens to be. The error is allowing externally set units to define appropriate quantity.                                                                                               | **Case:** A cinema sells popcorn in a huge bucket. A customer did not plan to eat that much, but finishes it because it feels like “one serving.” If the same popcorn had been divided into three smaller bags, they might have stopped earlier. The unit silently sets the norm.                                                                                   | Ask: Who set this unit? Would I choose the same quantity if I had to define the unit myself?                                                                                                     | Related to default effect, portion-size effects, framing, status quo bias, and consumption design.                                                                |
| E011 Less-is-better Effect                          | When evaluated separately, a smaller but more complete or coherent option may be preferred over a larger but slightly flawed option. The error is that separate evaluation can hide absolute value.                                                                | **Case:** One gift box has 24 high-quality chocolates and looks full. Another has 30 chocolates of the same quality, but the box looks slightly underfilled. When judged separately, people may prefer the smaller full-looking box. In direct comparison, the larger box is clearly better. Presentation completeness overrides quantity.                          | Ask: Am I evaluating this option separately or comparatively? Which option has greater total value when placed side by side?                                                                     | Related to distinction bias, contrast effect, framing, evaluability, and consumer judgment. It is not simply “small is better”; it depends on evaluation mode.    |
| E012 Fourfold Pattern                                 | People’s risk attitudes differ across gains/losses and high/low probability. They tend to be risk averse for high-probability gains, risk seeking for low-probability gains, risk seeking for high-probability losses, and risk averse for low-probability losses. | **Case:** A person buys lottery tickets for a tiny chance of a large gain, but also buys insurance against a tiny chance of a large loss. In one case they seek risk; in the other they avoid it. The inconsistency becomes understandable once probability level and gain/loss framing are separated.                                                              | Ask: Is this a gain or a loss? Is the probability high or low? Am I overweighting a small probability or trying to escape a near-certain loss?                                                   | A prospect-theory structure rather than a single bias. Related to loss aversion, probability weighting, pseudocertainty, zero-risk bias, insurance, and gambling. |

### E3. Sunk Cost and Escalation of Commitment

This group covers biases that appear after people have already invested money, time, effort, reputation, or identity into a choice. The rational question should be: from now on, which option has the best expected value? But the mind often asks a different question: how can I avoid feeling that the past investment was wasted? The shared error is letting irreversible past costs control future decisions. A bad project, relationship, investment, argument, or policy becomes harder to abandon precisely because so much has already been spent on it.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                      | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                        | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                   |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| E013 Sunk Cost Fallacy                            | People continue an action because of resources already spent, even though those resources cannot be recovered. The error is allowing past cost to influence a forward-looking decision.                                                    | **Case:** Someone buys an expensive movie ticket. After 30 minutes, the film is clearly terrible and they are exhausted. They think: “I paid for this, so I should finish it.” But the money is gone whether they stay or leave. The real choice is between spending the next 90 minutes badly or doing something better with that time.                                   | Ask: If I had not already paid, would I choose to start now? What future benefit justifies continuing?                                                      | Prototype decision bias. Related to loss aversion, regret avoidance, escalation of commitment, mental accounting, and effort justification.                                                           |
| E014 Escalation of Commitment                       | After choosing a course of action, people commit more resources to it in order to preserve consistency, reputation, identity, or responsibility for the original decision. The error is that self-justification replaces fresh evaluation. | **Case:** A department head approves a costly software migration. Six months later, delays, bugs, and employee resistance are severe. Independent reviewers suggest switching plans. The department head insists: “We just need one more phase.” Because the original choice was theirs, abandoning it now feels like personal failure.                                    | Ask: Would a new decision-maker with no responsibility for the original choice continue this project? What would we do if we were deciding today from zero? | Broader than sunk cost. Sunk cost focuses on spent resources; commitment escalation also includes ego, responsibility, reputation, and public consistency.                                            |
| E015 Effort Justification                             | People value an outcome more highly because they suffered, struggled, or worked hard to obtain it. The error is treating effort as evidence of worth.                                                                                      | **Case:** A student joins an extremely demanding training program. The teaching is mediocre, but the entrance ritual and workload are punishing. After finishing, the student says: “It changed my life; it must be valuable because it was so hard.” Difficulty helps justify the sacrifice. The program may have value, but the suffering itself is being used as proof. | Ask: If this had required little effort, would I still judge it valuable? Did effort create quality, or did it create attachment?                           | Related to cognitive dissonance, IKEA effect, sunk cost, initiation rites, and self-justification.                                                                                                    |
| E016 Processing Difficulty Effect                   | Difficult-to-process information may feel more serious, deep, effortful, or valuable, or may trigger more analytic attention. The error occurs when difficulty is mistaken for profundity, rigor, or truth.                                | **Case:** A reader compares two explanations of the same theory. One is clear and precise; the other is full of dense terminology and awkward syntax. The reader says: “The second one must be more advanced.” But opacity may reflect poor writing, not depth. Difficulty has been converted into prestige.                                                               | Ask: Is this difficult because the idea is complex, or because the explanation is unclear? Can the same content be stated clearly without loss?             | Boundary item. Sometimes disfluency improves attention and memory; sometimes it merely creates false depth. Related to cognitive ease, fluency effects, jargon, and pseudo-profundity.                |
| E017 Generation Effect                                | People remember information better when they generate it themselves rather than passively receive it. The possible error is overvaluing self-generated conclusions because they are more memorable and feel more owned.                    | **Case:** A student solves a statistics problem incorrectly but through their own reasoning. Because they generated the solution, it feels clear and sticks in memory. Later, even after seeing the correct answer, their own mistaken method remains persuasive because it is familiar, self-produced, and easy to retrieve.                                              | Ask: Did I generate this conclusion correctly? Is it memorable because it is right, or because I produced it myself?                                        | Primarily a learning and memory effect, not a bias by itself. Related to self-reference effect, testing effect, effort justification, IKEA effect, and overconfidence in self-generated explanations. |

### E4. Defaults, Status Quo, and Choice Architecture

This table follows the planned E4 sequence: status quo bias, default effect, reactance, reverse psychology, decoy effect, social comparison bias, Abilene paradox, Chesterton’s fence, and the HIPPO problem.

This group covers biases created by the structure of choice itself. People do not merely choose among options; they choose within an environment where one option is already normal, one option is preselected, one option is made to look better by comparison, one opinion comes from a high-status person, or one group preference is falsely assumed. The shared error is mistaking the shape of the choice environment for the independent strength of one’s own preference.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                          | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                              |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| E018 Status Quo Bias                                | People prefer the current state simply because it is current. The error is treating familiarity, inertia, and avoidance of regret as if they were evidence that the existing option is best.                                                   | **Case:** A company has used the same reporting process for years. Everyone complains that it wastes time, but when a new workflow is proposed, managers say: “At least the current system works.” What they mean is not that it works well, but that its costs are familiar and its risks are already normalized.           | Ask: If this were not the current arrangement, would I actively choose it today? What costs have become invisible because they are familiar?               | Related to default effect, loss aversion, endowment effect, system justification, normalcy bias, and omission bias.                                                                                              |
| E019 Default Effect                                 | People disproportionately accept the preselected option. The error is allowing the default setting to function as a recommendation, norm, or path of least resistance.                                                                         | **Case:** A website subscription page has “auto-renew every year” already checked. Many users keep it not because they strongly prefer auto-renewal, but because changing the default requires attention and effort. Later, the company says: “Users chose annual renewal.” The choice was partly engineered by the default. | Ask: Who set the default, and what interest does it serve? Would my choice remain the same if the opposite option were preselected?                        | Related to status quo bias, choice architecture, nudging, unit bias, opt-in / opt-out design, and dark patterns.                                                                                                 |
| E020 Reactance                                        | When people feel their freedom of choice is threatened, they become motivated to resist, reject, or do the opposite. The error is evaluating an option through the need to defend autonomy rather than through its merits.                     | **Case:** A teacher tells students: “You must read this book; anyone who dislikes it is lazy.” Some students become hostile to the book before reading it. Their resistance is partly a reaction to coercion and moral pressure, not only a judgment about the text.                                                         | Ask: Am I rejecting this because the option is bad, or because I feel controlled? What would I think if the same option were offered without pressure?     | Related to autonomy threat, reverse psychology, persuasion resistance, identity defense, and motivated reasoning.                                                                                                |
| E021 Reverse Psychology                               | A strategy that uses reactance by encouraging the opposite of the desired behavior. It is not itself a bias, but it exploits a bias: people’s desire to preserve autonomy.                                                                     | **Case:** A parent says to a teenager: “You probably cannot handle this advanced course anyway.” The teenager enrolls partly to prove the parent wrong. The decision may still be good, but the motivation has been shaped by provocation rather than independent evaluation.                                                | Ask: Am I choosing this because I want it, or because someone framed refusal or acceptance as a test of pride?                                             | More a persuasion tactic than a cognitive bias. Related to reactance, framing, manipulation, status competition, and self-image defense.                                                                         |
| E022 Decoy Effect                                   | A third option changes preferences between two original options by making one look clearly superior in comparison. The error is that preference is constructed by local comparison, not by stable underlying value.                            | **Case:** A subscription service offers Basic for $8 and Premium for $18. Many choose Basic. Then it adds a decoy: Standard for $17 with fewer features than Premium. Now Premium looks like a bargain, and more people choose it. The decoy was not meant to be chosen; it was meant to reshape comparison.                 | Ask: If the decoy option disappeared, would my preference remain the same? Which option best serves my actual needs, independent of dominated comparisons? | Related to contrast effect, anchoring, framing, asymmetric dominance, pricing design, and consumer choice architecture.                                                                                          |
| E023 Social Comparison Bias                         | People evaluate outcomes by relative position rather than absolute value. The error is letting rank, status, or comparison with others override one’s actual goals or welfare.                                                                 | **Case:** A worker can choose between two jobs: one pays $90,000 where peers earn $100,000; the other pays $75,000 where peers earn $65,000. They feel drawn to the second because it offers higher relative status, even though the first gives more income and may better support their life goals.                        | Ask: Do I want this because it improves my life, or because it improves my rank? Would I still choose it if I did not know others’ outcomes?               | Related to envy, status competition, zero-sum bias, relative deprivation, social proof, and identity-based decision-making.                                                                                      |
| E024 Abilene Paradox                               | A group takes an action that no member actually wants because each person mistakenly believes others support it. The error is pluralistic ignorance: private preferences are hidden, so the group acts against the preferences of its members. | **Case:** A team schedules a weekend “voluntary” workshop. Everyone privately wants to rest, but no one objects because each assumes the others are enthusiastic. After the workshop, people discover that nobody wanted it. The problem was not conflict; it was false agreement.                                           | Ask: Have private preferences been collected before group discussion? Does silence mean agreement, or fear of being the dissenter?                         | Different from groupthink. Groupthink involves pressure toward consensus; Abilene paradox involves mistaken belief that consensus already exists. Related to pluralistic ignorance and preference falsification. |

### E5. Ambiguity, Information, and Tool Fixation

This table follows the planned E5 sequence: ambiguity bias, information bias, belief bias, rhyme-as-reason effect, bikeshedding, law of triviality, Delmore effect, law of the instrument, law of the hammer, functional fixedness, and Occam’s Razor misuse.

This group covers biases that look like caution, analysis, or problem-solving, but often function as avoidance. When a decision is uncertain, people may prefer a known but worse risk, collect information that will not change the decision, argue about easy details, use the tool they already know, or select the simplest explanation too early. The shared error is mistaking cognitive manageability for correctness: what is clear, familiar, measurable, discussable, or tool-friendly is treated as what matters.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task    | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                        | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                  | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                              |
| --------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| E025 Ambiguity Bias                                   | People prefer options with known probabilities over options with unknown probabilities, even when the unknown option may have higher expected value. The error is treating uncertainty itself as worse than measurable risk. | **Case:** A graduate chooses a low-growth job with predictable salary over a startup role with unclear upside and unclear risk. The safer job may be reasonable, but the decision becomes biased if “unknown” is automatically treated as “bad,” without estimating learning value, option value, and downside protection.         | Ask: Is this option actually worse, or merely harder to quantify? What information would narrow the ambiguity? What is the downside if I explore it in a limited way? | Related to Ellsberg paradox, risk aversion, status quo bias, uncertainty avoidance, and information bias.                                                                                        |
| E026 Information Bias                                  | People seek additional information even when that information would not change the decision. The error is treating more information as automatically valuable.                                                               | **Case:** A patient has symptoms that require treatment regardless of which of two minor subtypes is present. They request expensive additional testing “just to be sure,” even though the result will not change treatment, timing, or risk management. The test provides psychological comfort, not decision value.              | Ask: What decision will this information change? If the answer is A, what will I do? If the answer is B, what will I do?                                              | Related to analysis paralysis, ambiguity bias, anxiety-driven checking, medical overtesting, and research design.                                                                                |
| E027 Bikeshedding Effect                             | Groups spend disproportionate time on simple, familiar, low-stakes issues while avoiding complex, high-stakes ones. The error is allocating discussion time according to ease of participation rather than importance.       | **Case:** In a meeting about a major data infrastructure migration, the team spends 35 minutes debating the color of dashboard labels and only 5 minutes on data security. Everyone can comment on label colors; fewer people understand security architecture. Easy participation crowds out serious judgment.                    | Ask: Is discussion time proportional to decision importance? Are we talking about this because it matters, or because everyone can easily have an opinion?            | Closely related to the law of triviality. Also related to avoidance, meeting failure, cognitive load, and organizational decision bias.                                                          |
| E028 Delmore Effect                                    | People focus on concrete, near-term, easily solvable minor issues while avoiding larger, abstract, or emotionally difficult decisions. The error is using small solvable tasks to escape large unresolved problems.          | **Case:** A student is unsure whether to change majors. Instead of confronting the decision, they spend hours reorganizing folders, choosing note-taking templates, and comparing pens. These tasks feel productive because they are concrete, but they do not answer the major-choice question.                                   | Ask: Is this small task moving the main decision forward, or giving me relief from it? What larger uncertain issue is being displaced?                                | Boundary item. Related to procrastination, bikeshedding, information bias, ambiguity avoidance, and productive avoidance.                                                                        |
| E029 Law of the Instrument                             | A familiar tool or method shapes how people define problems. The error is forcing the problem into the form that one’s preferred tool can handle.                                                                            | **Case:** A data analyst treats every user complaint as a dashboard problem: “Let’s add metrics and segment the funnel.” But the real issue may be confusing copy, broken trust, poor onboarding, or customer support tone. The tool is useful, but it narrows what counts as a real explanation.                                  | Ask: What would this problem look like if I did not have my favorite tool? What method does the problem require, not what method do I prefer?                         | Related to functional fixedness, Maslow’s hammer, professional deformation, streetlight effect, and reductionism.                                                                                |
| E030 Functional Fixedness                              | People have difficulty seeing alternative uses for objects, tools, concepts, or methods beyond their familiar function. The error is that learned use blocks creative problem-solving.                                       | **Case:** During a workshop, participants need to hold a candle upright on a wall. A box of thumbtacks sits nearby, but many see the box only as a container for tacks, not as a possible platform for the candle. The familiar function hides the solution.                                                                       | Ask: What else can this object, method, or concept do? If I had never seen its usual use, how might I use it?                                                         | Classic problem-solving bias. Related to law of the instrument, Einstellung effect, design fixation, and creativity blocks.                                                                      |

### E6. Time Discounting and Immediacy

This group covers biases caused by the distance between the present self and the future self. People often know what would be better in the long run, but the immediate self gives excessive weight to present comfort, present temptation, present emotion, and present bodily state. The shared error is not simple impatience. It is a failure to represent the future as psychologically real: future rewards feel weak, future costs feel abstract, future temptation is underestimated, and future emotion is predicted from the current state.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                             | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                    | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                        |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| E031 Hyperbolic Discounting                         | People discount future rewards in a non-linear way: the closer reward receives disproportionately high weight. The error is that preferences reverse as the immediate reward approaches.                                          | **Case:** A person prefers receiving $110 in 31 days over $100 in 30 days. But when the same choice becomes “$100 today or $110 tomorrow,” they choose $100 today. The objective delay difference is still one day, but immediacy changes the psychological weight.             | Ask: Would I make the same choice if both options were moved into the future? Can I precommit before the immediate reward becomes tempting?             | Core time-decision bias. Related to present bias, procrastination, addiction, impulsive spending, and self-control design. |
| E032 Present Bias                                   | Present costs and benefits receive excessive weight compared with future costs and benefits. The error is letting the current moment dominate a decision whose real consequences are distributed over time.                       | **Case:** A student knows that starting an essay today would reduce stress next week. But opening a video app gives immediate comfort, while the future deadline feels distant and abstract. The student chooses relief now and transfers the cost to their future self.        | Ask: Am I shifting cost to my future self? What would I choose if the future consequence were happening tonight?                                        | Related to hyperbolic discounting, procrastination, planning fallacy, akrasia, and future-self continuity.                 |
| E033 Restraint Bias                                   | People overestimate their ability to resist future temptation, especially when they are currently calm, satisfied, or distant from the tempting situation. The error is designing plans that depend too much on future willpower. | **Case:** Someone trying to reduce sugar says: “It’s fine to keep desserts at home; I’ll just control myself.” At night, tired and stressed, they eat them. The plan was made in a cool state and underestimated the hot state.                                                 | Ask: Am I relying on willpower in a future high-temptation state? Can I change the environment now so the future choice is easier?                      | Related to empathy gap, projection bias, present bias, addiction, and commitment devices.                                  |
| E034 Projection Bias                                | People project their current preferences, emotions, or bodily states onto the future, underestimating how much their state will change. The error is treating the current self as a stable model of the future self.              | **Case:** A person goes grocery shopping after a large meal and buys very little food, thinking: “I won’t need much this week.” Later, when hungry, the plan no longer fits. Their full state was projected onto future hungry states.                                          | Ask: Is my current state representative of the state in which I will use this decision? How will hunger, fatigue, stress, desire, or mood change later? | Related to empathy gap, present bias, hot-cold cognition, affective forecasting, and planning errors.                      |
| E035 Impact Bias                                    | People overestimate the intensity or duration of their future emotional reactions to events. The error is treating imagined emotion as if it accurately predicts lived emotion.                                                   | **Case:** A candidate thinks: “If I fail this interview, I’ll be devastated for months.” After rejection, they feel bad for several days, then adapt, apply elsewhere, and recover faster than expected. The imagined future emotion was stronger and longer than the real one. | Ask: How long did similar emotional events affect me in the past? What adaptation, distraction, support, or new goals will appear after the event?      | Related to affective forecasting, focusing illusion, durability bias, peak-end rule, and emotional decision-making.        |

## F. Self, Social, Attribution, and Group Biases

This class covers biases that arise when judgment serves self-image, group belonging, moral identity, status, and social explanation. The self is not a neutral observer. It protects coherence, avoids shame, claims credit, shifts blame, and compares itself favorably with others. This section follows the planned F sequence, beginning with self-evaluation and ability judgment.

### F1. Self-evaluation and Ability Judgment

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                        | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                             |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| F001 Self-serving Bias                            | People explain success as caused by their own ability, effort, or virtue, while explaining failure as caused by external circumstances, bad luck, unfairness, or others’ mistakes. The error is asymmetric attribution: success and failure are not interpreted by the same standard. | **Case:** A student receives a high grade and says: “I did well because I understand the material and worked hard.” Later, they receive a low grade and say: “The teacher asked strange questions and graded unfairly.” Either explanation may be partly true, but the pattern is suspicious: good outcomes are owned, bad outcomes are outsourced.    | Ask: Am I using the same explanatory standard for success and failure? What part of the success depended on luck or help? What part of the failure depended on my choices? | Prototype self-protective bias. Related to attribution bias, defensive attribution, optimism bias, illusory superiority, and group-serving bias.                                |
| F002 Egocentric Bias                                | People overestimate their own role, contribution, perspective, or visibility in events involving multiple people. The error is using one’s own experience as the default center of the situation.                                                                                     | **Case:** In a group project, each member privately thinks they did most of the work. One person remembers late nights writing, another remembers organizing meetings, another remembers fixing slides. Because each person has direct access to their own effort but only partial access to others’ effort, everyone’s share feels larger than it is. | Ask: What would the contribution look like from other people’s records? If everyone estimated their share, would the total exceed 100%?                                    | Related to spotlight effect, illusion of transparency, self-serving bias, false consensus effect, and perspective-taking failure.                                               |
| F003 Social Desirability Bias                       | People report beliefs, traits, or behaviors in ways that make them look socially acceptable, moral, competent, or normal. The error is treating self-report as transparent evidence of actual behavior or belief.                                                                     | **Case:** In a workplace survey, almost everyone says they “welcome critical feedback” and “respect different opinions.” But meeting recordings show that many interrupt dissenting colleagues or punish disagreement indirectly. The survey measures not only attitude, but also the desire to appear like the kind of person who welcomes criticism. | Ask: Is this answer measuring reality, or measuring what people want to be seen as? Are there behavioral records, anonymous methods, or indirect measures?                 | Important in survey design and self-assessment. Related to impression management, self-serving bias, implicit bias, moral credentialing, and demand characteristics.            |
| F004 Illusory Superiority                            | People rate themselves as better than average in desirable abilities, traits, or moral qualities more often than statistics allow. The error is that vague standards and selective comparison make self-enhancement easy.                                                             | **Case:** In a class, most students believe they are above average in “independent thinking.” One student thinks it means reading difficult books; another thinks it means disagreeing with teachers; another thinks it means not following trends. Because the criterion is vague, everyone can define it in a way that favors themselves.            | Ask: Better than which reference group? By what observable measure? What would peer ratings or performance records show?                                                   | Already appears in E1 as a confidence bias; here it belongs to self-evaluation. Related to Lake Wobegon effect, overconfidence, self-serving bias, and social comparison.       |
| F005 Pollyanna Principle                          | People tend to remember, interpret, or evaluate information more positively than negatively in some everyday contexts, especially when maintaining self-image or emotional stability. The error occurs when positive filtering hides risk, failure, or necessary correction.          | **Case:** A person reviews their past year and remembers “mostly progress”: a few good conversations, a completed course, several encouraging moments. They barely mention missed deadlines, failed plans, or conflicts they avoided. The positive memory helps preserve motivation, but it also weakens learning if negative feedback disappears.     | Ask: What negative or corrective evidence am I smoothing over? Does this positive interpretation help resilience, or does it prevent adjustment?                           | Related to optimism bias, rosy retrospection, self-serving bias, fading affect bias, and mood-congruent memory. It is not always harmful; positive bias can support resilience. |

### F2. Attribution Biases

This section follows the planned F2 sequence: fundamental attribution error, actor–observer bias, trait ascription bias, group attribution error, ultimate attribution error, and defensive attribution.

Attribution biases appear when people explain behavior. The mind wants causes, but social behavior usually has many causes: personality, situation, role, incentives, information, pressure, luck, and social norms. The shared error in this group is explanatory compression. People turn a complex action into a simple character judgment, treat their own behavior as situational but others’ behavior as dispositional, or use different causal standards for in-groups and out-groups.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                 | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                 | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| F006 Fundamental Attribution Error                | People overemphasize personality, character, or disposition when explaining others’ behavior, while underweighting situational constraints. The error is treating behavior as a transparent expression of “what kind of person they are.”                                                                         | **Case:** A colleague gives a short, cold reply in a meeting. Others say: “He is arrogant and difficult.” They do not know that he had just received bad news from home, was under deadline pressure, and had been asked to defend a decision he did not personally support. The behavior is real, but the character explanation is too quick.                                  | Ask: What situational pressures, roles, incentives, or constraints could produce the same behavior? Would I explain my own behavior this way under similar pressure? | Prototype social attribution bias. Related to actor–observer bias, hostile attribution bias, essentialism, stereotyping, and moral judgment.                                       |
| F007 Actor–Observer Bias                       | People explain their own actions more by situation, but explain others’ similar actions more by traits. The error is asymmetric access: one knows one’s own context and intentions, but sees only others’ outward behavior.                                                                                       | **Case:** I arrive late and say: “Traffic was terrible, and the previous meeting ran over.” Another person arrives late and I think: “She is unreliable.” The same behavior is explained differently because my own circumstances are vivid to me, while hers are invisible.                                                                                                    | Ask: If I were the actor, what situational explanation would I mention? If another person knew my full context, would their judgment change?                         | Related to fundamental attribution error, egocentric bias, self-serving bias, perspective-taking failure, and empathy gap.                                                         |
| F008 Trait Ascription Bias                          | People see their own behavior as flexible and context-dependent, while seeing others’ behavior as more stable and trait-driven. The error is giving oneself complexity while simplifying others.                                                                                                                  | **Case:** A student says: “I am quiet in class because the topic, teacher, room, and classmates affect me.” But they describe another quiet student as “an introverted person.” Their own silence has many reasons; the other person’s silence becomes a fixed trait.                                                                                                           | Ask: Am I granting myself situational complexity while turning others into stable types? What contexts might change the other person’s behavior?                     | Related to actor–observer bias, illusion of asymmetric insight, out-group simplification, and stereotyping.                                                                        |
| F009 Group Attribution Error                      | People infer a group’s traits, intentions, or values from the behavior of a single member, or infer an individual member’s view from a group decision. The error is moving too easily between individual and group levels.                                                                                        | **Case:** One student from a university behaves rudely at an event. Someone says: “Students from that university are arrogant.” A single member’s behavior is converted into a group property.
