In the reverse direction, if a committee makes a decision, people may assume every committee member personally supported it, ignoring compromise, pressure, or voting structure. | Ask: Is this evidence about one person, a subgroup, the whole group, or an institutional decision? What is the sample size and selection process?                    | Related to stereotyping, essentialism, base-rate neglect, ultimate attribution error, and collective responsibility.                                                               |
| F010 Ultimate Attribution Error                   | People apply different attribution rules to in-groups and out-groups. Positive behavior by the in-group is explained by virtue; positive behavior by the out-group is explained away. Negative behavior by the out-group is explained by character; negative behavior by the in-group is excused by circumstance. | **Case:** When my group wins a debate, I say: “We had better arguments.” When the opposing group wins, I say: “The judges were biased.” When my side behaves aggressively, I call it “passion under pressure”; when the other side does the same, I call it “their true nature.” The causal rules change with identity.                                                         | Ask: Am I explaining success and failure symmetrically across groups? Would I accept this explanation if the group identities were reversed?                         | Group-level extension of attribution bias. Related to in-group bias, group-serving bias, stereotyping, prejudice, and naïve realism.                                               |
| F011 Defensive Attribution Hypothesis              | People explain others’ misfortunes in ways that protect their own sense of safety and control. The error is blaming the victim or overemphasizing controllable factors so that the observer can believe the same harm will not happen to them.                                                                    | **Case:** After a person loses money in a sophisticated scam, observers say: “I would never be that gullible.” This makes them feel safer, but it ignores manipulation design, stress, trust, urgency, and social engineering. The attribution protects the observer from vulnerability more than it explains the event.                                                        | Ask: Am I choosing an explanation that makes me feel immune? What ordinary conditions could make a reasonable person vulnerable?                                     | Already appeared in C3 as part of moral order. Here it belongs to attribution. Related to just-world hypothesis, victim blaming, optimism bias, and fundamental attribution error. |

### F3. Group, Identity, and Position Biases

This section follows the established F3 sequence: in-group bias, out-group homogeneity bias, group-serving bias, groupthink, groupshift, bandwagon effect, authority bias, automation bias, reactive devaluation, not-invented-here bias, affinity bias, and implicit bias.

This group covers biases produced by belonging, loyalty, hierarchy, and social position. Human judgment rarely happens from nowhere. People judge as members of groups, organizations, professions, political camps, schools, companies, fandoms, and status systems. The shared error is that evidence is not weighed only by its content. It is also weighed by who says it, which group it helps, whether it threatens identity, whether a superior endorses it, and whether many others already seem to believe it.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                             | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                                         |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| F012 In-group Bias                                 | People favor members of their own group in trust, evaluation, resource allocation, and moral interpretation. The error is giving “us” the benefit of the doubt while demanding stronger proof from “them.”                                                                         | **Case:** In a debate club, a member from one’s own team makes a weak argument and people say: “He was under time pressure.” A rival team member makes the same weak argument and people say: “They don’t understand the topic.” The same behavior receives a charitable explanation inside the group and a harsher explanation outside it. | Ask: If an out-group member did the same thing, would I judge it the same way? What standard would I use if group identity were hidden?                                    | Core group bias. Related to out-group homogeneity bias, group-serving bias, ultimate attribution error, affinity bias, and naïve realism.                                                                                   |
| F013 Out-group Homogeneity Bias                 | People perceive members of out-groups as more similar to one another than members of their own group. The error is preserving complexity for “us” while flattening “them.”                                                                                                         | **Case:** A student says: “People in that major are all the same — practical, boring, and careerist.” But when describing their own major, they mention many subtypes: theoretical students, activists, career-driven students, quiet readers, and social organizers. The out-group becomes a block; the in-group remains a population.     | Ask: What internal differences exist within that group? Do I know enough members to judge variation, or only a few visible examples?                                       | Related to stereotyping, trait ascription bias, in-group bias, essentialism, and prejudice.                                                                                                                                 |
| F014 Group-serving Bias                             | Groups attribute their own successes to internal virtues and their failures to external obstacles, while reversing the pattern for rival groups. The error is self-serving bias scaled up to group identity.                                                                       | **Case:** A sports fan says after a win: “Our team showed character and discipline.” After a loss, the same fan says: “The referee destroyed the game.” When the rival team wins, it is luck; when they lose, it proves they are weak. Group identity controls the causal explanation.                                                      | Ask: Are success and failure being explained symmetrically across groups? What would the explanation look like if team names were removed?                                 | Related to self-serving bias, ultimate attribution error, in-group bias, defensive attribution, and group polarization.                                                                                                     |
| F015 Groupthink                                     | A group suppresses dissent and critical evaluation in order to preserve harmony, loyalty, or confidence in a shared plan. The error is mistaking smooth consensus for sound judgment.                                                                                              | **Case:** A leadership team prepares a risky product launch. Several members privately worry that the market data are weak, but no one challenges the CEO’s confidence because the group culture values unity. The launch fails. The problem was not lack of intelligence; it was a decision environment where doubt became disloyalty.     | Ask: Has anyone been assigned to argue the opposite? Were private objections collected before public discussion? Is disagreement socially safe?                            | Different from Abilene paradox. Groupthink involves pressure toward agreement; Abilene paradox involves false belief that others already agree. Related to conformity, authority bias, HIPPO, and escalation of commitment. |
| F016 Groupshift                                       | Group discussion can shift members toward more extreme or risky positions than their initial individual views. The error is that deliberation becomes amplification rather than correction.                                                                                        | **Case:** Before discussion, several team members mildly support an aggressive marketing campaign. During discussion, each person hears others express similar support, and the group gradually moves toward a much riskier campaign. Nobody began at the extreme, but mutual reinforcement created it.                                     | Ask: What were individual views before discussion? Did the group become more extreme after hearing itself? Are dissenting or moderating views still visible?               | Related to group polarization, bandwagon effect, social proof, echo chambers, and risky shift.                                                                                                                              |
| F017 Bandwagon Effect                               | People adopt beliefs, choices, or behaviors because many others seem to endorse them. The error is treating popularity as evidence of truth, quality, or inevitability.                                                                                                            | **Case:** An investor sees a stock trending on social media and says: “Everyone is buying it; there must be something real here.” They buy without reading financials, risks, or valuation. The crowd becomes a substitute for analysis.                                                                                                    | Ask: What evidence would support this belief if I did not know it was popular? Am I following information, or merely following visible adoption?                           | Related to conformity, social proof, herd behavior, availability, authority bias, and market bubbles.                                                                                                                       |
| F018 Authority Bias                                 | People give excessive weight to statements from authorities, experts, leaders, institutions, or prestigious figures. The error is replacing evidence evaluation with status evaluation.                                                                                            | **Case:** A famous professor comments on a topic outside their expertise. Audiences cite the statement as decisive because of the professor’s reputation. The title is real, but expertise is domain-specific. Authority becomes misleading when it travels beyond its evidential range.                                                    | Ask: Is this authority relevant to this specific question? What evidence supports the claim independent of the speaker’s status?                                           | Related to halo effect, HIPPO problem, prestige bias, expert overreach, and obedience.                                                                                                                                      |
| F019 Automation Bias                               | People overtrust automated systems, algorithms, GPS, AI tools, or decision-support software, especially when the output looks precise or objective. The error is treating machine output as neutral truth rather than as a model with assumptions, data limits, and failure modes. | **Case:** A doctor uses a diagnostic support system that labels a patient low-risk. The patient’s symptoms look unusual, but the doctor hesitates to override the software. Later, the case turns out to be serious. The system was useful, but the output suppressed professional judgment instead of supporting it.                       | Ask: What data and assumptions generated this output? What signs would justify overriding the system? How often does this tool fail, and in what cases?                    | Related to authority bias, algorithm aversion, AI overreliance, confirmation bias, and decision automation.                                                                                                                 |
| F020 Reactive Devaluation                            | People devalue a proposal because it comes from an opponent, rival, disliked group, or distrusted source. The error is evaluating origin before content.                                                                                                                           | **Case:** In a political negotiation, one party proposes a compromise that the other side privately considered reasonable before. Once it is publicly presented by the rival party, members reject it as a trick. The same proposal changes value because of its source.                                                                    | Ask: If this proposal came from my own side or a neutral source, would I evaluate it differently? What are its merits independent of authorship?                           | Related to identity-protective cognition, naïve realism, in-group bias, out-group distrust, and negotiation failure.                                                                                                        |
| F021 Not Invented Here Bias                         | Groups reject external ideas, methods, or technologies because they were developed elsewhere. The error is confusing origin with quality, fit, or legitimacy.                                                                                                                      | **Case:** An engineering team refuses to adopt an open-source tool because “we should build our own.” Months later, their internal version is slower, less secure, and harder to maintain. The external solution was rejected not because it failed the requirements, but because it threatened group pride and ownership.                  | Ask: Are we rejecting this because it is unsuitable, or because it is external? Would we value it differently if we had created it?                                        | Related to in-group bias, endowment effect, status competition, organizational identity, and tool fixation.                                                                                                                 |
| F022 Affinity Bias                                    | People favor those who resemble them in background, school, language, interests, personality, culture, or career path. The error is mistaking comfort and familiarity for merit or trustworthiness.                                                                                | **Case:** A hiring manager interviews two candidates. One attended the same university and enjoys the same sport; conversation feels easy. The manager later says this candidate is a “better culture fit,” although the other candidate has stronger evidence of job performance. Similarity becomes a proxy for quality.                  | Ask: Which parts of my evaluation come from job-relevant evidence, and which come from interpersonal comfort? Could structured criteria or blind review change the result? | Related to in-group bias, halo effect, implicit bias, social network homophily, and hiring discrimination.                                                                                                                  |
| F023 Implicit Bias                                  | Automatic associations influence perception, evaluation, or behavior without deliberate endorsement. The error is assuming that sincere egalitarian beliefs fully prevent biased behavior.                                                                                         | **Case:** A manager sincerely believes they are fair. In performance reviews, they describe assertive men as “leadership material” but assertive women as “difficult.” They may not consciously endorse sexism, but automatic associations and workplace stereotypes shape interpretation.                                                  | Ask: Do behavior patterns match stated values? Are evaluations structured, evidence-based, and checked for group differences?                                              | Related to implicit stereotype, affinity bias, prejudice, social desirability bias, and stereotype threat. It should not be reduced to conscious hatred.                                                                    |

### F4. Prejudice and Social-Structure Boundary

This group covers items at the boundary between cognitive bias, social psychology, moral judgment, and structural inequality. Prejudice cannot be reduced to a small individual reasoning error. It involves history, institutions, power, norms, material interests, and group identity. Still, cognitive mechanisms matter: people categorize, associate, simplify, moralize, misrecognize, and remember unevenly. The shared error is treating socially shaped judgments as if they were neutral perception, common sense, or natural reaction.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                    | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                 | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                                            |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| F024 Prejudice                                        | A negative or evaluative attitude toward a person based on group membership rather than sufficient individual evidence. The error is not only inaccurate classification, but morally loaded pre-judgment: the person is interpreted through a social category before being encountered as an individual. | **Case:** A landlord sees an applicant’s accent, surname, or region of origin and immediately thinks: “This tenant may be troublesome.” The applicant has stable income, good references, and a clean rental history, but group-based suspicion enters before the evidence is evaluated.                                                      | Ask: What individual evidence supports this judgment? Would I make the same inference if the group marker were removed? What social story is this category carrying?                 | Not fully reducible to cognitive bias. Related to stereotyping, implicit bias, discrimination, essentialism, in-group bias, and institutional power.                                                                           |
| F025 Implicit Associations                            | Automatic mental associations connect categories with traits, roles, emotions, or evaluations. The error is assuming that only explicit belief matters, while fast association silently shapes judgment and behavior.                                                                                    | **Case:** In a hiring review, evaluators repeatedly associate “technical leadership” with male-coded examples and “communication support” with female-coded examples. No one says women cannot lead technically, but the role associations affect who is imagined as a natural fit.                                                           | Ask: What associations appear before conscious reasoning begins? Do evaluation patterns differ by name, gender, race, accent, school, or background when qualifications are similar? | Related to implicit stereotype, implicit bias, affinity bias, stereotype threat, and social desirability bias.                                                                                                                 |
| F026 Cross-race Effect / Own-race Bias           | People are often less accurate at recognizing faces from racial or ethnic groups with which they have less perceptual experience. The error becomes serious when recognition confidence is treated as reliability, especially in eyewitness identification.                                              | **Case:** A witness briefly sees a suspect at night and later identifies someone from a lineup of another racial group. The witness feels confident, but the viewing conditions were poor and cross-race recognition is more error-prone. If the court treats confidence as accuracy, a mistaken identification may become powerful evidence. | Ask: What were the viewing conditions, exposure time, stress level, lineup procedure, and cross-race recognition risk? Is confidence being confused with accuracy?                   | Important in legal contexts. Related to eyewitness error, memory reconstruction, source confusion, stereotyping, and forensic safeguards.                                                                                      |
| F027 Puritanical Bias                               | People judge pleasure, sexuality, bodily expression, leisure, or moral impurity more harshly than actual harm warrants. The error is replacing harm-based judgment with purity-based condemnation.                                                                                                       | **Case:** An adult chooses an unconventional lifestyle that harms no one and involves consent. Observers respond with disgust and say: “People like that are corrupting society.” The moral reaction is driven less by demonstrable harm than by purity, discomfort, and symbolic threat.                                                     | Ask: What concrete harm is being identified? Is the judgment about injury, coercion, and rights, or about disgust, purity, and social conformity?                                    | Boundary item from moral psychology. Related to moralization, disgust sensitivity, authoritarian norms, stigma, and prejudice.                                                                                                 |

## G. Memory Encoding, Retrieval, and Reconstruction Biases

This class covers biases created by memory. Memory is not a recording device. It encodes selectively, stores fragments, reconstructs scenes, fills gaps, loses source tags, and updates the past with later information. The shared error is treating remembered experience as direct access to what happened. In reality, memory is evidence, but it is also a product of attention, emotion, suggestion, repetition, interpretation, and later storytelling.

### G1. Source, Misattribution, and False Memory

This section follows the planned G1 sequence: false memory, memory misattribution, source confusion, cryptomnesia, suggestibility, misinformation effect, continued influence effect, and confabulation.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                        | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                          |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| G001 False Memory                                   | People may remember events, details, or experiences that did not occur, or remember real events in seriously distorted form. The error is treating vividness, confidence, or emotional force as proof of accuracy.   | **Case:** A person is certain that they once got lost in a shopping mall as a child. They can describe the lights, the panic, and the adult who helped them. Later, family records and relatives suggest that this event likely never happened; it may have formed from repeated family stories about a different child, imagination, and later reconstruction. The memory feels lived, but feeling lived is not the same as being historically accurate. | Ask: Is there contemporaneous evidence, independent confirmation, or only later recollection? Could this memory have been built from stories, photos, suggestions, or repeated imagination? | Prototype memory bias. Related to suggestibility, misinformation effect, source confusion, confabulation, and eyewitness error.                                                              |
| G002 Misattribution of Memory                        | A person remembers information, but misattributes its source, time, context, or authorship. The error is that content survives while the tag explaining where it came from becomes wrong or missing.                 | **Case:** Someone says in a discussion: “I read a research article showing this.” Later, they realize the claim came from a podcast summary, not the paper itself. The idea was remembered, but its evidential status changed because the source was upgraded in memory.                                                                                                                                                                                  | Ask: Where exactly did I learn this? Did I see the original evidence, or a retelling, comment, screenshot, or summary?                                                                      | Related to source confusion, cryptomnesia, illusory truth effect, continued influence effect, and misinformation spread.                                                                     |
| G003 Source Confusion / Source-monitoring Error       | People confuse where a memory came from: direct perception, imagination, rumor, media, dream, inference, or another person’s report. The error is failing to distinguish memory content from memory origin.          | **Case:** A student remembers that “the professor said this would be on the exam.” Later, it turns out a classmate had guessed it in a group chat. The student remembered the content accurately but attached it to a more authoritative source.                                                                                                                                                                                                          | Ask: Did I witness this, infer it, imagine it, read it, or hear it from someone else? What source tag is attached to the memory, and how reliable is that tag?                              | A more specific form of memory misattribution. Related to rumor, eyewitness testimony, misinformation effect, and cryptomnesia.                                                              |
| G004 Cryptomnesia                                     | A person recalls an idea, phrase, melody, or solution but experiences it as original because the memory’s source has been lost. The error is unintentional plagiarism through source failure.                        | **Case:** A writer drafts a striking sentence and believes it is their own. Months later, they discover an almost identical sentence in a book they annotated last year. They did not intentionally copy it; the wording was retained while the memory of reading it disappeared.                                                                                                                                                                         | Ask: Have I encountered this idea or phrase before? Do my notes, highlights, browser history, or reading records show a source?                                                             | Not the same as deliberate plagiarism. Related to source confusion, memory misattribution, creativity, and familiarity without recollection.                                                 |
| G005 Suggestibility                                    | Memory can be altered by leading questions, social pressure, authority, repeated prompts, or implied expectations. The error is that people may incorporate external suggestions into their own memory.              | **Case:** After a minor classroom incident, a teacher asks a child: “When he pushed you, did you feel scared?” The child had not originally said they were pushed. After repeated questioning, the child begins to remember a push. The question did not merely retrieve memory; it helped shape it.                                                                                                                                                      | Ask: Were the questions neutral? Was the person exposed to repeated prompts, authority pressure, or expected answers before recalling?                                                      | Related to misinformation effect, false memory, demand characteristics, eyewitness testimony, and child interviews.                                                                          |
| G006 Misinformation Effect                        | Information introduced after an event can distort memory of the original event. The error is that later descriptions, questions, or reports become blended with the event itself.                                    | **Case:** Witnesses see a video of two cars making contact. Later, one group is asked: “How fast were the cars going when they smashed into each other?” Another group is asked: “How fast were they going when they contacted each other?” The “smashed” wording can increase later estimates of speed and even affect memory of broken glass.                                                                                                           | Ask: What did I actually perceive at the time, and what did I learn afterward? Has later wording changed the memory?                                                                        | Classic memory illusion. Related to suggestibility, source confusion, false memory, eyewitness error, and post-event information.                                                            |
| G007 Continued Influence Effect                   | Corrected misinformation continues to shape belief, memory, and reasoning after people have learned it is false. The error is that the original claim remains available as a causal story or familiar frame.         | **Case:** Initial reports say a warehouse fire was caused by illegal chemicals. Later, investigators state that no chemicals were involved. Months later, people still describe the incident as “that chemical warehouse scandal.” The correction was known, but the original explanation had already organized the story.                                                                                                                                | Ask: What false explanation did I first hear? What correct causal explanation replaced it? Am I still using the old story structure?                                                        | Also appears in belief filtering. Here the emphasis is memory and causal reconstruction. Related to illusory truth effect, misinformation effect, source confusion, and belief perseverance. |
| G008 Confabulation                                   | People unconsciously fill gaps in memory or explanation with invented but subjectively sincere details. The error is not deliberate lying, but producing a coherent account where memory or awareness is incomplete. | **Case:** Someone forgets why they entered a room. When asked, they quickly say: “I came here to check the window,” because that sounds plausible. A moment later, they find the book they meant to pick up. The first explanation was not intentional deception; it was a coherent filler created under uncertainty.                                                                                                                                    | Ask: Do I actually remember this, or am I constructing a plausible explanation? What evidence separates recollection from reconstruction?                                                   | Related to false memory, narrative coherence, source confusion, anosognosia in clinical contexts, and everyday rationalization.                                                              |

### G2. Association, Labeling, and Semantic Memory Illusions

This group covers memory errors created by association, labels, semantic expectation, and conceptual fit. The mind does not store isolated items. It stores relations, categories, scripts, labels, meanings, and gist. That structure makes memory efficient, but it also makes memory vulnerable: a word that was only implied may later feel presented; a label may reshape an ambiguous image; a familiar script may hide a wrong term; an implicit association may be remembered as an observed fact.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task         | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                          |
| -------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| G009 Associative Memory Illusion                          | Related concepts activate one another in memory. The error occurs when internally activated information is later confused with externally presented information.                                                                                     | **Case:** A participant studies the words “bed, rest, tired, dream, pillow, blanket.” Later, they confidently remember seeing the word “sleep,” although it never appeared. The word was strongly activated by the list’s meaning, so it feels like part of the original input.                            | Ask: Was this item actually presented, or was it only strongly implied by the theme? Can I identify its exact position or source?                                      | Core mechanism behind DRM false memories. Related to semantic gist, spreading activation, false recognition, and source monitoring.                                                                          |
| G010 DRM Paradigm / Deese–Roediger–McDermott Paradigm | A classic experimental paradigm in which lists of semantically related words produce false recall or false recognition of a non-presented critical lure. The error shows how memory stores gist and association, not only surface details.           | **Case:** After studying “thread, pin, eye, sewing, sharp, point,” a person later says they saw “needle.” They may even report remembering the word clearly. The list produced a semantic center, and the unshown center is falsely remembered as shown.                                                   | Ask: Is this memory based on verbatim trace or gist? Can I distinguish “I remember seeing it” from “it fits the list”?                                                 | A laboratory paradigm, not a separate everyday bias in the narrow sense. It is a tool for studying associative false memory. Related to false memory, source confusion, gist memory, and monitoring failure. |
| G011 Moses Illusion                                         | People fail to notice a distortion in a question when the wrong term is semantically related to the correct one and fits the broader script. The error is answering the intended question rather than checking the actual wording.                   | **Case:** Asked “How many animals of each kind did Moses take on the Ark?” many people answer “two,” missing that the biblical Ark figure is Noah, not Moses. The surrounding script activates the Ark story, and Moses is close enough within the religious schema that the mismatch slips through.       | Ask: Did I answer the question actually asked, or the question that the script made me expect? Which term should be checked word by word?                              | A semantic illusion and memory / comprehension boundary case. Related to partial matching, semantic similarity, source monitoring, cognitive ease, and question-answering heuristics.                        |
| G012 Effects of Labelling                                   | Labels guide perception, encoding, and later memory of ambiguous material. The error occurs when the label becomes part of what is remembered, even if the original stimulus was ambiguous.                                                          | **Case:** Two people see the same ambiguous drawing. One is told it is “a pair of eyeglasses”; the other is told it is “a dumbbell.” Later, each person redraws it in a way that resembles the label they received. The label did not merely name the object; it shaped memory of the object.              | Ask: What label was attached before encoding? Am I remembering the stimulus itself, or the interpretation supplied by the label?                                       | Classic memory-encoding effect. Related to framing, expectation bias, top-down perception, schema-based memory, and misinformation effect.                                                                   |
| G013 Orientation Illusions in Memory                          | People may remember the orientation, rotation, or spatial direction of an object incorrectly, often normalizing it toward familiar canonical forms. The error is that memory stores a reconstructed object, not a perfect spatial record.            | **Case:** A person sees a comet image tilted at an unusual angle. Later, when asked to recognize it, they choose a version closer to a familiar or expected orientation. The memory has been pulled toward a more standard visual arrangement.                                                             | Ask: Did I encode the exact orientation, or only the object identity? Could familiarity with the usual orientation be reconstructing the image?                        | More specific visual-memory illusion. Related to schema normalization, object recognition, spatial memory, and reconstruction.                                                                               |

### G3. Memory Selection, Learning, and Retrieval

This section follows the planned G3 sequence: spacing effect, testing effect, levels-of-processing effect, picture superiority effect, self-reference effect, absent-mindedness, next-in-line effect, tip-of-the-tongue phenomenon, Google effect, memory inhibition, and part-list cueing.

This group covers how memory is strengthened, weakened, selected, or blocked during learning and retrieval. Several entries here are not “biases” in the negative sense. They are memory mechanisms: spacing, testing, deep processing, images, and self-reference can genuinely improve memory. But they also show that memory is not neutral storage. What gets remembered depends on timing, attention, retrieval practice, personal relevance, cue structure, and competition among memories.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                 | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                  | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                                                 |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| G014 Spacing Effect                                 | Information is usually remembered better when learning is distributed over time rather than massed into one session. The possible error is mistaking short-term fluency after cramming for durable learning.                          | **Case:** A student reviews 100 legal terms for 4 hours the night before a quiz and feels fluent. A week later, most terms are gone. Another student studies the same terms in 20-minute sessions across 8 days and remembers more. The first student had temporary familiarity; the second built retrievable memory.            | Ask: Am I optimizing for tomorrow’s feeling of fluency, or next month’s retrieval? Can I schedule repeated exposures with increasing gaps? | A learning effect, not a bias by itself. Related to testing effect, forgetting curve, distributed practice, and desirable difficulty.                                                                                               |
| G015 Testing Effect / Retrieval Practice Effect     | Actively retrieving information strengthens later memory more than passive rereading. The error is believing that recognition during review equals ability to recall.                                                                 | **Case:** Two students prepare for an exam. One rereads highlighted notes and thinks: “I know this.” The other closes the book and tries to write the rule from memory, making several mistakes. The second feels worse during study, but performs better later because retrieval practice exposed and strengthened weak points. | Ask: Can I produce the answer without looking? Have I tested recall, or only recognized familiar material?                                 | A debiasing and learning tool. Related to generation effect, spacing effect, metacognition, and illusion of competence.                                                                                                             |
| G016 Levels-of-processing Effect                  | Deeper semantic processing usually produces better memory than shallow perceptual processing. The error occurs when people confuse surface exposure with meaningful encoding.                                                         | **Case:** A student copies a paragraph about negligence 5 times and remembers little. Another student asks: “What problem is this rule solving? How would it apply to a supermarket slip-and-fall case?” The second student processes meaning, not just words, so later retrieval is stronger.                                   | Ask: Did I process the sound, shape, and wording, or did I process meaning, relation, and use?                                             | A memory principle rather than a bias. Related to elaboration, self-reference effect, generation effect, and deep learning.                                                                                                         |
| G017 Picture Superiority Effect                   | Pictures are often remembered better than words because they support dual encoding, perceptual distinctiveness, and richer retrieval cues. The error appears when image memorability is mistaken for importance or truth.             | **Case:** In a public health presentation, one dramatic image of a damaged lung remains in everyone’s memory, while the actual risk table is forgotten. The image helps attention and recall, but if it dominates the statistical evidence, memory becomes visually biased.                                                      | Ask: Am I remembering this because it was visually strong, or because it was evidentially central? What did the accompanying data say?     | A robust memory effect. Related to salience bias, vividness effect, identifiable victim effect, and visual persuasion.                                                                                                              |
| G018 Self-reference Effect                        | Information is often remembered better when connected to the self: one’s goals, identity, experience, or personal examples. The error is that self-relevance can make information feel more important or universally true than it is. | **Case:** A student learning negotiation theory remembers the concept of BATNA deeply after connecting it to their own rental negotiation. Later, they overapply the same example to every negotiation, assuming all conflicts work like landlord–tenant bargaining. Self-reference helped memory, but narrowed transfer.        | Ask: Is this important generally, or mainly memorable because it connects to me? What cases differ from my personal example?               | Useful learning mechanism. Related to egocentric bias, availability, autobiographical memory, and identity-based reasoning.                                                                                                         |
| G019 Absent-mindedness                                | Memory failure occurs because attention was insufficient during encoding or retrieval. The error is thinking one has “forgotten” something that was never properly encoded.                                                           | **Case:** Someone puts their keys on a shelf while answering a message. Ten minutes later, they cannot find the keys and says: “My memory is terrible.” The problem is not storage failure; attention was elsewhere when the action happened, so the memory trace was weak.                                                      | Ask: Did I actually attend to this when it happened? Can I create deliberate placement routines or external cues?                          | Often a failure of attention rather than memory storage. Related to divided attention, prospective memory, cue-dependent forgetting, and everyday slips.                                                                            |
| G020 Next-in-line Effect                          | People remember less of what immediately precedes their own turn because attention is occupied by preparing their response. The error is believing one listened while actually rehearsing.                                            | **Case:** In a seminar, each student must give a short comment. The student who is next in line is so busy preparing their own sentence that they barely encode the previous speaker’s point. Later they say: “I don’t remember what she said.” The listening slot was consumed by self-preparation.                            | Ask: Was I listening, or rehearsing my own turn? Can I delay response preparation until after encoding the previous point?                 | Related to divided attention, social anxiety, working-memory load, and conversational listening failure.                                                                                                                            |
| G021 Tip-of-the-tongue Phenomenon                     | A person temporarily cannot retrieve a known word, name, or concept, while feeling that retrieval is close. The error is overtrusting the feeling of near-retrieval as evidence that the exact memory is available.                   | **Case:** Someone tries to remember an actor’s name. They say: “It starts with M; I can almost see it.” Several wrong names come to mind, all similar in sound or rhythm. The target memory exists, but competing partial cues block exact retrieval.                                                                            | Ask: Am I stuck in repeated failed cues? Would changing context, waiting, or retrieving related but different cues help?                   | A retrieval phenomenon, not a reasoning bias. Related to blocking, cue-dependent retrieval, interference, and metacognitive feeling-of-knowing.                                                                                     |
| G022 Google Effect / Digital Amnesia                | People remember less information itself when they expect it will be externally available, but may remember where or how to find it. The error appears when access is mistaken for knowledge.                                          | **Case:** A student bookmarks dozens of articles and says: “I have this topic covered.” In discussion, they cannot explain the arguments because they remember the folder structure, not the content. Searchability created an illusion of possession.                                                                           | Ask: Do I know the content, or only where to retrieve it? Could I explain the main point without opening the link?                         | Boundary item. External memory is often rational and useful; the bias appears when retrieval access is confused with internal understanding. Related to transactive memory, digital note-taking, and illusion of explanatory depth. |
| G023 Memory Inhibition / Retrieval-induced Forgetting | Retrieving some memories can suppress or weaken access to related competing memories. The error is assuming that practicing part of a category improves recall for the whole category.                                                | **Case:** A student studies European philosophers and repeatedly quizzes themselves on Kant, Hegel, and Nietzsche. Later, they have more trouble recalling other related names they studied less, such as Fichte or Schelling. Repeated retrieval strengthened some items while making competitors harder to access.             | Ask: Am I repeatedly retrieving only a narrow subset? What related items are being crowded out?                                            | More a retrieval mechanism than a bias. Related to interference, part-list cueing, selective retrieval, and forgetting through competition.                                                                                         |
| G024 Part-list Cueing Effect                      | Providing some items from a learned list can impair recall of the remaining items. The error is assuming that more cues always help memory.                                                                                           | **Case:** A teacher gives students several terms from a chapter to help them remember the rest. Unexpectedly, students recall fewer unlisted terms because the provided items dominate retrieval routes and block access to other items. Partial cues guide memory, but also constrain it.                                       | Ask: Are these cues opening retrieval paths, or locking me into one path? Can I recall freely before seeing partial cues?                  | Related to retrieval-induced forgetting, interference, cue-dependent memory, and exam prompt design.                                                                                                                                |

### G4. Sequence Position and Material Structure

This section follows the planned G4 sequence: serial position effect, primacy effect, recency effect, serial recall effect, list-length effect, modality effect, and suffix effect. The plan also notes that primacy, recency, and serial position should be treated as a linked cluster rather than as unrelated items.

This group covers memory distortions caused by the structure of the material itself. Items do not enter memory with equal probability. Their position in a sequence, distance from the beginning or end, list length, presentation channel, and post-list interference all affect recall. The shared error is assuming that what is remembered best is what mattered most. Often it is simply what occupied a privileged structural position.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                    | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                      |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| G025 Serial Position Effect                       | Items at the beginning and end of a sequence are usually remembered better than items in the middle. The error is treating high recall as evidence of importance rather than position.                                                                                                             | **Case:** After a 40-minute lecture, students remember the opening story and the final summary vividly, but forget the technical distinction explained in the middle. Later they say: “The lecture was mainly about those two points.” The remembered structure has replaced the actual content structure. | Ask: What information appeared in the middle? Am I reconstructing importance from recall strength? Can I review the full sequence rather than only the memorable edges? | Umbrella effect covering primacy and recency. Related to attention, rehearsal, working memory, presentation design, and memory selection.                |
| G026 Primacy Effect                                 | Early information receives privileged encoding and often frames later information. The error is allowing the first items, first impression, or first explanation to set the interpretive frame for everything that follows.                                                                        | **Case:** In a hiring interview, the candidate gives a confident opening answer. The interviewer forms the impression that the candidate is competent, then interprets later vague answers more generously. The first response did not merely get remembered; it shaped the meaning of later responses.    | Ask: Did the first information frame later evidence? If the same evidence appeared in a different order, would I evaluate it differently?                               | Part of serial position effect. Related to anchoring, halo effect, first-impression bias, confirmation bias, and order effects.                          |
| G027 Recency Effect                                 | Recent information is often remembered more easily and can dominate judgment, especially when evaluation happens immediately after exposure. The error is allowing the last item to stand for the whole sequence.                                                                                  | **Case:** A student performs well for most of the semester but gives a weak final presentation. The teacher’s overall impression becomes colder because the last performance is vivid and fresh. The recent event is relevant, but it may not represent the full pattern.                                  | Ask: Does the most recent evidence represent the long-term record? What would the judgment be if earlier evidence were equally accessible?                              | Part of serial position effect. Related to recency illusion, availability, peak-end rule, outcome bias, and performance evaluation.                      |
| G028 Serial Recall Effect                           | People’s recall is shaped by original order, neighboring items, and sequence structure. The error appears when memory of an ordered set is treated as complete, even though order reconstruction is fragile.                                                                                       | **Case:** A manager gives a team six instructions in one spoken sequence. Later, employees remember the first two and last one, but mix the order of the middle steps. The task fails not because people ignored instructions, but because the sequence exceeded reliable serial recall.                   | Ask: Does this task depend on exact order? Should the sequence be externalized into a checklist, written protocol, or visual workflow?                                  | Memory-task phenomenon rather than a broad bias. Related to working memory, list-length effect, chunking, procedural memory, and serial position effect. |
| G029 List-length Effect                             | As a list becomes longer, recall for individual items generally becomes harder because attention, rehearsal, and retrieval must be distributed across more material. The error is assuming that adding more items to a list only adds content, not forgetting.                                     | **Case:** A student writes a daily to-do list with 23 tasks. By afternoon, they remember urgent tasks, a few easy tasks, and the final item they wrote, but forget several important middle items. The long list created an illusion of control while overloading retrieval.                               | Ask: Is the list too long to function as memory? Can it be grouped, prioritized, scheduled, or moved into an external system?                                           | More a memory-load effect than a bias. Related to cognitive load, magic number limits, part-list cueing, attention, and task management.                 |
| G030 Modality Effect                                  | The channel of presentation affects memory. Auditory, visual, spoken, written, and multimodal materials leave different traces, and auditory presentation often supports stronger short-term recall of final items. The error is assuming that content is remembered equally regardless of medium. | **Case:** In a meeting, the final instruction is spoken aloud after a written agenda has already been shown. People remember the spoken final instruction more than the middle written items. The channel and timing made it more available, not necessarily more important.                               | Ask: Which channel carried this information? Was it heard, seen, written, acted out, or repeated? Should important information be reinforced across channels?           | Learning-design issue as much as memory effect. Related to picture superiority, recency effect, suffix effect, dual coding, and multimedia learning.     |
| G031 Suffix Effect                                    | An irrelevant item added after an auditory list can reduce recall of the final list items. The error is assuming that post-list noise or extra comments do not affect memory.                                                                                                                      | **Case:** A trainer reads five safety steps aloud, then immediately says: “Okay, anyway, let’s move on.” Trainees later recall the last safety step poorly. The irrelevant ending sound disrupted the auditory trace of the final item.                                                                    | Ask: Was the end of the list followed by irrelevant speech, noise, or distraction? Can the important final item be repeated, written, or separated from interference?   | Experimental memory effect. Related to modality effect, recency effect, auditory sensory memory, interference, and presentation design.                  |

### G5. Retrospection, Experience, and Autobiographical Memory

This group covers how people remember lived experience after the fact. The past is not merely retrieved; it is edited into a story. Outcomes make earlier events seem more predictable, endings reshape whole experiences, negative feelings fade unevenly, dates move closer or farther away, and present identity rewrites past attitudes. The shared error is treating autobiographical memory as a stable archive, when it often functions as a narrative system for explanation, self-continuity, and emotional regulation.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                      | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                         | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                          |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| G032 Hindsight Bias                                 | After knowing the outcome, people overestimate how predictable it was. The error is that outcome knowledge rewrites uncertainty: what was one possibility among several now feels obvious.                                                 | **Case:** A startup launches a product and fails. After failure, people say: “It was obvious. The market was too crowded and the retention data were weak.” But before launch, the same data also contained positive user interviews, strong early engagement, and investor interest. The final outcome makes the warning signs look clearer than they were. | Ask: What did I predict before the result was known? What alternatives seemed plausible at the time? Are timestamped notes available?                                        | Related to outcome bias, narrative fallacy, inevitability illusion, overconfidence, and historical explanation.                                                              |
| G033 Outcome Bias                                     | People evaluate the quality of a decision by its result rather than by the evidence, reasoning, and probabilities available when the decision was made. The error is confusing decision quality with outcome quality.                      | **Case:** A doctor chooses a treatment with an 80% success rate and low expected harm. The patient unfortunately falls into the 20% failure group. The family says: “The doctor made the wrong decision.” The result was bad, but the decision may still have been reasonable given the information at the time.                                             | Ask: If the outcome were hidden, would the decision process look sound? What probability distribution did the decision-maker face beforehand?                                | Closely related to hindsight bias, but not identical. Hindsight bias says “I knew it would happen”; outcome bias says “because it happened, the decision was bad or good.”   |
| G034 Rosy Retrospection                              | People remember past experiences more positively than they actually felt at the time. The error is that later memory smooths discomfort, boredom, conflict, and inconvenience.                                                             | **Case:** A person remembers a trip as “one of the best weeks of my life.” Their diary from the trip says otherwise: missed trains, exhaustion, arguments, stomach pain, and repeated complaints about heat. The later memory keeps the sunset, the photos, and the final feeling of meaning, while much of the real-time discomfort disappears.             | Ask: What did I record or feel at the time? Am I remembering the experience, or the story I now tell about it?                                                               | Related to nostalgia, fading affect bias, peak-end rule, self-continuity, and autobiographical storytelling.                                                                 |
| G035 Fading Affect Bias                             | Emotional intensity attached to negative memories often fades faster than emotional intensity attached to positive memories. The error occurs when changed emotion is mistaken for changed facts or changed risk.                          | **Case:** Someone leaves a stressful job and initially writes: “I was constantly anxious and could not sleep.” A year later, they say: “It wasn’t that bad; maybe I overreacted.” The pain has faded, which may be healthy, but the workplace conditions may not have changed. Emotional recovery can distort practical evaluation.                          | Ask: Did the facts change, or only my emotional reaction to them? What did I document when the experience was fresh?                                                         | Often adaptive. Related to rosy retrospection, emotion regulation, autobiographical memory, trauma memory, and optimism bias.                                                |
| G036 Peak-end Rule                                  | Retrospective evaluation of an experience is heavily influenced by its most intense moment and its ending, rather than by the average of the whole experience. The error is letting the remembered story replace the experienced duration. | **Case:** A customer waits 50 minutes for service, feels frustrated most of the time, but the staff member at the end apologizes sincerely, solves the problem, and gives a small compensation. Later the customer says: “The service was actually pretty good.” The ending softened the memory of the whole experience.                                     | Ask: What was the average experience like, not only the peak and ending? How much time was spent in each state?                                                              | Central to the distinction between experiencing self and remembering self. Related to duration neglect, recency effect, affective forecasting, and satisfaction measurement. |
| G037 Duration Neglect                               | People’s retrospective evaluation often underweights how long an experience lasted. The error is failing to integrate duration into the total value or cost of an experience.                                                              | **Case:** A patient undergoes two uncomfortable procedures. Procedure A is shorter but ends at the most painful moment. Procedure B lasts longer, but the pain decreases near the end. The patient may remember B as less bad, even though it contained more total discomfort. The ending overrides duration.                                                | Ask: Am I evaluating total experience, or only the shape of the ending? What happens if I calculate duration × intensity?                                                    | Usually discussed together with peak-end rule. Related to remembered utility, pain memory, experience sampling, and recency effect.                                          |
| G038 Telescoping Effect                               | People misdate events, remembering them as more recent or more distant than they really are. The error is that subjective time compresses or stretches autobiographical chronology.                                                        | **Case:** Someone says: “I changed my study method last semester,” but calendar records show it happened two years ago. Because the method still feels part of the current self, the memory moves closer. In other cases, an embarrassing event may feel much older because the person wants distance from it.                                               | Ask: What timestamp, message, file, photo, or calendar entry fixes the date? Am I moving the event closer because it still matters, or farther because it no longer fits me? | Related to autobiographical memory, recency illusion, self-narrative, frequency estimation, and source monitoring.                                                           |
| G039 Leveling and Sharpening                         | In repeated recall, some details are omitted or flattened, while others become exaggerated, sharper, or more central. The error is that repeated storytelling makes memory cleaner but less faithful.                                      | **Case:** After a difficult interview, a person first says: “The interviewer seemed neutral, but one question was aggressive.” After telling the story several times, it becomes: “The interviewer hated me from the first minute.” Nuance is leveled; one striking moment is sharpened into the whole story.                                                | Ask: Which details disappeared over repeated retelling? Which detail became more dramatic or central? What did the earliest version say?                                     | Related to narrative memory, rumor transmission, schema, confirmation bias, and emotional memory.                                                                            |
| G040 Illusions of Change or Stability            | People may exaggerate either how much they have changed or how stable they have always been. The error is reconstructing the past to fit the present identity story.                                                                       | **Case:** A person who now values discipline says: “I used to be completely chaotic; now I’m a different person.” Old messages show that they already had structured periods before. Another person says: “I’ve always believed this,” but past essays show a different view. Memory is adjusted to support either transformation or continuity.             | Ask: Am I telling a change story or a continuity story? What records from the past support or contradict it?                                                                 | Related to self-consistency bias, identity memory, rosy retrospection, autobiographical reasoning, and self-enhancement.                                                     |
| G041 Self-consistency Bias                         | People remember their past attitudes, values, and beliefs as more similar to their current ones than they actually were. The error is using current identity as a template for past identity.                                              | **Case:** Someone now strongly supports a policy and says: “I was always basically on this side.” Later, old posts show that they were uncertain or even opposed it several years earlier.
