The present belief has recolored the remembered past, making personal development look more continuous.                                                           | Ask: What did I actually say, write, choose, or believe then? Am I remembering the past, or making it coherent with who I am now?                                            | A specific form of stability illusion. Related to confirmation bias, self-serving memory, autobiographical reconstruction, and belief updating.                              |

## H. Deduction, Belief Evaluation, and Logical Boundary Zone

This class covers the boundary between cognitive bias and logical fallacy. The common error is that formal reasoning is contaminated by belief, content, surface language, familiar conclusions, and argumentative posture. Some items here are not cognitive biases in the strict experimental sense; they are logical or argumentative errors. They are still worth keeping because they show where psychological processing interferes with formal validity. The plan identifies H as this logical boundary section, with H1 focused on deductive and formal reasoning biases.

### H1. Deductive and Formal Reasoning Biases

Research on deductive reasoning often measures performance against formal logic, but this does not mean every everyday reasoning situation should be judged only by formal logic. The key point is narrower: when a task explicitly asks whether a conclusion follows necessarily from given premises, background belief, plausibility, and real-world knowledge should not decide the answer. In these cases, people are often influenced by content and context even when those factors are logically irrelevant.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                            | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                                      | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                                                          |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| H001 Belief Bias                                    | People judge whether an argument is logically valid by whether its conclusion seems believable. The error is confusing truth of conclusion with validity of inference. A valid argument can have an unbelievable conclusion if the premises are strange; an invalid argument can have a believable conclusion if the conclusion happens to be true. | **Case:** Someone sees: “All mammals need oxygen. All whales need oxygen. Therefore, all whales are mammals.” The conclusion is true, so the argument feels acceptable. But the form is invalid: two groups can both need oxygen without one being included in the other. The believable conclusion hides the invalid structure.                                                                                                                           | Ask: Does the conclusion follow necessarily from the premises, or does it merely sound true? Replace the content with symbols: All A are C; all B are C; therefore all B are A. Does the form still work? | Cross-listed earlier under decision fluency, but here it is treated as a formal reasoning bias. Related to confirmation bias, syllogistic reasoning bias, content effects, and motivated reasoning.                          |
| H002 Matching Bias                                    | People choose options that match the terms mentioned in a rule, rather than options needed to test the rule. The error is surface matching: attention follows what is named, not what is logically diagnostic.                                                                                                                                      | **Case:** In the Wason selection task, the rule is: “If a card has a vowel on one side, then it has an even number on the other side.” The visible cards are A, D, 4, and 7. Many people choose A and 4 because they match “vowel” and “even number.” But to test the rule, one must turn A and 7: A checks whether a vowel has an even number; 7 checks whether an odd number has a vowel, which would falsify the rule.                                  | Ask: Which case could falsify the rule? Am I choosing what matches the words, or what tests the conditional?                                                                                              | Classic deductive reasoning bias. Related to confirmation bias, Wason selection task, conditional reasoning, and falsification failure.                                                                                      |
| H003 Atmosphere Effect                                | The quantifiers or “mood” of syllogistic premises influence the kind of conclusion people choose. The error is that the linguistic atmosphere of “all,” “some,” or “no” pulls the answer, even when the logical relation does not justify it.                                                                                                       | **Case:** A person sees: “Some artists are teachers. Some teachers are runners. Therefore, some artists are runners.” The conclusion feels natural because both premises use “some,” and the categories are plausible. But the conclusion does not necessarily follow: the artists who are teachers may be different from the teachers who are runners. The word “some” creates an atmosphere of partial overlap, but not a valid inference.               | Ask: Am I following the quantifier mood, or checking whether the conclusion must follow? Draw a Venn diagram or translate the argument into set relations.                                                | Formal reasoning boundary item. Related to syllogistic reasoning bias, belief bias, conversion error, and linguistic surface effects.                                                                                        |
| H004 Conversion Error                                 | People wrongly reverse the subject and predicate of a statement, treating “All A are B” as if it also means “All B are A.” The error is confusing one-way inclusion with two-way equivalence.                                                                                                                                                       | **Case:** A child learns: “All cats are animals.” Later they say: “So all animals are cats.” The original statement only places cats inside the larger category of animals. It says nothing about dogs, birds, fish, or other animals. The direction of inclusion has been reversed.                                                                                                                                                                       | Ask: Is the statement reversible? Does “All A are B” also imply “All B are A”? Try a category diagram: which set is inside which?                                                                         | Related to illicit conversion, affirming the consequent, category mistakes, and set-inclusion errors.                                                                                                                        |
| H005 Illicit Conversion                               | A logical conversion is applied where it is not licensed. Some propositions can be converted safely, but universal affirmative statements usually cannot. The error is applying a valid-looking transformation outside its permitted conditions.                                                                                                    | **Case:** Someone argues: “All certified lawyers passed the bar exam. Therefore, everyone who passed the bar exam is a certified lawyer.” The conversion is invalid because passing the exam may be necessary but not sufficient; there may be licensing, character, admission, or jurisdiction requirements.                                                                                                                                              | Ask: What kind of proposition is being converted? Is the inference preserving logical equivalence, or changing a one-way condition into a two-way condition?                                              | More technical than ordinary conversion error. Related to necessary / sufficient confusion, affirming the consequent, biconditional confusion, and formal fallacies.                                                         |
| H006 Syllogistic Reasoning Bias                    | In syllogisms, people often mix logical form with content plausibility, prior knowledge, quantifier mood, and mental models. The error is treating a formally constrained task as ordinary real-world plausibility judgment.                                                                                                                        | **Case:** Argument A: “All birds are animals. All sparrows are birds. Therefore, all sparrows are animals.” This is valid and believable. Argument B: “All birds are machines. All sparrows are birds. Therefore, all sparrows are machines.” This is also valid, if the premises are assumed true, but many reject it because the conclusion conflicts with real knowledge. In formal deduction, premise acceptance is separated from inference validity. | Ask: Am I evaluating whether the premises are true, or whether the conclusion follows if the premises are true? Separate premise truth from inferential validity.                                         | Umbrella category for belief bias, atmosphere effect, conversion errors, and content effects in syllogisms.                                                                                                                  |
| H007 Masked Man Fallacy                              | People infer that two descriptions refer to different entities because they know something under one description but not under another. The error is treating knowledge context as transparent across identity descriptions.                                                                                                                        | **Case:** Someone says: “I know who Professor Li is. I do not know who the anonymous reviewer is. Therefore, Professor Li cannot be the anonymous reviewer.” This is invalid. One can know a person under one description and fail to know that the same person fits another description. Ignorance of identity does not prove difference of identity.                                                                                                     | Ask: Am I reasoning about the object itself, or about what someone knows under a description? Could the same entity be known in one way and unknown in another?                                           | More a logical fallacy than a cognitive bias, but psychologically tempting because knowledge feels like a property of the object. Related to intensional contexts, opacity, identity confusion, and source misunderstanding. |

### H2. Conditional, Necessary–Sufficient, and Modal Reasoning Errors

This group covers errors around conditional reasoning: “if,” “only if,” “unless,” “necessary,” “sufficient,” “possible,” and “must.” Conditional logic is difficult because everyday language often compresses several relations into one sentence. A sentence may express causation, evidence, permission, threat, rule, guarantee, expectation, or strict implication. The core task is to separate logical structure from conversational habit. Standard critical-thinking materials distinguish the valid forms of affirming the antecedent and denying the consequent from the invalid forms of affirming the consequent and denying the antecedent.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task  | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                                         | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                               | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                 |
| ------------------------------------------------------------- | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| H008 Affirming the Consequent                        | From “If P, then Q” and “Q,” people infer “P.” The error is treating a sufficient condition as if it were also necessary. Q may have many causes or routes.                                                                                                                                   | **Case:** “If a student plagiarized, their essay may contain strange citation patterns. This essay contains strange citation patterns. Therefore, the student plagiarized.” The conclusion may be possible, but it does not follow. Strange citation patterns may come from poor note-taking, unfamiliarity with citation style, translation tools, or rushed editing.        | Ask: Are there other ways Q could happen without P? Does the argument prove P, or only make P one possible explanation?                                | Formal fallacy. Related to confirmation bias, diagnostic reasoning error, necessary / sufficient confusion, and abductive reasoning.                                |
| H009 Denying the Antecedent                          | From “If P, then Q” and “not P,” people infer “not Q.” The error is assuming that because one route to Q is absent, Q cannot occur.                                                                                                                                                           | **Case:** “If this candidate has a law degree from a top school, they will be competent. This candidate does not have a law degree from a top school. Therefore, they will not be competent.” The inference fails because competence may come from experience, training, discipline, mentorship, or another institution.                                                      | Ask: Is P the only route to Q, or merely one route? What other sufficient conditions could produce Q?                                                  | Formal fallacy. Related to false exclusion, credential bias, necessary / sufficient confusion, and binary thinking.                                                 |
| H010 Necessary–Sufficient Condition Confusion | People confuse what is required for something with what is enough to produce it. A necessary condition must be present; a sufficient condition guarantees the result. The error reverses explanatory direction.                                                                               | **Case:** “A visa application requires a valid passport. He has a valid passport. Therefore, his visa application should be approved.” A passport is necessary, not sufficient. Approval may also require eligibility, funds, documents, timing, health checks, and legal status.                                                                                             | Ask: Is this condition required, enough, both, or neither? Can the condition be present while the result still fails?                                  | Central conditional-reasoning error. Related to affirming the consequent, denying the antecedent, biconditional confusion, and causal oversimplification.           |
| H011 Conditional–Biconditional Confusion       | People treat “If P, then Q” as if it meant “P if and only if Q.” The error is adding a reverse implication that the original sentence did not state.                                                                                                                                          | **Case:** A teacher says: “If your essay lacks citations, it will fail.” A student concludes: “So if I add citations, it will pass.” But the rule only says lack of citations is sufficient for failure. It does not say citations are sufficient for passing. The essay may still fail for weak argument, irrelevance, or plagiarism.                                        | Ask: Does the sentence state one direction or both directions? Is the reverse also explicitly asserted?                                                | Related to necessary / sufficient confusion, affirming the consequent, false security, and rule misreading.                                                         |
| H012 Unless Misreading                               | “Unless” statements are often mistranslated into conditionals. The error is missing that “A unless B” usually means “if not B, then A,” or “A except when B.”                                                                                                                                 | **Case:** A policy says: “You cannot enter the exam room unless you bring valid ID.” A student reads this as: “If I bring valid ID, I can enter.” But valid ID may be necessary, not sufficient. They may also need registration, arrival before the cutoff time, and permitted materials only.                                                                               | Ask: What happens if the exception is absent? Does the exception guarantee the result, or merely remove one barrier?                                   | Related to necessary conditions, conditional translation, policy interpretation, and biconditional confusion.                                                       |
| H013 Entailment–Implication Confusion         | People confuse strict logical entailment with conversational implication or ordinary hinting. The error is treating what a speaker suggests as if it follows logically, or treating logical implication as if it carried normal conversational meaning.                                       | **Case:** A manager says: “Some applicants met the minimum requirements.” In ordinary conversation, people may infer that not all applicants met them. But logically, “some” does not always exclude “all” unless the context adds that contrast. The conversational implication may be reasonable, but it is not the same as strict entailment.                              | Ask: Is this logically entailed, or only pragmatically suggested? Would the sentence remain true if the stronger claim were also true?                 | Boundary between logic and pragmatics. Related to scalar implicature, ambiguity, equivocation, and ordinary-language fallacies.                                     |
| H014 Modal Confusion                                 | People confuse different kinds of possibility and necessity: logical, physical, legal, practical, epistemic, moral, or technological. The error is moving from one modal sense to another without argument.                                                                                   | **Case:** “It is technically possible to collect every user’s location data. Therefore, the company can do it.” The first “possible” means technically feasible; the second “can” may imply legal permission, ethical acceptability, organizational capacity, or social legitimacy. The argument slides between modal domains.                                                | Ask: Possible in what sense? Necessary under which rule? Is this logical, physical, legal, moral, practical, or epistemic modality?                    | Related to equivocation, category error, feasibility fallacy, ought–can issues, and policy reasoning.                                                               |
| H015 Conceivability–Possibility Confusion      | People infer that because something can be imagined or described, it is genuinely possible in the relevant sense. The error is confusing psychological conceivability with logical, physical, or practical possibility.                                                                       | **Case:** Someone says: “I can imagine a machine that reads every human thought perfectly, so such a machine is possible.” But imagination may omit physical limits, measurement problems, privacy constraints, computational cost, and conceptual ambiguity about “thought.” A picture in the mind is not a proof of possibility.                                            | Ask: What kind of possibility is being claimed? Does the imagined scenario satisfy the constraints of logic, physics, biology, law, or implementation? | Important in philosophy and technology debates. Related to modal confusion, science-fiction reasoning, thought experiments, and possibility inflation.              |

### H3. Argument, Language, and Principle Misuse

This section keeps only argument-level items that are not better housed in probability, social judgment, automation, or tool-fixation sections. The remaining cluster covers argument from fallacy, rhyme-as-reason, and Occam’s Razor misuse.

The common error is not merely “using a bad argument.” It is allowing surface markers of credibility to replace actual support: a claim sounds simple, has a memorable phrase, or exposes a flaw in one argument and is then treated as if the whole conclusion were settled. Argument evaluation then shifts from “What follows from what?” to “What feels credible, fluent, or rhetorically satisfying?”

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                         | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                     |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| H016 Argument from Fallacy / Fallacy Fallacy      | People infer that a conclusion is false because the argument offered for it contains a fallacy. The error is confusing failure of one argument with falsity of the conclusion.                | **Case:** Person A says: “This medicine works because a famous actor said it saved him.” That is a weak appeal to celebrity. Person B replies: “That is an appeal to authority, so the medicine does not work.” Person B has correctly attacked the argument, but has not evaluated clinical evidence. The conclusion may be true, false, or uncertain for other reasons. | Ask: Have I shown that this argument fails, or that the conclusion is false? What independent evidence bears on the conclusion?                                                        | A logical fallacy, not a standard cognitive bias. Related to ad hominem reversal, belief polarization, motivated reasoning, and argument evaluation.                    |
| H017 Rhyme-as-reason Effect                      | Rhyming, rhythm, parallelism, or slogan-like compression makes a claim feel more truthful. In argument form, verbal elegance performs the work of evidence.                                   | **Case:** A fitness slogan says: “No pain, no gain.” Someone uses it to justify training through injury. The phrase is memorable and forceful, but it collapses different kinds of discomfort: productive effort, fatigue, injury warning, and overtraining risk. The rhyme hides the distinction.                                                                        | Ask: If I remove the rhyme or slogan form, is the claim still supported? Which distinctions does the phrase compress or erase?                                                         | Related to cognitive ease, illusory truth, rhetoric, aphorisms, propaganda, and E5 language-fluency effects.                                                            |
| H018 Occam’s Razor Misuse                        | A simpler explanation is preferred even when it does not account for the evidence. In argument form, “simple” becomes a rhetorical weapon against necessary complexity.                       | **Case:** Someone explains a university’s falling enrollment by saying: “It is simply poor marketing.” Marketing may matter, but enrollment may also involve tuition, visa rules, labor-market expectations, rankings, housing costs, curriculum design, and demographic changes. The simple explanation wins because it is clean, not because it explains enough.        | Ask: Does the simpler explanation cover all major evidence? Are competing explanations equally adequate, or am I discarding complexity that the facts require?                         | Occam’s Razor itself is a methodological principle, not a bias. Misuse occurs when simplicity overrides explanatory adequacy. Related to reductionism, narrative simplification, and single-cause explanations.                       |

## I. Boundary, Appendix, and Merge Zone

This class collects entries that should not simply be treated as new, separate biases. Some are duplicate names, some are subtypes, some are principles, some are social or organizational mechanisms, and some are better handled as cross-references. The purpose of this section is editorial control: keep the catalogue comprehensive without inflating it into a list where every phrase becomes a new bias. The original plan places I after the logic boundary section and uses it to handle merge decisions, boundary notes, and appendix-level items.

### I1. Items Recommended for Merge or Cross-reference

This table does not expand the system by adding entirely new mechanisms. It records how repeated or overlapping entries should be handled. The shared rule is: if two names describe the same mechanism, merge them; if they describe different aspects of one mechanism, cross-reference them; if a phrase is only a slogan, principle, or applied example, keep it in the boundary column rather than treating it as a full independent bias.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                       | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                      | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                       | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                              |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | --------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| I001 Baader–Meinhof Phenomenon                               | A popular name for the frequency illusion: once something has entered attention, it appears to occur everywhere. The error is mistaking changed attention for changed frequency.                                            | **Case:** A person learns the term “Bayesian reasoning” for the first time. Within two days, they see it in a book, a video title, and a forum post. They conclude: “Everyone suddenly started talking about this.” More likely, the term was already present; their attentional filter changed.                                     | Ask: Did the world change, or did my attention change? What was the baseline frequency before I noticed it?                                                | Merge into `Frequency Illusion`. Baader–Meinhof is a memorable popular label, not a separate mechanism. Related to availability, priming, and recency illusion.                                  |
| I002 Law of the Hammer / Maslow’s Hammer / Golden Hammer     | Duplicate or near-duplicate names for the law of the instrument: a familiar tool or method shapes how problems are understood. The error is tool-driven problem definition.                                                 | **Case:** A team has recently adopted a new AI workflow. Soon every problem is described as an AI problem: customer service, writing, research, hiring, note-taking, even decisions requiring legal or interpersonal judgment. The tool becomes the default lens before the problem is diagnosed.                                    | Ask: Am I choosing this tool because the task requires it, or because it is available and fashionable? What would the problem look like without this tool? | Merge into `Law of the Instrument`. Keep “hammer” names as aliases. Related to functional fixedness, automation bias, and professional deformation.                                              |
| I003 Irrational Escalation                                   | Another label for escalation of commitment: continuing to invest in a failing course of action because of prior investment, identity, or public commitment. The error is using past cost to justify future cost.            | **Case:** A company has already spent 8 months building an internal tool. Tests show that buying an external tool would now be cheaper and better. The team says: “We have come too far to stop.” The previous investment is real, but it cannot make future investment rational by itself.                                          | Ask: If no money or effort had already been spent, would we choose this option today? What is the expected future value from this point forward?           | Merge into `Escalation of Commitment` or `Sunk Cost Fallacy`, depending on emphasis. “Irrational escalation” is usually the organizational / decision-process label.                             |
| I004 Lake Wobegon Effect                                     | A named form of illusory superiority: most people rate themselves above average on desirable traits. The error depends on vague standards, selective comparison, and self-enhancement.                                      | **Case:** In a writing group, most members believe their own taste is above average. One defines taste as liking difficult books; another defines it as emotional sensitivity; another defines it as not following trends. Because the criterion is flexible, everyone can build an above-average self.                              | Ask: Above average relative to which group, by which measurable criterion? What would blind peer evaluation show?                                          | Merge into `Illusory Superiority`. Keep as an alias or classic cultural label. Related to self-serving bias, overconfidence, and Dunning–Kruger effect.                                          |
| I005 Forer Effect / Barnum Effect                            | Two names for the same basic mechanism: vague, general personality descriptions are experienced as highly personal and accurate. The error is subjective validation of broadly applicable statements.                       | **Case:** A personality test says: “You need others to like you, but you also value independence; you have unused potential; you can be disciplined, but sometimes procrastinate.” The reader says: “This is exactly me.” The statement feels personal because it is broad enough to fit many people.                                | Ask: Would many other people also accept this description? Does the statement make risky, specific, falsifiable predictions?                               | Merge the two names. `Forer Effect` is tied to the classic experimental demonstration; `Barnum Effect` is the broader popular name. Related to subjective validation and cold reading.           |
| I006 Bike-shedding Effect / Law of Triviality                | Two names for the same group-decision tendency: people overdiscuss simple, low-stakes issues because they are easier to understand and comment on.                                                                          | **Case:** A committee must approve a major curriculum reform. It spends 10 minutes on graduation requirements and 50 minutes debating the wording of a course title. Everyone can comment on the title; fewer people understand the reform’s structural consequences.                                                                | Ask: Is discussion time proportional to importance, risk, and irreversibility? Are we talking about this because it is easy?                               | Merge or treat as one cluster. `Bike-shedding` is the vivid example; `Law of Triviality` is the general formulation. Related to Delmore effect, ambiguity avoidance, and group decision failure. |
| I007 Primacy / Recency / Serial Position Effect              | These should be handled as a linked memory cluster. Serial position effect is the umbrella; primacy and recency are the two main position advantages.                                                                       | **Case:** After a 12-item presentation, the audience remembers the opening story and the final recommendation, but forgets the middle evidence. Later they judge the talk by those remembered edges. The problem is not that the edges were necessarily most important; they occupied privileged positions.                          | Ask: What appeared in the middle? Am I confusing recall strength with importance? Should I review the full sequence?                                       | Treat under `Serial Position Effect`, with `Primacy Effect` and `Recency Effect` as subentries. Related to peak-end rule, modality effect, and presentation design.                              |
| I008 Loss Aversion / Endowment Effect / Status Quo Bias      | These are related but not identical. Loss aversion is sensitivity to losses relative to gains; endowment effect is increased valuation through ownership; status quo bias is preference for the current state.              | **Case:** A person refuses to switch phone plans. Part of the reluctance comes from fear of losing familiar benefits; part comes from overvaluing the current plan because it is “mine”; part comes from not wanting to disturb the existing arrangement. These overlap, but each mechanism explains a different part of the choice. | Ask: Is the resistance caused by loss framing, ownership, default inertia, switching cost, or real comparative value?                                      | Do not fully merge. Cross-reference them as a reference-point / ownership / default cluster. Each deserves separate treatment in decision sections.                                              |
| I009 Source Confusion / Misattribution                       | These are closely related memory errors. Misattribution is the broader category; source confusion is a specific form in which the origin of remembered information is mistaken.                                             | **Case:** A person says: “A legal database showed this rule.” Later, they realize they saw it in a blog summarizing a case. The content survived, but the source was upgraded in memory. That is source confusion within the broader class of memory misattribution.                                                                 | Ask: What exactly is remembered: the claim, the source, the time, or the evidence chain? Can the original source be located?                               | Treat `Misattribution of Memory` as umbrella and `Source Confusion` as a major subtype. Related to cryptomnesia, false memory, misinformation effect, and citation errors.                       |
| I010 Frequency Illusion / Recency Illusion                   | These are adjacent but not identical. Frequency illusion concerns noticing something and then perceiving it as common; recency illusion concerns believing something recently noticed is newly created or newly widespread. | **Case:** A reader notices the word “vibe” in several essays and says: “This word is suddenly everywhere.” That may be frequency illusion. If they add, “This word didn’t exist before,” that becomes recency illusion. The first misjudges frequency; the second misjudges historical age.                                          | Ask: Am I claiming it is frequent, or that it is new? What do older records, corpora, archives, or timelines show?                                         | Cross-reference rather than fully merge. Both arise from attention and availability, but one concerns frequency estimation, the other temporal origin.                                           |

### I2. Items That Belong in Boundary Notes

Items I011–I028 belong in the boundary-note section: these are useful concepts, but they should not all be treated as standard cognitive biases. Some are principles, some are empirical regularities, some are research-ecology effects, some are organizational mechanisms, some are valid logical tools, and some are discussion habits.

The editorial rule is simple: keep them, but mark their status clearly. A bias catalogue becomes weaker when every useful proverb, theory, or management label is inflated into a psychological bias. These entries are valuable precisely because they help define the edge of the catalogue.

| No. + Bias Name / Aliases / Classic Task | Mechanism and Cognitive Function + Core Definition and Error Analysis                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Typical Triggers + Typical Case                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                              | Debiasing Method / Checking Question                                                                                                                   | Boundaries and Neighboring Concepts                                                                                                                                                    |
| ------------------------------------------------------------ | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- |
| I011 Murphy’s Law                                     | “Anything that can go wrong will go wrong” is a risk-management proverb, not a cognitive bias in the strict sense. Its value is to remind people that systems fail through overlooked contingencies. The error appears when the phrase becomes fatalism rather than risk analysis. | **Case:** A team says before launch: “Murphy’s Law — something will break.” If they use that sentence to run failure-mode checks, backup plans, and monitoring, it is useful. If they use it as vague pessimism without estimating probability or preparing responses, it becomes rhetorical noise.                                          | Ask: What exactly can fail, how likely is it, and what would we do if it fails?                                                                        | Not a standard cognitive bias. Related to planning fallacy, normalcy bias, risk management, pessimism bias, and pre-mortem analysis.                                                   |
| I012 Hofstadter’s Law                              | “It always takes longer than you expect, even when you take Hofstadter’s Law into account.” This is closer to a planning proverb than a distinct bias. It captures recursive underestimation in complex projects.                                                                  | **Case:** A software team doubles its initial estimate from 2 weeks to 4 weeks “to be safe,” but the task still takes 8 weeks because integration issues, unclear requirements, and review delays were not represented. The law names the pattern, but does not explain it alone.                                                            | Ask: What happened in comparable past projects? Which hidden coordination, testing, review, or dependency costs are missing?                           | Better treated as a planning-rule boundary item. Related to planning fallacy, optimism bias, outside view, and project-management estimation.                                          |
| I013 Chesterton’s Fence                             | A principle: do not remove an existing rule, institution, or practice before understanding what problem it solved. The error is not “changing old things,” but reforming without functional diagnosis.                                                                             | **Case:** A company removes a tedious approval step to “increase efficiency.” Months later, billing errors increase because that step had quietly caught contract mismatches. The old rule was annoying, but it had a function. Reform failed because no one first asked what the fence was doing.                                           | Ask: What problem did this rule originally solve? Is that problem gone, changed, or still present?                                                     | A prudential principle, not a bias. Related to status quo bias, system justification, institutional memory, and reform design.                                                         |
| I014 Occam’s Razor                                   | A methodological principle: when explanations account for the evidence equally well, prefer the simpler one. It becomes a bias only when simplicity is chosen before checking explanatory adequacy.                                                                                | **Case:** A manager says: “Sales dropped because the team is lazy.” This is simple, but it ignores pricing, seasonality, product quality, competitor action, channel changes, and sales incentives. Simplicity becomes a shortcut that erases necessary causes.                                                                              | Ask: Are the explanations equally adequate? What evidence does the simple explanation fail to cover?                                                   | The principle belongs in boundary notes; `Occam’s Razor Misuse` belongs in the main bias / fallacy table. Related to reductionism, narrative simplification, and single-cause fallacy. |
| I015 Sapir–Whorf–Korzybski Hypothesis                        | A broad language-and-thought hypothesis: language may influence what distinctions are salient, easy to express, or habitually noticed. The mistake is turning a complex theoretical claim into a simple bias label.                                                                | **Case:** A company has only one word for several kinds of “user dissatisfaction”: bugs, confusion, unmet need, distrust, and price resistance. Because everything is called “complaint,” different problems are handled with the same customer-service script. Vocabulary shapes diagnosis, but it does not mechanically determine thought. | Ask: What distinctions does the language make easy or hard to see? Are missing terms causing missing analysis?                                         | A theory / hypothesis, not a standard bias. Related to framing, labeling, category design, linguistic relativity, and concept formation.                                               |
| I016 Magic Number 7±2                             | A classic statement about working-memory capacity, often simplified as “people can hold about seven items.” It describes a cognitive limit more than a bias. The risk is overusing the number as if it were an exact universal law.                                                | **Case:** A trainer gives employees 12 new procedural rules verbally and expects accurate recall. People remember fragments and reorder steps. The issue is not laziness; the instruction exceeded practical working-memory capacity.                                                                                                        | Ask: Can the material be chunked, externalized, written, or practiced? How many independent units are actually being held?                             | Cognitive-capacity limit, not a bias. Related to cognitive load, chunking, list-length effect, serial recall, and interface design.                                                    |
| I017 Placebo Effect                                  | Expectation, context, ritual, and meaning can produce subjective or physiological improvement. It is not simply “thinking makes it true,” and not purely a cognitive bias.                                                                                                         | **Case:** A patient receives an inert pill from a trusted doctor in a convincing medical setting and reports reduced pain. The improvement may be real at the level of subjective pain and bodily response, but it does not prove that the pill has a specific pharmacological effect.                                                       | Ask: Was there a control group? Is the improvement specific to the treatment mechanism, or due to expectation, care, ritual, and natural recovery?     | Not fully a cognitive bias. Related to expectation bias, conditioning, pain perception, clinical trial design, and nocebo effect.                                                      |
| I018 Weber–Fechner Law                            | A psychophysical law: perceived change often depends on proportional rather than absolute change. It describes perception, not faulty reasoning by itself.                                                                                                                         | **Case:** A 10-dollar discount feels large on a 30-dollar item but trivial on a 2,000-dollar laptop. The absolute amount is identical, but subjective salience changes with baseline size.                                                                                                                                                   | Ask: Am I evaluating absolute value, proportional value, or subjective felt difference? Which one matters for this decision?                           | Psychophysical regularity. Related to contrast effect, framing, denomination effect, money illusion, and diminishing sensitivity in prospect theory.                                   |
| I019 Publication Bias                                 | Significant, positive, surprising, or publishable findings are more likely to appear in the literature than null or negative findings. This is mainly a research-ecosystem bias, not an individual judgment bias.                                                                  | **Case:** A reader reviews published studies and sees that most report a supplement improves memory. Unpublished null results, failed replications, and small negative studies are harder to find. The visible literature overstates the effect.                                                                                             | Ask: Are unpublished studies, preregistrations, replications, and meta-analytic corrections available?                                                 | Research-system bias. Related to file-drawer problem, selection bias, p-hacking, survivorship bias, and evidence synthesis.                                                            |
| I020 Observer Effect                                 | Observation can change the phenomenon being observed. In social contexts, people alter behavior when they know they are being watched. The error is assuming measurement is passive.                                                                                               | **Case:** Managers monitor customer-service calls for one week. Employees become unusually polite and careful during that period. The recorded behavior is real, but it is not necessarily ordinary behavior; observation has changed the target.                                                                                            | Ask: Did observation alter the behavior? Can measurement be unobtrusive, repeated, blinded, or compared with baseline data?                            | Boundary item. Related to Hawthorne effect, observer-expectancy effect, demand characteristics, experimenter bias, and measurement effects.                                            |
| I021 HIPPO Problem / Highest Paid Person’s Opinion           | In organizations, the highest-paid or highest-status person’s opinion can dominate group judgment. This is an organizational power mechanism more than a pure cognitive bias.                                                                                                      | **Case:** A product team privately prefers option B based on user data. In the meeting, the senior executive casually says option A “feels more premium.” The room aligns with A. The decision appears consensual, but status has overwritten independent assessment.                                                                        | Ask: Were judgments collected before senior opinion was revealed? Is disagreement safe? What does the evidence show without hierarchy?                 | Organizational decision mechanism. Related to authority bias, groupthink, conformity, power distance, and group decision design.                                                       |
| I022 Swimmer’s Body Illusion                       | People mistake selection effects for training effects: they infer that an activity produces the traits that actually helped people enter or succeed in that activity.                                                                                                              | **Case:** Someone joins swimming because elite swimmers have long, lean bodies and thinks swimming will produce that body type. Training matters, but elite swimmers are also selected partly because their body structure suits the sport. The observed body is not pure treatment effect.                                                  | Ask: Did the activity cause the trait, or did people with the trait select into / survive in the activity? What comparison group would test causality? | Related to survivorship bias, selection bias, causal inference, fitness marketing, and reverse causality.                                                                              |
| I023 Armchair Fallacy                                | People make confident judgments from abstract reasoning while lacking practice, field exposure, data, or institutional constraints. It is more a discussion habit than a named experimental bias.                                                                                  | **Case:** Someone designs a policy for delivery workers without interviewing workers, reading operational data, or observing routes. The proposal is elegant on paper but fails when weather, platform incentives, customer behavior, and time pressure enter.                                                                               | Ask: What practical constraints, empirical data, or lived experience constrain this claim? Has the idea met the field?                                 | Boundary item. Related to illusion of explanatory depth, overconfidence, streetlight effect, and technocratic simplification.                                                          |
| I024 Bucket Error                                      | Heterogeneous phenomena are thrown into one explanatory bucket, so important distinctions disappear. The error is bad classification: one label absorbs too much.                                                                                                                  | **Case:** In an online debate, every disliked social problem is classified as “capital,” “human nature,” or “algorithm.” Each label explains something, but when used as a universal bucket, it prevents finer distinctions between incentives, institutions, culture, technology, and individual action.                                    | Ask: Are these cases actually the same kind of thing? What distinctions disappear if I use this label?                                                 | Better treated as classification error or explanatory overcompression. Related to category mistakes, reductionism, narrative gravity, and single-cause explanation.                    |
| I025 Law of Narrative Gravity                       | Facts are pulled into a strong story until details that do not fit are ignored, bent, or reinterpreted. This is a useful name for narrative over-assimilation, but not a standard cognitive-bias label.                                                                            | **Case:** A public scandal begins with mixed evidence: incompetence, incentives, poor oversight, and ordinary error. Online discussion soon turns it into a clean villain story. Every new fact is interpreted as proof of the villain plot; contradictory facts are called cover-up. The story becomes stronger than the evidence.          | Ask: Is the story organizing evidence, or replacing it? Which facts resist the narrative? What would a messier explanation look like?                  | Boundary / narrative-analysis item. Related to narrative fallacy, confirmation bias, conspiracy thinking, law of triviality, and bucket error.                                         |

| I026 Modus Ponens / Affirming the Antecedent         | A valid conditional form: If P, then Q; P; therefore Q. The reasoning is valid when the conditional is accepted. The common error is not the form itself, but confusing validity with soundness: the conclusion follows if the premises are true, but the premises still require examination. | **Case:** “If the contract was signed after the deadline, it is invalid under this rule. The contract was signed after the deadline. Therefore, it is invalid under this rule.” The form is valid. But one still must check whether the rule applies, whether the deadline was legally extended, and whether “signed” means executed, submitted, or received.                 | Ask: The form is valid, but are the premises true and applicable? Are there exceptions, qualifications, or hidden definitions?                         | Valid inference form, not a bias. Included to mark the contrast with affirming the consequent. Related to soundness, rule application, and hidden-premise analysis. |
| I027 Modus Tollens / Denying the Consequent          | A valid conditional form: If P, then Q; not Q; therefore not P. The reasoning works because if P guarantees Q, and Q is absent, P cannot be true. The possible error is misidentifying what Q actually requires.                                                                              | **Case:** “If the server is completely down, no user can log in. Some users can log in. Therefore, the server is not completely down.” This is valid if the conditional is correct. But it does not prove that the system is healthy; there may be partial failure, regional outage, or degraded service.                                                                     | Ask: Is Q really absent? Did P guarantee exactly Q, or only a version of Q? What weaker conclusion follows?                                            | Valid inference form. Related to falsification, diagnostic reasoning, conditional testing, and overstrong conclusion.                                               |
| I028 Contraposition                                   | A valid transformation: “If P, then Q” is equivalent to “If not Q, then not P.” The error occurs when people confuse contraposition with invalid reversal or invalid negation.                                                                                                                | **Case:** From “If a device is fully charged, it can turn on,” the valid contrapositive is: “If it cannot turn on, then it is not fully charged,” assuming the original rule is true. But people often produce the invalid converse: “If it can turn on, it is fully charged,” or the invalid inverse: “If it is not fully charged, it cannot turn on.” Both add assumptions. | Ask: Did I negate both parts and reverse the direction, or did I only reverse / only negate?                                                           | Valid logical tool, not a bias. Related to modus tollens, conversion error, inverse error, and conditional translation.                                             |

# 中文表格

## A1. 基础概念与理论框架

这个表是整部“认知偏误大全”的概念入口。这些条目不是普通偏误，而是用来界定什么叫偏误、偏误为什么不同于普通错误、快速思维为什么有时有用、纠偏为什么不能只靠自我提醒的理论工具。它们共同针对的问题是概念混乱：把所有错误都叫偏误，把所有启发式都当成非理性，或者把纠偏理解成“更努力地保持理性”。

| 编号 + 偏误名称（中文 / English / 别名 / 经典题型）                   | 所属机制与认知功能 + 核心定义与错误分析                                                                       | 典型触发条件 + 典型案例                                                                                                                        | 纠偏方法 / 检查问题                                     | 边界与相邻概念                                                                  |
| ----------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| A001 `认知偏差 / Cognitive Bias`                          | 指判断、推理、决策或记忆相对于事实、统计、逻辑或理性标准发生的系统性偏离。关键不只是“这个人错了”，而是错误具有稳定方向。认知偏差解释的是：为什么人在相似条件下会反复以相似方式误判。 | **案例：** 一家公司收到一条愤怒用户投诉，用户说：“你们的产品毁了我这一整周。”会议上，所有人开始像讨论“多数用户都很愤怒”一样讨论产品问题。但真实数据是 97% 的用户满意。那条投诉未必是假的，错误在于把一个情绪强烈、容易想起的个案，当成了整体用户群的代表。 | 偏离了什么标准？这是孤立失误，还是重复出现的模式？有什么分布、比较组或记录可以检验这个判断？  | 范围比逻辑谬误更宽。不等于无知、说谎、价值分歧或普通信息不足。聪明人也会有认知偏差。                               |
| A002 `认知幻觉 / Cognitive Illusion`                      | 认知偏差中更严格的一类。类似视觉错觉，认知幻觉指人在特定任务结构下，在思维、判断或记忆中出现稳定、可重复的扭曲。它并不要求当事人故意不理性。                      | **案例：** 老师问：“摩西把每种动物带了几只上方舟？”很多学生会回答“两只”，因为这句话符合一个熟悉的圣经故事脚本。等反应过来才发现，方舟故事中的人物是诺亚，不是摩西。心智先接受了语义契合，后检查具体词项。                            | 这个错误是否会在人群和任务中反复出现？它是否由问题结构、记忆结构或表征方式诱发？        | 比认知偏差更窄。特别适合分析概率幻觉、记忆幻觉和推理幻觉。并非所有坏判断都是认知幻觉。                              |
| A003 `启发式 / Heuristic`                                | 在时间、信息或计算能力有限时使用的判断捷径。启发式本身不是错误；只有当捷径被用于不适合的环境，或者它依赖的线索不可靠时，才会产生偏差。                         | **案例：** 旅行者来到陌生城市，看到一家餐馆排队很多，就决定进去吃。很多时候这是有用的启发式：人多可能说明味道好。但如果排队来自旅游促销、刷评、地理位置便利，而非真实质量，这个捷径就失灵了。错误不是使用捷径，而是假设捷径永远指向质量。              | 这个捷径依赖什么线索？在当前环境中，这个线索可靠吗？什么反馈能证明这个启发式有效？       | 可得性、代表性、锚定常被视为主要启发式家族。启发式在一个环境中可能适应，在另一个环境中可能误导。                         |
| A004 双系统理论 / System 1 / System 2                    | 区分快速、自动、联想式加工，与较慢、费力、受控制推理的框架。许多偏差发生在自动答案显得足够好，而缓慢系统没有检查任务是否需要另一种推理方式时。                     | **案例：** 有人听到：“球拍和球一共 1.10 美元，球拍比球贵 1 美元。”他马上回答：“球 10 美分。”这个答案很顺，但错了。仔细算会发现球是 5 美分。System 1 给出直觉答案，System 2 没有及时介入。                   | 我是否接受了第一个流畅答案？慢思考是在真正检查，还是在解释为什么第一反应看起来对？       | 不能简化成 “System 1 = 非理性，System 2 = 理性”。System 1 经常高效而聪明；System 2 也可能为偏差辩护。 |
| A005 认知轻松感 / Cognitive Ease                           | 加工流畅会产生熟悉、真实、安全、优雅或连贯的感觉。错误在于把内部加工轻松感误认为外部对象的真实性或可靠性。                                       | **案例：** 一个政治口号连续几个月反复出现：“强边境，安全家庭。”即使没有新证据，它也会因为短、顺、熟悉、容易回忆而显得“很有道理”。后来人们说“它听起来就是真的”，实际上体验到的是加工流畅。                                   | 它显得真实，是因为证据充分，还是因为熟悉、重复、简单、表达顺？                 | 与真相错觉效应、曝光效应、押韵即理由效应、熟悉感相邻。轻松感是一种感觉，不是证明。                                |
| A006 所见即全部 / WYSIATI / What You See Is All There Is | 心智会根据当前可得信息建构一个连贯故事，并忽略缺席、隐藏、延迟或未收集的信息。错误在于把当前可见证据场当成完整证据场。                                 | **案例：** 招聘时，一个候选人表达自信、学历亮眼、职业故事讲得完整。面试组很快说：“她显然是最强的。”但他们还没有检查作品样本、推荐信质量、真实团队表现、面试预测准确率基础数据。可见的东西变成了全部现实；缺失的信息甚至不显得缺失。                | 还缺什么信息？在把这个故事当成完整解释前，我还需要知道什么？哪种缺失证据可能反转判断？     | 与可得性、叙事谬误、确认偏误、过度自信、光环效应相邻。WYSIATI 是许多具体偏误背后的总机制。                        |
| A007 属性替代 / Attribute Substitution / 问题替代             | 一个困难的目标问题被无意识替换成一个更容易回答的问题。因为替代过程隐藏，答案仍然感觉与原问题相关。                                           | **案例：** 选民被问：“哪位候选人的经济方案更好？”他没有分析政策、权衡、执行条件和约束，而是回答了更容易的问题：“哪位候选人看起来更真诚、更自信？”情绪印象替代了政策评价。                                            | 我实际回答的是什么问题？它和原来的问题是同一个问题吗？如果不是，我回避了哪些困难信息？     | 是解释可得性、代表性、情感启发式、规划谬误和许多政治判断的重要机制。                                       |
| A008 有限理性 / Bounded Rationality                       | 人类推理受时间、注意、记忆、信息和计算能力限制。偏差部分来自心智无法计算一切，因此必须简化。                                              | **案例：** 一个学生选择研究生项目时，要考虑排名、费用、签证、导师、城市、就业、课程、校友网络、个人适配等上百个变量。完全理性计算几乎不可能，于是他使用声誉、朋友意见、城市偏好等捷径。这些捷径必要，但也可能扭曲选择。                       | 当前判断受到哪些约束？能否用清单、比较表、外部数据或专家反馈降低认知负担？           | 说明完美理性不现实，但不是为粗糙推理开脱；它指出何处需要结构化工具。                                       |
| A009 生态理性 / Ecological Rationality                    | 一个判断策略是否理性，取决于它所处的环境。若线索可靠、反馈稳定，启发式可能有效；若环境噪声大、事件稀有、充满对抗，启发式可能失灵。                           | **案例：** 经验丰富的消防员进入房间后，突然感觉“不对劲”，还没说清理由就撤退。这个环境中，长期重复经验和即时反馈可能让直觉可靠。但同样的“相信直觉”若用在股票选择上，就可能不可靠，因为市场反馈嘈杂、延迟，而且混入大量运气。                   | 这个环境是否提供有效线索、重复练习和及时反馈？还是充满噪声、稀有事件、策略互动和运气？     | 与对人工偏差任务的批评相关，也与专业直觉、专家判断和启发式有效性相邻。                                      |
| A010 热认知与冷认知 / Hot Cognition / Cold Cognition         | 热认知带有情绪和动机负荷；冷认知更抽离、更分析化。错误在于，一个由恐惧、愤怒、自尊、欲望或身份驱动的判断，会感觉像中立推理。                              | **案例：** 一个人在网上收到尖锐评论后写道：“我已经冷静分析过了，这个人明显是恶意的。”但这次“分析”从羞辱感和愤怒开始。判断中可能有真实成分，但证据权重已经被自我防御需求重新分配。                                        | 现在有什么情绪在场？如果我更冷静、更少受威胁、更不饥饿、更不愤怒、更不急于获胜，我会怎样判断？ | 与情感启发式、动机性推理、道德判断、身份保护性认知和社会冲突相邻。                                        |
| A011 `纠偏 / Debiasing`                                 | 通过程序、格式、记录、激励和反馈减少可预测扭曲。纠偏不是消除所有偏差，而是让判断更少暴露在已知失败模式下。                                       | **案例：** 医院发现医生在高压急救中有时会漏掉步骤。医院没有只说“请大家更小心”，而是引入检查清单。清单并没有让医生变聪明，而是改变任务结构，让记忆失败和压力更少有机会造成错误。                                          | 能否重新设计这个判断任务？盲审、基础率提示、决策日志、独立估计或结构化反馈能否降低偏差？    | 单纯知道偏差名称通常很弱。有效纠偏更多依赖外部结构，而不是自信或道德真诚。                                    |

### A2. 规范标准、统计工具与纠偏工具

这些条目不是普通偏误，而是用来识别和降低偏误的标准、格式与程序。一个判断之所以能被称为“有偏”，必须有某种基准：基础率、概率结构、事实记录、校准记录、参考点或外部痕迹。它们共同的功能，是防止印象伪装成证据。一个故事可能显得连贯，一个个案可能显得有代表性，一个选择可能显得像损失，一段记忆可能显得真实；但如果没有这些工具，评价判断所需的结构仍然不可见。

| 编号 + 偏误名称（中文 / English / 别名 / 经典题型）  | 所属机制与认知功能 + 核心定义与错误分析                                                                     | 典型触发条件 + 典型案例                                                                                                                                  | 纠偏方法 / 检查问题                                                  | 边界与相邻概念                                            |
| ------------------------------------ | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------ | -------------------------------------------------- |
| A012 `基础率 / Base Rate`               | 指某个事件、条件或类别在相关总体中的一般发生率。它是概率判断的起点。常见错误是直接跳到鲜明个案、人格描述或新线索，而不先问目标事件在线索出现之前本来有多常见。           | **案例：** 一个病人在网上查症状，看到某种罕见疾病包括“疲劳、头晕、偶尔胸闷”。他很害怕，因为这些描述和自己吻合。但这些症状也常见于压力、睡眠不足、贫血、咖啡因摄入和许多轻微问题。疾病描述不是无关，但如果没有基础率，他无法知道这是高概率解释，还是只是吓人的匹配。          | 相关总体是什么？在新证据出现之前，这种情况或事件本来有多常见？                              | 工具概念，不是偏误。后续基础率忽视、贝叶斯推理错误、医学诊断、法律证据、招聘和风险判断都以它为核心。 |
| A013 `自然频率 / Natural Frequencies`    | 一种用总体中的计数表达概率的信息格式。它让分母、真阳性和假阳性变得可见。它解决的问题是：百分比经常隐藏正确判断所需的比较结构。                           | **案例：** 医生说：“这个检测准确率是 90%。”病人以为阳性结果意味着自己几乎一定患病。更清楚的说法是：“每 1000 个与你类似的人中，10 人患病；这 10 人中 9 人检测阳性；另外 990 个未患病者中，也有 99 人检测阳性。”这时病人才能看见，很多阳性结果是假阳性。 | 把百分比改写成计数，画频率树。检查：所有阳性结果中，真阳性有多少，假阳性有多少？                     | 不是偏误，而是纠偏格式。与贝叶斯推理、基础率忽视、分母忽视、检察官谬误密切相关。           |
| A014 `校准 / Calibration`              | 主观信心与真实准确率之间的匹配。好的判断不是永远不出错，而是信心程度能够对应真实表现。错误出现在一个人说“我有 90% 把握”，但长期正确率只有 60%。             | **案例：** 一个评论员每周做政治预测，总用绝对语言：“这一定会发生。”六个月后回看，许多“一定”都没有发生。问题不只是有些预测错了，而是他的信心和准确率之间没有稳定关系。                                                        | 记录带概率的预测，并在事后复盘。检查：当我说“很可能”“几乎确定”“不可能”时，这些词在我的真实记录中分别对应多少概率？ | 工具概念。是过度自信、专家判断、预测、风险评估、法律判断和医学诊断的核心检验标准。          |
| A015 `参考点 / Reference Point`         | 结果被编码为收益或损失时所依据的心理基准。人经常不是对最终状态作反应，而是对相对于自己拥有、期待、应得或想象之物的变化作反应。错误出现在任意或被操纵的参考点定义了决策的情绪意义。 | **案例：** 一个员工获得 10% 加薪。客观上，收入增加了。但她原本期待 20%，因为同事暗示“你这个级别通常会更多”。于是这次加薪反而感觉像损失。情绪反应不是只由最终薪资决定，而是由期待制造的参考点决定。                                      | 这是相对于什么的收益或损失？这个比较点是事实性的、任意的、继承来的、谈判出来的，还是被别人策略性框定的？         | 前景理论、损失厌恶、禀赋效应、现状偏差、框架效应和谈判分析的核心工具。                |
| A016 `外部视角 / Outside View`           | 一种纠偏方法：不只依赖当前案例的内部故事，而是把它放进同类案例中比较。它用经验比较替代想象中的顺利进程，从而降低过度乐观。                             | **案例：** 学生说：“这篇 20 页论文我两天就能写完，我已经知道题目了。”内部故事很顺：今晚列提纲，明天写初稿，后天早上改完。外部视角则会问：过去类似论文实际花了多久？如果以往都需要 5 到 7 天，那么两天计划很可能是幻想。                           | 相关参考类别是什么？同类案例通常发生了什么？类似项目实际花了多久？                            | 是纠正规划谬误、乐观偏差、项目预测、预算、法律策略、产品发布和学术写作的重要方法。          |
| A017 内部视角 / Inside View              | 聚焦当前案例的具体计划、意图、因果故事和想象路径。它对规划有必要，但如果忽略同类案例的统计记录，就容易有偏。                                    | **案例：** 创业团队说：“我们的产品不一样。我们有更好的创始人、更好的设计、更清楚的市场空缺。”每个失败公司也都有一个解释自己为什么特殊的内部故事。内部视角可能包含真实细节，但仅凭它无法估计失败率、执行风险和市场采用速度。                              | 当前计划中哪些是个案细节，哪些应当与同类案例比较？外部视角会怎样评价这种案例？                      | 本身不是偏误。但如果排斥基础率、外部视角和参考类别预测，就会变得危险。                |
| A018 体验自我 / Experiencing Self        | 活在逐时经验中的自我，记录快乐、痛苦、注意、无聊、疲惫和情绪在经历发生时的实际流动。                                                | **案例：** 一个人在博物馆待了 3 小时，大部分时间疲惫、饥饿、轻微无聊，但其中有 10 分钟看到一幅画时极为震撼。体验自我经历的是许多普通甚至不舒服的时刻，不只是那个高光瞬间。                                                    | 这个经历在每个时刻分别是什么样？整个持续时间都愉快，还是只有某些片段愉快？                        | 不是偏误。它与记忆自我成对出现，是理解峰终定律、持续时间忽视和幸福评价的关键。            |
| A019 记忆自我 / Remembering Self         | 储存、叙述并在事后评价经验的自我。它常用峰值、结尾和叙事意义，而不是完整时间平均值。错误出现在被记住的故事被误当成完整经验。                            | **案例：** 博物馆参观结束后，那个人说：“这个下午太棒了。”因为最后那幅画确实极其震撼。这段记忆不是虚构的，但它是选择性的。它把整段经历组织成围绕最佳瞬间和结尾的故事，同时忽略了长时间疲惫。                                              | 我后来的故事是否忠实于完整经历？整个持续过程中，真实体验是什么样？                            | 与峰终定律、持续时间忽视、事后偏差、自传记忆和生活满意度判断相邻。                  |
| A020 `决策日志 / Decision Log`           | 程序性纠偏工具：在结果未知前记录证据、预测、概率、理由、疑虑和替代方案。它防止后来的记忆悄悄改写当时“显而易见”的东西。                              | **案例：** 投资者买入一只股票，并写下：“我认为中等增长概率 60%，停滞概率 25%，严重下跌概率 15%。主要风险：监管和需求不足。”一年后股票下跌，这份日志会阻止两种扭曲：“我早就知道它有风险”和“这明显是无法预见的”。                            | 决策前写下：我相信什么，为什么相信，概率是多少，什么证据会改变我的想法？结果出来后复盘。                 | 不是偏误。用于减少事后偏差、结果偏差、过度自信、自我服务偏差和记忆重构。               |
| A021 `预先验尸法 / Pre-mortem`            | 一种决策程序：假设项目已经失败，再在承诺硬化之前列出可能失败原因。它让怀疑在群体忠诚和乐观压制它之前变得合法。                                   | **案例：** 一个 App 发布前，产品负责人说：“想象 6 个月后，这次发布失败得很严重。发生了什么？”设计师说新手引导太复杂；工程师说服务器成本失控；市场同事说用户根本没理解价值。这些风险本来已经存在，但预先验尸法让它们可以被说出来。                       | 如果这件事失败，最可能的原因是什么？早期会出现哪些预警信号？                               | 用于降低乐观偏差、规划谬误、群体迷思、承诺升级和产品风险。                      |
| A022 红队审查 / Red Teaming              | 结构化对抗性审查，用来暴露薄弱假设、缺失证据和替代解释。它要求一个判断在现实检验之前，先经受有能力反对者的检验。                                  | **案例：** 一个法律团队认为自己的论证很强。红队必须替对方说话：“你们最强的判例可以被区分，你们的事实记录有缺口，你们请求的救济过宽。”这不是拆台，而是在法院提供不可控反对之前，先提供可控反对。                                            | 一个有能力的反对者会攻击哪里？哪一个假设、来源、推断或缺失事实最脆弱？                          | 纠偏程序。与考虑相反面、对抗性合作、魔鬼代言人角色和结构化分析技术相邻。               |
| A023 盲审 / Blind Review               | 在评价前移除无关身份线索的程序。它减少地位、声望、光环效应、刻板印象、性别、种族、学校背景、口音或个人熟悉度带来的扭曲。                              | **案例：** 招聘委员会先看到候选人的姓名、学校和照片，很快形成印象。盲审版本中，委员会先评价匿名作品样本和结构化回答。有些看起来没那么“名校光环”的候选人，在真实任务质量上反而更好。盲审阻止地位信号伪装成证据。                                    | 哪些身份线索与判断无关？能否在任务相关评价完成前隐藏它们？                                | 不是偏误。用于降低光环效应、亲和偏差、刻板印象、声望偏差、性别 / 种族偏差和权威偏差。       |

| A024 风险政策 / Risk Policy             | 一种处理重复风险的决策规则：把多次风险看成组合，而不是把每一次都当成孤立情绪选择。它纠正的是窄框架错误：逐个评价小风险，并因为每次都令人不适而拒绝有利的风险承担。 | **案例：** 公司可以资助 20 个小型实验功能。每个都有 40% 的失败概率，但成功项目收益很大。如果经理逐个审批，他们会因为每次失败都显得难堪而拒绝很多实验。采用风险政策时，他们预先决定：“我们资助一组实验，用总回报评价。”          | 这是一次性决策，还是一类重复决策的一部分？如果我要做 100 次同类选择，我希望采用什么规则？                 | 不完全是偏误，而是纠偏工具。与损失厌恶、窄框架、期望值、组合思维和前景理论相邻。            |

## B. 信息过滤与注意偏误

信息过滤偏误发生在明确推理开始之前。心智无法处理一切信息，所以它必须先选择什么变得显眼、可记、熟悉、带有情绪重量，或者容易被提取。这一类偏误的共同错误是：更容易被注意、记住、重复或感受到的信息，被误认为更真实、更常见、更重要或更有代表性。

### B1. 可得性、重复与熟悉感

| 编号 + 偏误名称（中文 / English / 别名 / 经典题型）         | 所属机制与认知功能 + 核心定义与错误分析                                                                     | 典型触发条件 + 典型案例                                                                                                 | 纠偏方法 / 检查问题                                          | 边界与相邻概念                                     |
| ------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------- |
| B001 `可得性启发式 / Availability Heuristic`      | 心智会根据例子进入脑海的容易程度，估计事件频率、概率或重要性。当记忆可得性真实反映频率时，这很有效；当鲜明性、近期性、媒体曝光或情绪强度让某些例子更容易被提取时，它就会造成偏差。 | **案例：** 一个人连续看了几条空难新闻后说：“我知道统计上飞机很安全，但最近飞机好像总是在出事。”空难未必真的更频繁，只是报道足够鲜明、情绪强烈、距离很近，所以在脑中更容易被提取。                  | 这些例子容易想起来，是因为它们真的常见，还是因为它们鲜明、近期、戏剧化、被反复报道？真实基础率是多少？  | 原型级偏误。与显著性偏差、频率错觉、可得性级联、负性偏差和概率忽视相邻。        |
| B002 注意偏差 / Attentional Bias                | 注意会被当前关切、威胁、欲望、目标或情绪状态相关的信息选择性吸引。错误在于把抓住注意的东西，当成客观上最重要的东西。                                | **案例：** 一个担心考试失败的学生读老师反馈。十句话是肯定的，只有一句说“论证还需要更精确”。学生整晚都在想这一句，并得出结论：“老师觉得我文章很差。”注意选择了和威胁有关的信息，并让它显得像完整评价。       | 我最先注意到了哪类信息？哪些因为情绪中性、积极、普通或不构成威胁而被忽略？                | 与焦虑、威胁监测、负性偏差、确认偏误和情绪一致性记忆偏差相邻。             |
| B003 `真相错觉效应 / Illusory Truth Effect`       | 被重复的陈述更容易加工，而加工流畅会被误读为真实。错误并不在于人有意识地相信“重复就是真理”，而是熟悉感会制造类似真实的感觉。                           | **案例：** 一条传言说：“这种补剂可以提高记忆力。”读者一开始没有在意。后来，他在广告、评论、短视频和推荐帖里多次看到这句话。再后来他说：“我不确定，但我好像经常听说它有效。”重复制造了熟悉感，熟悉感开始模仿证据。 | 我见过真实证据，还是只是多次遇到这个说法？我能指出原始来源及其质量吗？                  | 与有效性效应、认知轻松感、曝光效应、来源混淆和持续影响效应相邻。            |
| B004 有效性效应 / Validity Effect                | 重复会提高一个陈述被判断为有效、可信或真实的程度，尤其当重复来源被遗忘时。错误在于让重复曝光替代独立验证。                                     | **案例：** 选举期间，一个口号反复出现：“X 候选人在安全问题上软弱。”选民可能不记得最早在哪里听到、有没有证据、是否来自对手阵营。后来，这句话变得像常识。重复陈述通过熟悉感获得了有效性，而不是通过证据获得有效性。 | 这个主张是否有独立证据支持，还是因为反复流通而显得可信？我还记得来源吗？                 | 常与真相错觉效应重叠。“有效性效应”适合强调被判断为真实或可信，而不只是熟悉。     |
| B005 曝光效应 / Mere Exposure Effect            | 重复接触会提高喜欢或偏好，即使没有新的质量证据。错误在于把熟悉感误认为客观价值、安全、美感或可信度。                                        | **案例：** 一个人在背景音乐里多次听到一首歌，起初觉得普通。一周后，他开始喜欢这首歌，并说：“其实这歌挺好听。”歌曲本身没有变化；重复曝光让它变得更顺、更可亲。                            | 我偏好它，是因为它真的更好，还是因为它更熟悉？如果我第一次把它和其他选项放在一起评价，会一样吗？     | 不同于真相错觉效应：曝光效应关乎喜欢，真相错觉关乎真实感。与品牌熟悉度、现状偏差相邻。 |
| B006 语境效应 / Context Effect                  | 周围环境、比较集合或解释框架会改变对象被知觉和评价的方式。错误在于把判断当成对象自身属性，而忽略塑造判断的语境。                                  | **案例：** 在奢侈品店里，一个 300 美元的包放在 2000 美元的包旁边，会显得“还挺便宜”。在普通超市里，同一个 300 美元的包会显得奢侈。对象没有变，比较语境改变了价格的心理意义。             | 如果换一个语境、比较集合或标签，我还会这样判断吗？                            | 与对比效应、框架效应、锚定、诱饵效应和区分偏差相邻。                  |
| B007 线索依赖性遗忘 / Cue-dependent Forgetting     | 记忆提取依赖编码和提取时的线索。当关键线索缺席时，信息可能感觉像丢失了，尽管仍然储存在记忆中。错误在于把暂时提取失败当成知识不存在或不重要。                    | **案例：** 一个人走进厨房后突然忘了自己为什么来。回到书桌旁才想起来：原来是要拿剪刀。这个意图并没有被抹掉，而是原始情境提供了提取线索。                                        | 当我形成这个意图或学习这条信息时，现场有哪些线索？能否重建当时的语境、路线、笔记或物理环境？       | 更像记忆机制，而不是典型偏误。与心不在焉、语境依赖记忆和提取失败相邻。         |
| B008 情绪一致性记忆偏差 / Mood-congruent Memory Bias | 当前情绪会让与该情绪一致的记忆更容易被提取。错误在于把当前情绪状态下的记忆样本，当作对过去的平衡记录。                                       | **案例：** 一次面试失败后，求职者想：“我总是把事情搞砸。”他的心智开始提取过去失败、尴尬对话和被拒绝经历，而成功面试和他人赞扬变得难以想起。当前失落感筛选了记忆档案。                        | 在这种情绪下，哪些记忆更容易被想起？如果我更平静、更安全、心情更好，我会记起哪些证据？          | 与注意偏差、负性偏差、抑郁相关认知、可得性启发式和情感预测相邻。            |
| B009 频率错觉 / Frequency Illusion              | 当人第一次注意到某物后，会开始到处注意到它，并推断它变得更频繁。错误在于把注意力变化误认为世界变化。                                        | **案例：** 一个人刚学会 “gaslighting” 这个词，突然在文章、评论、视频和谈话中到处看到它。他说：“为什么大家突然都在说这个？”这个词未必突然变多；变化的可能是他的检测过滤器。              | 这个现象是真的变常见了，还是我刚刚开始对它敏感？历史数据或搜索趋势能否检验？               | 常与 Baader–Meinhof 现象重叠。与可得性、近期错觉和注意定势相邻。    |
| B010 巴德尔—迈因霍夫现象 / Baader–Meinhof Phenomenon | 对“刚注意到某物，随后反复遇见它”这种体验的通俗命名。机制上，它结合了选择性注意和由重复检测带来的确认感。                                     | **案例：** 一个人决定买一辆蓝色 Mazda。第二天，他觉得街上到处都是蓝色 Mazda。那些车很可能原本就在那儿，只是购车目标把注意调到了这个车型上。                                | 发生变化的是世界频率，还是我的注意过滤器？在此之前我是否统计过这些案例？                 | 最好作为频率错觉的子类型或通俗标签处理，而不是独立核心偏误。              |
| B011 近期错觉 / Recency Illusion                | 人会把自己最近才注意到的现象，误认为该现象本身最近才出现。错误在于把“对我来说是新的”转换成“在世界中是新的”。                                  | **案例：** 一个读者看到年轻人在网上使用某个俚语，就说：“这一代人刚刚发明了这个词。”简单查一下历史会发现，这个词几十年前就存在。新的不是词本身，而是读者的意识。                           | 这个现象真的新，还是只是最近才对我可见？有没有历史证据？                         | 与频率错觉、可得性启发式、当下主义和文化评论错误相邻。                 |
| B012 共情鸿沟 / Empathy Gap                     | 处于某种情绪、身体或动机状态中的人，会低估自己或他人在另一种状态下会怎样思考。错误在于把当前状态当作默认人类状态。                                 | **案例：** 一个人晚饭后很饱，说：“明天我只吃一份轻食沙拉。我真不懂自己为什么总在晚上吃零食。”到了半夜，他又饿又累，把冰箱里的东西吃了一遍。早先那个自我没能模拟后来的身体状态。                   | 我现在所处的状态，是否不同于真正做决定时的状态？饥饿、愤怒、疼痛、恐惧、欲望、疲惫或孤独会怎样改变判断？ | 与投射偏差、克制偏差、当下偏差、情感预测和冷热共情鸿沟相邻。              |

### B2. 显著性、奇异性与负性
